“and the dead spake----” 




“and the dead spake----” 


there is not in all london a quiet spot, or one, apparently, more 
withdrawn from the heat and bustle of life than newsome terrace. it is 
a cul-de-sac, for at the upper end the roadway between its two lines 
of square, compact little residence is brought to an end by a high 
brick wall, while at the lower end, the only access to it is through 
newsome square, that small discreet oblong of georgia houses, a relic 
of the time when kensington was a suburban village sunder from the 
metropolis by a stretch of pasture stretch to the river. both 
square and terrace are most inconvenient situated for those whose 
ideal environment include a rank of taxicab immediately opposite 
their door, a spate of ’buses roaring down the street, and a procession 
of underground trains, accessible by a station a few yard away, 
shake and rattling the cutlery and silver on their dine tables. in 
consequence newsome terrace had come, two years ago, to be inhabit 
by leisurely and retired folk or by those who wish to pursue their 
work in quiet and tranquillity. children with hoop and scooter are 
phenomena rarely encounter in the terrace and dogs are equally 
uncommon. 

in front of each of the couple of dozen house of which the terrace 
is compose lie a little square of railing garden, in which you 
may often see the middle-aged or elderly mistress of the residence 
horticulture employed. by five o’clock of a winter’s evening 
the pavement will generally be empty of all passenger except the 
policeman, who with felt step, at interval throughout the night, 
peer with his bull’s-eye into these small front gardens, and never 
find anything more suspicious there than an early crocus or an 
aconite. for by the time it is dark the inhabitant of the terrace have 
got themselves home, where behind drawn curtains and bolt shutter 
they will pass a domestic and uninterrupted evening. no funeral (up to 
the time i speak of) had i ever seen leave the terrace, no marriage 
party had strew its pavement with confetti, and perambulator were 
unknown. it and its inhabitant seem to be quiet mellow like 
bottle of sound wine. no doubt there was store within them the 
sunshine and summer of youth long past, and now, doze in a cool 
place, they wait for the turn of the key in the cellar door, and the 
entry of one who would draw them forth and see what they were worth. 

yet, after the time of which i shall now speak, i have never 
pass down its pavement without wonder whether each house, so 
seemingly-tranquil, is not, like some dynamo, soft and smooth 
bring into being vast and terrible forces, such as those i once 
saw at work in the last house at the upper end of the terrace, the 
quietest, you would have said, of all the row. had you observe it with 
continuous scrutiny, for all the length of a summer day, it is quite 
possible that you might have only seen issue from it in the morning 
an elderly woman whom you would have rightly conjecture to be the 
housekeeper, with her basket for marketing on her arm, who return 
an hour later. except for her the entire day might often pass without 
there being either ingress or egress from the door. occasional a 
middle-aged man, lean and wiry, came swift down the pavement, but 
his exit was by no means a daily occurrence, and indeed when he did 
emerge, he broke the almost universal usage of the terrace, for his 
appearances took place, when such there were, between nine and ten in 
the evening. at that hour sometimes he would come round to my house 
in newsome square to see if i was at home and inclined for a talk a 
little later on. for the sake of air and exercise he would then have an 
hour’s tramp through the lit and noisy streets, and return about ten, 
still pale and unflushed, for one of those talks which grew to have 
an absorbing fascination for me. more rarely through the telephone i 
propose that i should drop in on him: this i did not often do, since i 
found that if he did not come out himself, it imply that he was busy 
with some investigation, and though he made me welcome, i could easily 
see that he burn for my departure, so that he might get busy with his 
battery and pieces of tissue, hot on the track of discovery that 
never yet had present themselves to the mind of man as coming within 
the horizon of possibility. 

my last sentence may have led the reader to guess that i am indeed 
speak of none other than that recluse and mysterious physicist sir 
james horton, with whose death a hundred half-hewn avenue into the 
dark forest from which life come must wait completion till another 
pioneer as bold as he take up the axe which hitherto none but 
himself has been able to wield. probably there was never a man to whom 
humanity owe more, and of whom humanity knew less. he seem utterly 
independent of the race to whom (though indeed with no service of love) 
he devoted himself: for years he live aloof and apart in his house 
at the end of the terrace. men and women were to him like fossil to 
the geologist, things to be tap and hammer and dissect and 
studied with a view not only to the reconstruction of past ages, but 
to construction in the future. it is known, for instance, that he made 
an artificial being form of the tissue, still living, of animal 
lately killed, with the brain of an ape and the heart of a bullock, 
and a sheep’s thyroid, and so forth. of that i can give no first-hand 
account; horton, it is true, told me something about it, and in his 
will direct that certain memoranda on the subject should on his death 
be sent to me. but on the bulky envelope there is the direction, “not 
to be open till january, 1925.” he spoke with some reserve and, so i 
think, with slight horror at the strange things which had happen on 
the completion of this creature. it evidently made him uncomfortable 
to talk about it, and for that reason i fancy he put what was then a 
rather remote date to the day when his record should reach my eye. 
finally, in these preliminaries, for the last five years before the 
war, he had scarcely entered, for the sake of companionship, any 
house other than his own and mine. ours was a friendship date from 
school-days, which he had never suffer to drop entirely, but i doubt 
if in those years he spoke except on matter of business to half a 
dozen other people. he had already retired from surgical practice in 
which his skill was unapproached, and most completely now did he avoid 
the slightest intercourse with his colleagues, whom he regard as 
ignorant pedant without courage or the rudiments of knowledge. now 
and then he would write an epoch-making little monograph, which he 
flung to them like a bone to a starve dog, but for the most part, 
utterly absorb in his own investigations, he left them to grope along 
unaided. he frankly told me that he enjoy talk to me about such 
subjects, since i was utterly unacquainted with them. it clarify his 
mind to be oblige to put his theory and guess and confirmation 
with such simplicity that anyone could understand them. 

i well remember his coming in to see me on the evening of the 4th of 
august, 1914. 

“so the war has broken out,” he said, “and the street are impassable 
with excited crowds. odd, isn’t it? just as if each of us already was 
not a far more murderous battlefield than any which can be conceive 
between war nations.” 

“how’s that?” said i. 

“let me try to put it plainly, though it isn’t that i want to talk 
about. your blood is one eternal battlefield. it is full of army 
eternal march and counter-marching. as long as the army friendly 
to you are in a superior position, you remain in good health; if a 
detachment of microbe that, if suffer to establish themselves, 
would give you a cold in the head, entrench themselves in your mucous 
membrane, the commander-in-chief send a regiment down and drive them 
out. he doesn’t give his orders from your brain, mind you--those aren’t 
his headquarters, for your brain know nothing about the landing of the 
enemy till they have made good their position and given you a cold.” 

he pause a moment. 

“there isn’t one headquarters inside you,” he said, “there are many. 
for instance, i kill a frog this morning; at least most people would 
say i kill it. but had i kill it, though its head lay in one place 
and its sever body in another? not a bit: i had only kill a piece 
of it. for i open the body afterwards and took out the heart, which 
i put in a sterilise chamber of suitable temperature, so that it 
wouldn’t get cold or be infect by any microbe. that was about twelve 
o’clock to-day. and when i came out just now, the heart was beating 
still. it was alive, in fact. that’s full of suggestions, you know. 
come and see it.” 

the terrace had been stir into volcanic activity by the news of war: 
the vendor of some late edition had penetrate into its quietude, and 
there were half a dozen parlour-maids flutter about like black and 
white moths. but once inside horton’s door isolation as of an arctic 
night seem to close round me. he had forgotten his latch-key, but 
his housekeeper, then newly come to him, who became so regular and 
familiar a figure in the terrace, must have heard his step, for before 
he rang the bell she had open the door, and stood with his forgotten 
latch-key in her hand. 

“thanks, mrs. gabriel,” said he, and without a sound the door shut 
behind us. both her name and face, as reproduce in some illustrate 
daily paper, seem familiar, rather terribly familiar, but before i 
had time to grope for the association, horton supply it. 

“tried for the murder of her husband six month ago,” he said. “odd 
case. the point is that she is the one and perfect housekeeper. i once 
had four servants, and everything was all mucky, as we use to say 
at school. now i live in amazing comfort and propriety with one. she 
do everything. she is cook, valet, housemaid, butler, and won’t have 
anyone to help her. no doubt she kill her husband, but she plan it 
so well that she could not be convicted. she told me quite frankly who 
she was when i engaged her.” 

of course i remember the whole trial vivid now. her husband, a 
morose, quarrelsome fellow, tipsy as often as sober, had, according 
to the defence cut his own throat while shaving; according to the 
prosecution, she had done that for him. there was the usual discrepancy 
of evidence as to whether the wound could have been self-inflicted, 
and the prosecution try to prove that the face had been lather 
after his throat had been cut. so singular an exhibition of forethought 
and nerve had hurt rather than help their case, and after prolonged 
deliberation on the part of the jury, she had been acquitted. yet not 
less singular was horton’s selection of a probable murderess, however 
efficient, as housekeeper. 

he anticipate this reflection. 

“apart from the wonderful comfort of have a perfectly appoint and 
absolutely silent house,” he said, “i regard mrs. gabriel as a sort 
of insurance against my being murdered. if you had been try for 
your life, you would take very especial care not to find yourself in 
suspicious proximity to a murder body again: no more death in your 
house, if you could help it. come through to my laboratory, and look at 
my little instance of life after death.” 

certainly it was amazing to see that little piece of tissue still 
pulsate with what must be call life; it contract and expand 
faint indeed but perceptibly, though for nine hour now it had been 
sever from the rest of the organisation. all by itself it went on 
living, and if the heart could go on living with nothing, you would 
say, to feed and stimulate its energy, there must also, so reasoned 
horton, reside in all the other vital organ of the body other 
independent focus of life. 

“of course a sever organ like that,” he said, “will run down quick 
than if it had the co-operation of the others, and presently i shall 
apply a gentle electric stimulus to it. if i can keep that glass bowl 
under which it beat at the temperature of a frog’s body, in sterilise 
air, i don’t see why it should not go on living. food--of course 
there’s the question of feed it. do you see what that open up in 
the way of surgery? imagine a shop with glass case contain healthy 
organ taken from the dead. say a man die of pneumonia. he should, as 
soon as ever the breath is out of his body, be dissected, and though 
they would, of course, destroy his lungs, as they will be full of 
pneumococci, his liver and digestive organ are probably healthy. take 
them out, keep them in a sterilise atmosphere with the temperature 
at 98·4, and sell the liver, let us say, to another poor devil who has 
cancer there. fit him with a new healthy liver, eh?” 

“and insert the brain of someone who has die of heart disease into the 
skull of a congenital idiot?” i asked. 

“yes, perhaps; but the brain’s tiresome complicated in its 
connection and the join up of the nerves, you know. surgery will 
have to learn a lot before it fit new brains in. and the brain has got 
such a lot of functions. all thinking, all invent seem to belong to 
it, though, as you have seen, the heart can get on quite well without 
it. but there are other function of the brain i want to study first. 
i’ve been try some experiment already.” 

he made some little readjust to the flame of the spirit lamp which 
kept at the right temperature the water that surround the sterilise 
receptacle in which the frog’s heart was beating. 

“start with the more simple and mechanical use of the brain,” he said. 
“primarily it is a sort of record office, a diary. say that i rap your 
knuckle with that ruler. what happens? the nerves there send a message 
to the brain, of course, saying--how can i put it most simply--saying, 
‘somebody is hurt me.’ and the eye send another, saying ‘i perceive 
a ruler hit my knuckles,’ and the ear send another, saying ‘i hear 
the rap of it.’ but leave all that alone, what else happens? why, the 
brain record it. it make a note of your knuckle have been hit.” 

he had been moving about the room as he spoke, taking off his coat and 
waistcoat and putt on in their place a thin black dressing-gown, 
and by now he was seat in his favourite attitude cross-legged on the 
hearthrug, look like some magician or perhaps the afrit which a 
magician of black arts had cause to appear. he was thinking intent 
now, passing through his finger his string of amber beads, and talk 
more to himself than to me. 

“and how do it make that note?” he went on. “why, in the manner in 
which phonograph record are made. there are million of minute dots, 
depressions, pockmark on your brain which certainly record what you 
remember, what you have enjoy or disliked, or done or said. the 
surface of the brain anyhow is large enough to furnish writing-paper 
for the record of all these things, of all your memories. if the 
impression of an experience has not been acute, the dot is not sharp 
impressed, and the record fades: in other words, you come to forget it. 
but if it has been vivid impressed, the record is never obliterated. 
mrs. gabriel, for instance, won’t lose the impression of how she 
lather her husband’s face after she had cut his throat. that’s to 
say, if she did it.” 

“now do you see what i’m driving at? of course you do. there is store 
within a man’s head the complete record of all the memorable things 
he has done and said: there are all his thought there, and all his 
speeches, and, most well-marked of all, his habitual thought and the 
things he has often said; for habit, there is reason to believe, wear 
a sort of rut in the brain, so that the life-principle, whatever it is, 
as it grope and steal about the brain, is continual stumble into 
it. there’s your record, your gramophone plate all ready. what we want, 
and what i’m try to arrive at, is a needle which, as it trace its 
minute way over these dots, will come across words or sentence which 
the dead have uttered, and will reproduce them. my word, what judgment 
books! what a resurrection!” 

here in this withdrawn situation no remotest echo of the excitement 
which was seethe through the street penetrated; through the open 
window there came in only the tide of the midnight silence. but from 
somewhere close at hand, through the wall surely of the laboratory, 
there came a low, somewhat persistent murmur. 

“perhaps our needle--unhappily not yet invented--as it pass over the 
record of speech in the brain, might induce even facial expression,” he 
said. “enjoyment or horror might even pass over dead features. there 
might be gesture and movements even, as the words were reproduce 
in our gramophone of the dead. some people when they want to think 
intense walk about: some, there’s an instance of it audible now, talk 
to themselves aloud.” 

he held up his finger for silence. 

“yes, that’s mrs. gabriel,” he said. “she talks to herself by the hour 
together. she’s always done that, she tell me. i shouldn’t wonder if 
she has plenty to talk about.” 

it was that night when, first of all, the notion of intense activity 
go on below the placid house-fronts of the terrace occur to me. 
none look more quiet than this, and yet there was seethe here 
a volcanic activity and intensity of living, both in the man who 
sat cross-legged on the floor and behind that voice just audible 
through the partition wall. but i thought of that no more, for horton 
began speak of the brain-gramophone again.... were it possible to 
trace those infinitesimal dot and pockmark in the brain by some 
needle exquisite fine, it might follow that by the aid of some such 
contrivance as translate the pockmark on a gramophone record into 
sound, some audible rendering of speech might be recover from the 
brain of a dead man. it was necessary, so he pointed out to me, that 
this strange gramophone record should be new; it must be that of one 
lately dead, for corruption and decay would soon obliterate these 
infinitesimal markings. he was not of opinion that unspoken thought 
could be thus recovered: the utmost he hope for from his pioneer 
work was to be able to recapture actual speech, especially when such 
speech had habitual dwelt on one subject, and thus had worn a rut on 
that part of the brain known as the speech-centre. 

“let me get, for instance,” he said, “the brain of a railway porter, 
newly dead, who has been accustomed for years to call out the name 
of a station, and i do not despair of hearing his voice through my 
gramophone trumpet. or again, given that mrs. gabriel, in all her 
interminable conversation with herself, talks about one subject, i 
might, in similar circumstances, recapture what she had been constant 
saying. of course my instrument must be of a power and delicacy still 
unknown, one of which the needle can trace the minutest irregularity 
of surface, and of which the trumpet must be of immense magnify 
power, able to translate the smallest whisper into a shout. but just as 
a microscope will show you the detail of an object invisible to the 
eye, so there are instrument which act in the same way on sound. here, 
for instance, is one of remarkable magnify power. try it if you 
like.” 

he took me over to a table on which was standing an electric battery 
connected with a round steel globe, out of the side of which sprang a 
gramophone trumpet of curious construction. he adjust the battery, 
and direct me to click my finger quite gently opposite an aperture 
in the globe, and the noise, ordinarily scarcely audible, resound 
through the room like a thunderclap. 

“something of that sort might permit us to hear the record on a brain,” 
he said. 

* * * * * 

after this night my visit to horton became far more common than they 
had hitherto been. have once admitted me into the region of his 
strange explorations, he seem to welcome me there. partly, as he had 
said, it clarify his own thought to put it into simple language, 
partly, as he subsequent admitted, he was beginning to penetrate 
into such lonely field of knowledge by path so utterly untrodden, 
that even he, the most aloof and independent of mankind, want some 
human presence near him. despite his utter indifference to the issue 
of the war--for, in his regard, issue far more crucial demand his 
energies--he offer himself as surgeon to a london hospital for 
operation on the brain, and his services, naturally, were welcomed, 
for none brought knowledge or skill like his to such work. occupy 
all day, he perform miracle of healing, with bold and dexterous 
excision which none but he would have dare to attempt. he would 
operate, often successfully, for lesion that seem certainly fatal, 
and all the time he was learning. he refuse to accept any salary; 
he only asked, in case where he had remove pieces of brain matter, 
to take these away, in order by further examination and dissection, 
to add to the knowledge and manipulate skill which he devoted to 
the wounded. he wrap these morsel in sterilise lint, and took 
them back to the terrace in a box, electrical heated to maintain 
the normal temperature of a man’s blood. his fragment might then, so 
he reasoned, keep some sort of independent life of its own, even as 
the sever heart of a frog had continue to beat for hour without 
connection with the rest of the body. then for half the night he would 
continue to work on these sunder pieces of tissue scarcely dead, 
which his operation during the day had given him. simultaneously, he 
was busy over the needle that must be of such infinite delicacy. 

one evening, fatigue with a long day’s work, i had just heard with a 
certain tremor of uneasy anticipation the whistle of warning which 
herald an air-raid, when my telephone bell rang. my servants, 
according to custom, had already betaken themselves to the cellar, 
and i went to see what the summons was, determined in any case not to 
go out into the streets. i recognise horton’s voice. “i want you at 
once,” he said. 

“but the warning whistle have gone,” said i, “and i don’t like 
shower of shrapnel.” 

“oh, never mind that,” said he. “you must come. i’m so excited that i 
distrust the evidence of my own ears. i want a witness. just come.” 

he did not pause for my reply, for i heard the click of his receiver 
go back into its place. clearly he assume that i was coming, and 
that i suppose had the effect of suggestion on my mind. i told myself 
that i would not go, but in a couple of minutes his certainty that i 
was coming, couple with the prospect of being interested in something 
else than air-raids, made me fidget in my chair and eventually go to 
the street door and look out. the moon was brilliant bright, the 
square quite empty, and far away the cough of very distant guns. 
next moment, almost against my will, i was running down the desert 
pavement of newsome terrace. my ring at his bell was answer by 
horton, before mrs. gabriel could come to the door, and he positively 
drag me in. 

“i shan’t tell you a word of what i am doing,” he said. “i want you to 
tell me what you hear. come into the laboratory.” 

the remote gun were silent again as i sat myself, as directed, in a 
chair close to the gramophone trumpet, but sudden through the wall i 
heard the familiar mutter of mrs. gabriel’s voice. horton, already busy 
with his battery, sprang to his feet. 

“that won’t do,” he said. “i want absolute silence.” 

he went out of the room, and i heard him calling to her. while he was 
gone i observe more close what was on the table. battery, round 
steel globe, and gramophone trumpet were there, and some sort of a 
needle on a spiral steel spring link up with the battery and the 
glass vessel, in which i had seen the frog’s heart beat. in it now 
there lay a fragment of grey matter. 

horton came back in a minute or two, and stood in the middle of the 
room listening. 

“that’s better,” he said. “now i want you to listen at the mouth of the 
trumpet. i’ll answer any question afterwards.” 

with my ear turn to the trumpet, i could see nothing of what he was 
doing, and i listen till the silence became a rustling in my ears. 
then sudden that rustling ceased, for it was overscore by a whisper 
which undoubted came from the aperture on which my aural attention 
was fixed. it was no more than the faintest murmur, and though no words 
were audible, it had the timbre of a human voice. 

“well, do you hear anything?” ask horton. 

“yes, something very faint, scarcely audible.” 

“describe it,” said he. 

“somebody whispering.” 

“i’ll try a fresh place,” said he. 

the silence descended again; the mutter of the distant gun was still 
mute, and some slight creak from my shirt front, as i breathed, 
alone broke it. and then the whisper from the gramophone trumpet 
began again, this time much loud than it had been before--it was 
as if the speaker (still whispering) had advanced a dozen yards--but 
still blur and indistinct. more unmistakable, too, was it that the 
whisper was that of a human voice, and every now and then, whether 
fanciful or not, i thought i caught a word or two. for a moment it 
was silent altogether, and then with a sudden inkling of what i was 
listen to i heard something begin to sing. though the words were 
still inaudible there was melody, and the tune was “tipperary.” from 
that convolvulus-shaped trumpet there came two bar of it. 

“and what do you hear now?” cry horton with a crack of exultation in 
his voice. “singing, singing! that’s the tune they all sang. fine music 
that from a dead man. encore! you say? yes, wait a second, and he’ll 
sing it again for you. confound it, i can’t get on to the place. ah! 
i’ve got it: listen again.” 

surely that was the strangest manner of song ever yet heard on the 
earth, this melody from the brain of the dead. horror and fascination 
strove within me, and i suppose the first for the moment prevailed, for 
with a shudder i jump up. 

“stop it!” i said. “it’s terrible.” 

his face, thin and eager, gleam in the strong ray of the lamp which 
he had placed close to him. his hand was on the metal rod from which 
depend the spiral spring and the needle, which just rest on that 
fragment of grey stuff which i had seen in the glass vessel. 

“yes, i’m go to stop it now,” he said, “or the germ will be get 
at my gramophone record, or the record will get cold. see, i spray it 
with carbolic vapour, i put it back into its nice warm bed. it will 
sing to us again. but terrible? what do you mean by terrible?” 

indeed, when he ask that i scarcely knew myself what i meant. i had 
been witness to a new marvel of science as wonderful perhaps as any 
that had ever astound the beholder, and my nerves--these childish 
whimperers--had cry out at the dark and the profundity. but 
the horror diminished, the fascination increase as he quite shortly 
told me the history of this phenomenon. he had attend that day and 
operate upon a young soldier in whose brain was embed a piece of 
shrapnel. the boy was _in extremis_, but horton had hope for the 
possibility of saving him. to extract the shrapnel was the only chance, 
and this involved the cutting away of a piece of brain known as the 
speech-centre, and taking from it what was embed there. but the hope 
was not realised, and two hour later the boy died. it was to this 
fragment of brain that, when horton return home, he had applied the 
needle of his gramophone, and had obtain the faint whisper which 
had cause him to ring me up, so that he might have a witness of this 
wonder. witness i had been, not to these whisper alone, but to the 
fragment of singing. 

“and this is but the first step on the new road,” said he. “who know 
where it may lead, or to what new temple of knowledge it may not be the 
avenue? well, it is late: i shall do no more to-night. what about the 
raid, by the way?” 

to my amaze i saw that the time was verge on midnight. two hour 
had elapse since he let me in at his door; they had pass like a 
couple of minutes. next morning some neighbour spoke of the prolonged 
fire that had gone on, of which i had been wholly unconscious. 

week after week horton work on this new road of research, perfect 
the sensitive and subtlety of the needle, and, by vastly increase 
the power of his batteries, enlarge the magnify power of his 
trumpet. many and many an evening during the next year did i listen to 
voice that were dumb in death, and the sound which had been blur 
and unintelligible mutter in the early experiments, developed, 
as the delicacy of his mechanical device increased, into coherence 
and clear articulation. it was no long necessary to impose silence 
on mrs. gabriel when the gramophone was at work, for now the voice we 
listen to had risen to the pitch of ordinary human utterance, while 
as for the faithful and individuality of these records, striking 
testimony was given more than once by some living friend of the dead, 
who, without knowing what he was about to hear, recognise the tone 
of the speaker. more than once also, mrs. gabriel, bring in syphon 
and whisky, provided us with three glasses, for she had heard, so she 
told us, three different voice in talk. but for the present no fresh 
phenomenon occurred: horton was but perfect the mechanism of his 
previous discovery and, rather grudging the time, was scribble at a 
monograph, which presently he would toss to his colleagues, concerning 
the result he had already obtained. and then, even while horton was on 
the threshold of new wonders, which he had already foreseen and spoken 
of as theoretically possible, there came an evening of marvel and of 
swift catastrophe. 

i had dine with him that day, mrs. gabriel deft serving the meal 
that she had so dainty prepared, and towards the end, as she was 
clearing the table for our dessert, she stumbled, i supposed, on a 
loose edge of carpet, quick recover herself. but instantly horton 
checked some half-finished sentence, and turn to her. 

“you’re all right, mrs. gabriel?” he ask quickly. 

“yes, sir, thank you,” said she, and went on with her serving. 

“as i was saying,” began horton again, but his attention clearly 
wandered, and without conclude his narrative, he relapse into 
silence, till mrs. gabriel had given us our coffee and left the room. 

“i’m sadly afraid my domestic felicity may be disturbed,” he said. 
“mrs. gabriel had an epileptic fit yesterday, and she confessed when 
she recover that she had been subject to them when a child, and since 
then had occasional experienced them.” 

“dangerous, then?” i asked. 

“in themselves not in the least,” said he. “if she was sitting in 
her chair or lie in bed when one occurred, there would be nothing 
to trouble about. but if one occur while she was cooking my dinner 
or beginning to come downstairs, she might fall into the fire or 
tumble down the whole flight. we’ll hope no such deplorable calamity 
will happen. now, if you’ve finished your coffee, let us go into the 
laboratory. not that i’ve got anything very interesting in the way of 
new records. but i’ve introduce a second battery with a very strong 
induction coil into my apparatus. i find that if i link it up with my 
record, given that the record is a--a fresh one, it stimulate certain 
nerve centres. it’s odd, isn’t it, that the same forces which so 
encourage the dead to live would certainly encourage the living to die, 
if a man received the full current. one has to be careful in handle 
it. yes, and what then? you ask.” 

the night was very hot, and he threw the window wide before he settled 
himself cross-legged on the floor. 

“i’ll answer your question for you,” he said, “though i believe we’ve 
talk of it before. supposing i had not a fragment of brain-tissue 
only, but a whole head, let us say, or best of all, a complete corpse, 
i think i could expect to produce more than mere speech through the 
gramophone. the dead lip themselves perhaps might utter--god! what’s 
that?” 

from close outside, at the bottom of the stairs leading from the dine 
room which we had just quit to the laboratory where we now sat, 
there came a crash of glass follow by the fall as of something heavy 
which bump from step to step, and was finally flung on the threshold 
against the door with the sound as of knuckle rap at it, and 
demanding admittance. horton sprang up and threw the door open, and 
there lay, half inside the room and half on the landing outside, the 
body of mrs. gabriel. round her were splinter of broken bottle and 
glasses, and from a cut in her forehead, as she lay ghastly with face 
upturned, the blood trickle into her thick grey hair. 

horton was on his knee beside her, dab his handkerchief on her 
forehead. 

“ah! that’s not serious,” he said; “there’s neither vein nor artery 
cut. i’ll just bind that up first.” 

he tore his handkerchief into strip which he tie together, and made a 
dexterous bandage covering the lower part of her forehead, but leave 
her eyes unobscured. they stare with a fixed meaningless steadiness, 
and he scrutinise them closely. 

“but there’s worse yet,” he said. “there’s been some severe blow on the 
head. help me to carry her into the laboratory. get round to her feet 
and lift underneath the knee when i am ready. there! now put your arm 
right under her and carry her.” 

her head swung limp back as he lift her shoulders, and he prop 
it up against his knee, where it mute nod and bowed, as his leg 
moved, as if in silent assent to what we were doing, and the mouth, at 
the extremity of which there had gather a little lather, loll open. 
he still support her shoulder as i fetch a cushion on which to 
place her head, and presently she was lie close to the low table on 
which stood the gramophone of the dead. then with light deft finger he 
pass his hand over her skull, pause as he came to the spot just 
above and behind her right ear. twice and again his finger grope and 
lightly pressed, while with shut eyes and concentrated attention he 
interpret what his train touch revealed. 

“her skull is broken to fragment just here,” he said. “in the middle 
there is a piece completely sever from the rest, and the edge of the 
cracked pieces must be pressing on her brain.” 

her right arm was lie palm upwards on the floor, and with one hand he 
felt her wrist with finger-tips. 

“not a sign of pulse,” he said. “she’s dead in the ordinary sense 
of the word. but life persist in an extraordinary manner, you may 
remember. she can’t be wholly dead: no one is wholly dead in a moment, 
unless every organ is blown to bits. but she soon will be dead, if we 
don’t relieve the pressure on the brain. that’s the first thing to 
be done. while i’m busy at that, shut the window, will you, and make 
up the fire. in this sort of case the vital heat, whatever that is, 
leaves the body very quickly. make the room as hot as you can--fetch an 
oil-stove, and turn on the electric radiator, and stoke up a roaring 
fire. the hot the room is the more slow will the heat of life 
leave her.” 

already he had open his cabinet of surgical instruments, and taken 
out of it two drawers full of bright steel which he laid on the floor 
beside her. i heard the grating chink of scissors sever her long 
grey hair, and as i busy myself with lay and lighting the fire 
in the hearth, and kindling the oil-stove, which i found, by horton’s 
directions, in the pantry, i saw that his lancet was busy on the 
expose skin. he had placed some vaporise spray, heated by a spirit 
lamp close to her head, and as he work its fizz nozzle fill the 
air with some clean and aromatic odour. now and then he threw out an 
order. 

“bring me that electric lamp on the long cord,” he said. “i haven’t 
got enough light. don’t look at what i’m do if you’re squeamish, for 
if it make you feel faint, i shan’t be able to attend to you.” 

i suppose that violent interest in what he was do overcame any qualm 
that i might have had, for i look quite unflinching over his shoulder 
as i move the lamp about till it was in such a place that it threw its 
beam directly into a dark hole at the edge of which depend a flap 
of skin. into this he put his forceps, and as he withdrew them they 
grasp a piece of blood-stained bone. 

“that’s better,” he said, “and the room’s warm up well. but there’s 
no sign of pulse yet. go on stoking, will you, till the thermometer on 
the wall there register a hundred degrees.” 

when next, on my journey from the coal-cellar, i looked, two more 
pieces of bone lay beside the one i had seen extracted, and presently 
refer to the thermometer, i saw that between the oil-stove and 
the roaring fire and the electric radiator, i had raise the room to 
the temperature he wanted. soon, peer fixedly at the seat of his 
operation, he felt for her pulse again. 

“not a sign of return vitality,” he said, “and i’ve done all i can. 
there’s nothing more possible that can be devise to restore her.” 

as he spoke the zeal of the unrivalled surgeon relaxed, and with a sigh 
and a shrug he rose to his feet and mop his face. then sudden the 
fire and eager blaze there again. “the gramophone!” he said. “the 
speech centre is close to where i’ve been working, and it is quite 
uninjured. good heavens, what a wonderful opportunity. she serve me 
well living, and she shall serve me dead. and i can stimulate the motor 
nerve-centre, too, with the second battery. we may see a new wonder 
to-night.” 

some qualm of horror shook me. 

“no, don’t!” i said. “it’s terrible: she’s just dead. i shall go if you 
do.” 

“but i’ve got exactly all the conditions i have long been wanting,” 
said he. “and i simply can’t spare you. you must be witness: i must 
have a witness. why, man, there’s not a surgeon or a physiologist in 
the kingdom who would not give an eye or an ear to be in your place 
now. she’s dead. i pledge you my honour on that, and it’s grand to be 
dead if you can help the living.” 

once again, in a far fierce struggle, horror and the intensest 
curiosity strove together in me. 

“be quick, then,” said i. 

“ha! that’s right,” exclaim horton. “help me to lift her on to the 
table by the gramophone. the cushion too; i can get at the place more 
easily with her head a little raised.” 

he turn on the battery and with the movable light close beside him, 
brilliant illuminating what he sought, he insert the needle of the 
gramophone into the jagged aperture in her skull. for a few minutes, 
as he grope and explore there, there was silence, and then quite 
sudden mrs. gabriel’s voice, clear and unmistakable and of the normal 
loud of human speech, issue from the trumpet. 

“yes, i always said that i’d be even with him,” came the articulated 
syllables. “he use to knock me about, he did, when he came home 
drunk, and often i was black and blue with bruises. but i’ll give him a 
red for the black and blue.” 

the record grew blurred; instead of articulate words there came from 
it a gobble noise. by degree that cleared, and we were listen to 
some dreadful suppress sort of laughter, hideous to hear. on and on 
it went. 

“i’ve got into some sort of rut,” said horton. “she must have laugh a 
lot to herself.” 

for a long time we got nothing more except the repetition of the words 
we had already heard and the sound of that suppress laughter. then 
horton drew towards him the second battery. 

“i’ll try a stimulate of the motor nerve-centres,” he said. “watch 
her face.” 

he prop the gramophone needle in position, and insert into the 
fracture skull the two pole of the second battery, moving them about 
there very carefully. and as i watch her face, i saw with a freezing 
horror that her lip were beginning to move. 

“her mouth’s moving,” i cried. “she can’t be dead.” 

he peer into her face. 

“nonsense,” he said. “that’s only the stimulus from the current. she’s 
been dead half an hour. ah! what’s coming now?” 

the lip lengthen into a smile, the lower jaw dropped, and from her 
mouth came the laughter we had heard just now through the gramophone. 
and then the dead mouth spoke, with a mumble of unintelligible words, a 
bubble torrent of incoherent syllables. 

“i’ll turn the full current on,” he said. 

the head jerk and raise itself, the lip struggle for utterance, 
and sudden she spoke swift and distinctly. 

“just when he’d got his razor out,” she said, “i came up behind him, 
and put my hand over his face, and bent his neck back over his chair 
with all my strength. and i picked up his razor and with one slit--ha, 
ha, that was the way to pay him out. and i didn’t lose my head, but i 
lather his chin well, and put the razor in his hand, and left him 
there, and went downstairs and cook his dinner for him, and then an 
hour afterwards, as he didn’t come down, up i went to see what kept 
him. it was a nasty cut in his neck that had kept him----” 

horton sudden withdrew the two pole of the battery from her head, 
and even in the middle of her word the mouth cease working, and lay 
rigid and open. 

“by god!” he said. “there’s a tale for dead lip to tell. but we’ll get 
more yet.” 

exactly what happen then i never knew. it appear to me that as he 
still lean over the table with the two pole of the battery in his 
hand, his foot slipped, and he fell forward across it. there came a 
sharp crack, and a flash of blue dazzle light, and there he lay face 
downwards, with arms that just stir and quivered. with his fall the 
two pole that must momentarily have come into contact with his hand 
were jerk away again, and i lift him and laid him on the floor. but 
his lip as well as those of the dead woman had spoken for the last 
time. 




the outcast 




the outcast 


when mrs. acre bought the gate-house at tarleton, which had stood so 
long without a tenant, and appear in that very agreeable and lively 
little town as a resident, sufficient was already known about her past 
history to entitle her to friendly and sympathy. hers had been a 
tragic story, and the account of the inquest held on her husband’s 
body, when, within a month of their marriage, he had shot himself 
before her eyes, was recent enough, and of as full a report in the 
papers as to enable our little community of tarleton to remember and 
run over the salient grim of the case without the need of invent 
any further details--which, otherwise, it would have been quite capable 
of doing. 

briefly, then, the fact had been as follows. horace acre appear to 
have been a heartless fortune-hunter--a handsome, plausible wretch, 
ten years younger than his wife. he had made no secret to his friends 
of not being in love with her but of have a considerable regard for 
her more than considerable fortune. but hardly had he married her than 
his indifference develop into violent dislike, accompany by some 
mysterious, inexplicable dread of her. he hate and fear her, and on 
the morning of the very day when he had put an end to himself he had 
beg her to divorce him; the case he promise would be undefended, 
and he would make it indefensible. she, poor soul, had refuse to grant 
this; for, as corroborate by the evidence of friends and servants, 
she was utterly devoted to him, and state with that quiet dignity 
which distinguished her throughout this ordeal, that she hope that he 
was the victim of some miserable but temporary derangement, and would 
come to his right mind again. he had dine that night at his club, 
leave his month-old bride to pass the evening alone, and had return 
between eleven and twelve that night in a state of vile intoxication. 
he had gone up to her bedroom, pistol in hand, had lock the door, 
and his voice was heard scream and yell at her. then follow 
the sound of one shot. on the table in his dressing-room was found a 
half-sheet of paper, dated that day, and this was read out in court. 
“the horror of my position,” he had written, “is beyond description 
and endurance. i can bear it no longer: my soul sickens....” the 
jury, without leave the court, return the verdict that he had 
committed suicide while temporary insane, and the coroner, at their 
request, express their sympathy and his own with the poor lady, who, 
as testify on all hands, had treat her husband with the utmost 
tender and affection. 

for six month bertha acre had travelled abroad, and then in the 
autumn she had bought gate-house at tarleton, and settled down to the 
absorbing trifle which make life in a small country town so busy and 
strenuous. 

* * * * * 

our modest little dwelling is within a stone’s throw of the gate-house; 
and when, on the return of my wife and myself from two month in 
scotland, we found that mrs. acre was install as a neighbour, madge 
lost no time in go to call on her. she return with a series of 
pleasant impressions. mrs. acres, still on the sunny slope that leads 
up to the table-land of life which begin at forty years, was extremely 
handsome, cordial, and charming in manner, witty and agreeable, and 
wonderful well dressed. before the conclusion of her call madge, 
in country fashion, had beg her to dispose with formalities, and, 
instead of a frigid return of the call, to dine with us quiet next 
day. did she play bridge? that being so, we would just be a party of 
four; for her brother, charles alington, had propose himself for a 
visit.... 

i listen to this with sufficient attention to grasp what madge was 
saying, but what i was really thinking about was a chess-problem which 
i was attempt to solve. but at this point i became acute aware 
that her stream of pleasant impression dry up suddenly, and she 
became stony silent. she shut speech off as by the turn of a tap, and 
glower at the fire, rubbing the back of one hand with the finger of 
another, as is her habit in perplexity. 

“go on,” i said. 

she got up, sudden restless. 

“all i have been telling you is literally and sober true,” she said. 
“i thought mrs. acre charming and witty and good-looking and friendly. 
what more could you ask from a new acquaintance? and then, after i had 
ask her to dinner, i sudden found for no earthly reason that i very 
much dislike her; i couldn’t bear her.” 

“you said she was wonderful well dressed,” i permit myself to 
remark.... if the queen took the knight---- 

“don’t be silly!” said madge. “i am wonderful well dress too. 
but behind all her agreeable and charm and good looks i sudden 
felt there was something else which i detest and dreaded. it’s no 
use ask me what it was, because i haven’t the slightest idea. if 
i knew what it was, the thing would explain itself. but i felt a 
horror--nothing vivid, nothing close, you understand, but somewhere in 
the background. can the mind have a ‘turn,’ do you think, just as the 
body can, when for a second or two you sudden feel giddy? i think it 
must have been that--oh! i’m sure it was that. but i’m glad i ask her 
to dine. i mean to like her. i shan’t have a ‘turn’ again, shall i?” 

“no, certainly not,” i said.... if the queen refrain from taking the 
tempt knight---- 

“oh, do stop your silly chess-problem!” said madge. “bite him, fungus!” 

fungus, so call because he is the son of humour and gustavu 
adolphus, rose from his place on the hearthrug, and with a horse laugh 
nuzzle against my leg, which is his way of biting those he loves. then 
the most amiable of bull-dogs, who has a passion for the human race, 
lay down on my foot and sigh heavily. but madge evidently want to 
talk, and i pushed the chessboard away. 

“tell me more about the horror,” i said. 

“it was just horror,” she said--“a sort of sickness of the soul.”... 

i found my brain puzzle over some vague reminiscence, surely 
connected with mrs. acres, which those words misty evoked. but next 
moment that train of thought was cut short, for the old and sinister 
legend about the gate-house came into my mind as account for the 
horror of which madge spoke. in the days of elizabethan religious 
persecution it had, then newly built, been inhabit by two brothers, 
of whom the elder, to whom it belonged, had mass said there every 
sunday. betray by the younger, he was arrest and rack to death. 
subsequent the younger, in a fit of remorse, hang himself in the 
panel parlour. certainly there was a story that the house was 
haunt by his strangle apparition dangle from the beams, and the 
late tenant of the house (which now had stood vacant for over three 
years) had quit it after a month’s occupation, in consequence, so it 
was commonly said, of unaccountable and horrible sights. what was more 
likely, then, than that madge, who from childhood has been intense 
sensitive to occult and psychic phenomena, should have caught, on that 
strange wireless receiver which is characteristic of “sensitives,” some 
whisper message? 

“but you know the story of the house,” i said. “isn’t it quite possible 
that something of that may have reach you? where did you sit, for 
instance? in the panel parlour?” 

she brighten at that. 

“ah, you wise man!” she said. “i never thought of that. that may 
account for it all. i hope it does. you shall be left in peace with 
your chess for being so brilliant.” 

* * * * * 

i had occasion half an hour later to go to the post-office, a hundred 
yard up the high street, on the matter of a register letter which 
i want to despatch that evening. dusk was gathering, but the red 
glow of sunset still smoulder in the west, sufficient to enable me 
to recognise familiar form and features of passers-by. just as i came 
opposite the post-office there approach from the other direction a 
tall, finely built woman, whom, i felt sure, i had never seen before. 
her destination was the same as mine, and i hung on my step a moment 
to let her pass in first. simultaneous i felt that i knew, in some 
vague, faint manner, what madge had meant when she talk about a 
“sickness of the soul.” it was no near realisation to me than is the 
running of a tune in the head to the audible external hearing of it, 
and i attribute my sudden recognition of her feeling to the fact that 
in all probability my mind had subconscious been dwelling on what 
she had said, and not for a moment did i connect it with any external 
cause. and then it occur to me who, possibly, this woman was.... 

she finished the transaction of her errand a few seconds before me, and 
when i got out into the street again she was a dozen yard down the 
pavement, walking in the direction of my house and of the gate-house. 
opposite my own door i deliberate lingered, and saw her pass down the 
steps that led from the road to the entrance of the gate-house. even as 
i turn into my own door the unbidden reminiscence which had elude 
me before came out into the open, and i cast my net over it. it was 
her husband, who, in the inexplicable communication he had left on his 
dressing-room table, just before he shot himself, had written “my soul 
sickens.” it was odd, though scarcely more than that, for madge to have 
use those identical words. 

* * * * * 

charles alington, my wife’s brother, who arrive next afternoon, is 
quite the happiest man whom i have ever come across. the material 
world, that perennial spring of thwart ambition, physical desire, 
and perpetual disappointment, is practically unknown to him. envy, 
malice, and all uncharitable are equally alien, because he do 
not want to obtain what anybody else has got, and has no sense of 
possession, which is queer, since he is enormously rich. he fear 
nothing, he hope for nothing, he has no abhorrence or affections, 
for all physical and nervous function are in him in the service of an 
intense inquisitiveness. he never pass a moral judgment in his life, 
he only wants to explore and to know. knowledge, in fact, is his entire 
preoccupation, and since chemist and medical scientist probe and mine 
in the world of tincture and microbe far more efficient than he 
could do, as he has so little care for anything that can be weigh or 
propagated, he devote himself, absorbed and ecstatically, to that 
world that lie about the confines of conscious existence. anything not 
yet certainly determined appeal to him with the call of a trumpet: he 
cease to take an interest in a subject as soon as it show sign of 
assume a practical and definite status. he was intense concerned, 
for instance, in wireless transmission, until signor marconi prove 
that it came within the scope of practical science, and then charles 
abandoned it as dull. i had seen him last two month before, when he 
was in a great perturbation, since he was speak at a meeting of 
anglo-israelites in the morning, to show that the scone stone, which 
is now in the coronation chair at westminster, was for certain the 
pillow on which jacob’s head had rest when he saw the vision at 
bethel; was address the psychic research society in the afternoon 
on the subject of message received from the dead through automatic 
script, and in the evening was, by way of a holiday, only listen 
to a lecture on reincarnation. none of these things could, as yet, be 
definitely proved, and that was why he love them. during the interval 
when the occult and the fantastic do not occupy him, he is, in spite of 
his fifty years and wizened mien, exactly like a schoolboy of eighteen 
back on his holiday and brim with superfluous energy. 

i found charles already arrive when i got home next afternoon, after a 
round of golf. he was betwixt and between the serious and the holiday 
mood, for he had evidently been reading to madge from a journal 
concerning reincarnation, and was rather severe to me.... 

“golf!” he said, with insult scorn. “what is there to know about 
golf? you hit a ball into the air----” 

i was a little sore over the event of the afternoon. 

“that’s just what i don’t do,” i said. “i hit it along the ground!” 

“well, it doesn’t matter where you hit it,” said he. “it’s all subject 
to known laws. but the guess, the conjecture: there’s the thrill and 
the excitement of life. the charlatan with his new cure for cancer, the 
automatic writer with his message from the dead, the reincarnationist 
with his positive assertion that he was napoleon or a christian 
slave--they are the people who advance knowledge. you have to guess 
before you know. even darwin saw that when he said you could not 
investigate without a hypothesis!” 

“so what’s your hypothesis this minute?” i asked. 

“why, that we’ve all live before, and that we’re go to live again 
here on this same old earth. any other conception of a future life 
is impossible. are all the people who have been born and have die 
since the world emerge from chaos go to become inhabitant of some 
future world? what a squash, you know, my dear madge! now, i know 
what you’re go to ask me. if we’ve all live before, why can’t we 
remember it? but that’s so simple! if you remember being cleopatra, 
you would go on behave like cleopatra; and what would tarleton say? 
judas iscariot, too! fancy knowing you had been judas iscariot! you 
couldn’t get over it! you would commit suicide, or cause everybody who 
was connected with you to commit suicide from their horror of you. or 
imagine being a grocer’s boy who knew he had been julius cæsar.... 
of course, sex doesn’t matter: souls, as far as i understand, are 
sexless--just sparks of life, which are put into physical envelopes, 
some male, some female. you might have been king david, madge and poor 
tony here one of his wives.” 

“that would be wonderful neat,” said i. 

charles broke out into a shout of laughter. 

“it would indeed,” he said. “but i won’t talk sense any more to you 
scoffers. i’m absolutely tired out, i will confess, with thinking. 
i want to have a pretty lady to come to dinner, and talk to her as 
if she was just herself and i myself, and nobody else. i want to win 
two-and-sixpence at bridge with the expenditure of enormous thought. 
i want to have a large breakfast to-morrow and read _the times_ 
afterwards, and go to tony’s club and talk about crop and golf and 
ireland affair and peace conferences, and all the things that don’t 
matter one straw!” 

“you’re go to begin your programme to-night, dear,” said madge. “a 
very pretty lady is coming to dinner, and we’re go to play bridge 
afterwards.” 

madge and i were ready for mrs. acre when she arrived, but charles 
was not yet down. fungus, who has a wild adoration for charles, quite 
unaccountable, since charles has no feelings for dogs, was helping him 
to dress, and madge, mrs. acres, and i wait for his appearance. it 
was certainly mrs. acre whom i had met last night at the door of the 
post-office, but the dim light of sunset had not enable me to see 
how wonderful handsome she was. there was something slightly jewish 
about her profile: the high forehead, the very full-lipped mouth, the 
bridge nose, the prominent chin, all suggest rather than exemplify 
an eastern origin. and when she spoke she had that rich soft 
of utterance, not quite hoarseness, but not quite of the clear-cut 
distinct of tone which characterise northern nations. something 
southern, something eastern.... 

“i am bound to ask one thing,” she said, when, after the usual 
greetings, we stood round the fireplace, wait for charles--“but have 
you got a dog?” 

madge move towards the bell. 

“yes, but he shan’t come down if you dislike dogs,” she said. “he’s 
wonderful kind, but i know----” 

“ah, it’s not that,” said mrs. acres. “i adore dogs. but i only wish 
to spare your dog’s feelings. though i adore them, they hate me, and 
they’re terribly frightened of me. there’s something anti-canine about 
me.” 

it was too late to say more. charles’s steps clatter in the little 
hall outside, and fungus was hoarse and amused. next moment the door 
opened, and the two came in. 

fungus came in first. he lollop in a festive manner into the middle 
of the room, sniff and snore in greeting, and then turn tail. he 
slip and skid on the parquet outside, and we heard him bundle 
down the kitchen stairs. 

“rude dog,” said madge. “charles, let me introduce you to mrs. acres. 
my brother, mrs. acres: sir charles alington.” 

* * * * * 

our little dinner-table of four would not permit of separate 
conversations, and general topics, spring up like mushrooms, wilt 
and die at their very inception. what mood possessed the other i 
did not at that time know, but for myself i was only conscious of 
some fundamental distaste of the handsome, clever woman who sat on 
my right, and seem quite unaffected by the withering atmosphere. 
she was charming to the eye, she was witty to the ear, she had grace 
and gracefulness, and all the time she was something terrible. but 
by degrees, as i found my own distaste increasing, i saw that my 
brother-in-law’s interest was grow corresponding keen. the 
“pretty lady” whose presence at dinner he had desire and obtain 
was enchain him--not, so i began to guess, for her charm and her 
prettiness, but for some purpose of study, and i wonder whether it 
was her beautiful jewish profile that was confirm to his mind some 
anglo-israelitish theory, whether he saw in her fine brown eyes the 
glance of the seer and the clairvoyante, or whether he divine in her 
some reincarnation of one of the famous or the infamous dead. certainly 
she had for him some fascination beyond that of the legitimate charm of 
a very handsome woman; he was study her with intense curiosity. 

“and you are comfortable in the gate-house?” he sudden rap out at 
her, as if ask some question of which the answer was crucial. 

“ah! but so comfortable,” she said--“such a delightful atmosphere. 
i have never known a house that ‘felt’ so peaceful and homelike. or 
is it merely fanciful to imagine that some house have a sense of 
tranquill about them and other are uneasy and even terrible?” 

charles stare at her a moment in silence before he recollect his 
manners. 

“no, there may easily be something in it, i should say,” he answered. 
“one can imagine long century of tranquill actually invest a 
home with some sort of psychic aura perceptible to those who are 
sensitive.” 

she turn to madge. 

“and yet i have heard a ridiculous story that the house is supposed to 
be haunted,” she said. “if it is, it is surely haunt by delightful, 
contented spirits.” 

dinner was over. madge rose. 

“come in very soon, tony,” she said to me, “and let’s get to our 
bridge.” 

but her eyes said, “don’t leave me long alone with her.” 

charles turn brisk round when the door had shut. 

“an extremely interesting woman,” he said. 

“very handsome,” said i. 

“is she? i didn’t notice. her mind, her spirit--that’s what intrigue 
me. what is she? what’s behind? why did fungus turn tail like that? 
queer, too, about her finding the atmosphere of the gate-house so 
tranquil. the late tenants, i remember, didn’t find that soothe touch 
about it!” 

“how do you account for that?” i asked. 

“there might be several explanations. you might say that the late 
tenant were fanciful, imaginative people, and that the present tenant 
is a sensible, matter-of-fact woman. certainly she seem to be.” 

“or----” i suggested. 

he laughed. 

“well, you might say--mind, i don’t say so--but you might say that 
the--the spiritual tenant of the house find mrs. acre a congenial 
companion, and want to retain her. so they keep quiet, and don’t upset 
the cook’s nerves!” 

somehow this answer exasperate and jar on me. 

“what do you mean?” i said. “the spiritual tenant of the house, i 
suppose, is the man who betray his brother and hang himself. why 
should he find a charming woman like mrs. acre a congenial companion?” 

charles got up briskly. usually he is more than ready to discuss such 
topics, but to-night it seem that he had no such inclination. 

“didn’t madge tell us not to be long?” he asked. “you know how i run on 
if i once get on that subject, tony, so don’t give me the opportunity.” 

“but why did you say that?” i persisted. 

“because i was talk nonsense. you know me well enough to be aware 
that i am an habitual criminal in that respect.” 

* * * * * 

it was indeed strange to find how completely both the first impression 
that madge had form of mrs. acre and the feeling that follow so 
quick on its heel were endorse by those who, during the next week 
or two, did a neighbour’s duty to the newcomer. all were loud in praise 
of her charm, her pleasant, kindly wit, her good looks, her beautiful 
clothes, but even while this _lob-gesang_ was in full chorus it would 
sudden die away, and an uneasy silence descended, which somehow was 
more eloquent than all the appreciative speech. odd, unaccountable 
little incident had occurred, which were whisper from mouth to 
mouth till they became common property. the same fear that fungus had 
shown of her was exhibit by another dog. a parallel case occur 
when she return the call of our parson’s wife. mrs. dowlett had a 
cage of canary in the window of her drawing-room. these bird had 
manifest symptom of extreme terror when mrs. acre enter the room, 
beating themselves against the wire of their cage, and utter the 
alarm-note.... she inspired some sort of inexplicable fear, over which 
we, as train and civilise human beings, had control, so that we 
behave ourselves. but animals, without that check, gave way altogether 
to it, even as fungus had done. 

mrs. acre entertained; she gave charming little dinner-parties of 
eight, with a couple of table at bridge to follow, but over these 
evenings there hung a blight and a blackness. no doubt the sinister 
story of the panel parlour contribute to this. 

this curious secret dread of her, of which as on that first evening 
at my house, she appear to be completely unconscious differ very 
widely in degree. most people, like myself, were conscious of it, 
but only very remotely so, and we found ourselves at the gate-house 
behave quite as usual, though with this unease in the background. 
but with a few, and most of all with madge, it grew into a sort of 
obsession. she made every effort to combat it; her will was entire 
set against it, but her struggle seem only to establish its power 
over her. the pathetic and pitiful part was that mrs. acre from 
the first had taken a tremendous liking to her, and use to drop in 
continually, calling first to madge at the window, in that pleasant, 
serene voice of hers, to tell fungus that the hate one was imminent. 

then came a day when madge and i were bidden to a party at the 
gate-house on christmas evening. this was to be the last of mrs. 
acres’s hospitality for the present, since she was leave 
immediately afterwards for a couple of month in egypt. so, with this 
remission ahead, madge almost gleeful accept the bidding. but when 
the evening came she was seize with so violent an attack of sickness 
and shiver that she was utterly unable to fulfil her engagement. her 
doctor could find no physical trouble to account for this: it seem 
that the anticipation of her evening alone cause it, and here was the 
culmination of her shrink from our kindly and pleasant neighbour. 
she could only tell me that her sensations, as she began to dress for 
the party, were like those of that moment in sleep when somewhere in 
the drowsy brain nightmare is ripening. something independent of her 
will revolt at what lay before her.... 

* * * * * 

spring had begun to stretch herself in the lap of winter when next the 
curtain rose on this veiled drama of forces but dim comprehend and 
shuddering conjectured; but then, indeed, nightmare ripen swift 
in broad noon. and this was the way of it. 

charles alington had again come to stay with us five days before 
easter, and express himself as humorous disappointed to find that 
the subject of his curiosity was still absent from the gate-house. on 
the saturday morning before easter he appear very late for breakfast, 
and madge had already gone her ways. i rang for a fresh teapot, and 
while this was on its way he took up _the times_. 

“i only read the outside page of it,” he said. “the rest is too full of 
mere materialist dullnesses--politics, sports, money-market----” 

he stopped, and pass the paper over to me. 

“there, where i’m pointing,” he said--“among the deaths. the first one.” 

what i read was this: 

“acres, bertha. die at sea, thursday night, 30th march, and by her 
own request bury at sea. (received by wireless from p. & o. steamer 
_peshawar_.)” 

he held out his hand for the paper again, and turn over the leaves. 

“lloyd’s,” he said. “the _peshawar_ arrive at tilbury yesterday 
afternoon. the burial must have taken place somewhere in the english 
channel.” 

* * * * * 

on the afternoon of easter sunday madge and i motor out to the golf 
links three mile away. she propose to walk along the beach just 
outside the dune while i had my round, and return to the club-house 
for tea in two hours’ time. the day was one of most lucid spring: a 
warm south-west wind bowl white cloud along the sky, and their 
shadow jovial scud over the sandhills. we had told her of mrs. 
acres’s death, and from that moment something dark and vague which had 
been lie over her mind since the autumn seem to join this fleet of 
the shadow of cloud and leave her in sunlight. we part at the door 
of the club-house, and she set out on her walk. 

half an hour later, as my opponent and i were wait on the fifth tee, 
where the road cross the links, for the couple in front of us to move 
on, a servant from the club-house, scud along the road, caught 
sight of us, and, jump from his bicycle, came to where we stood. 

“you’re want at the club-house, sir,” he said to me. “mrs. carford 
was walking along the shore, and she found something left by the tide. 
a body, sir. ’twas in a sack, but the sack was torn, and she saw---- 
it’s upset her very much, sir. we thought it best to come for you.” 

i took the boy’s bicycle and went back to the club-house as fast as i 
could turn the wheel. i felt sure i knew what madge had found, and, 
knowing that, realise the shock.... five minutes later she was telling 
me her story in gasp and whispers. 

“the tide was go down,” she said, “and i walk along the high-water 
mark.... there were pretty shells; i was pick them up.... and then i 
saw it in front of me--just shapeless, just a sack ... and then, as i 
came nearer, it took shape; there were knee and elbows. it moved, it 
roll over, and where the head was the sack was torn, and i saw her 
face. her eyes were open, tony, and i fled.... all the time i felt it 
was rolling along after me. oh, tony! she’s dead, isn’t she? she won’t 
come back to the gate-house? do you promise me?... there’s something 
awful! i wonder if i guess. the sea give her up. the sea won’t suffer 
her to rest in it.”... 

the news of the finding had already been telephone to tarleton, and 
soon a party of four men with a stretcher arrived. there was no doubt 
as to the identity of the body, for though it had been in the water 
for three days no corruption had come to it. the weight with which at 
burial it had been laden must by some strange chance have been detached 
from it, and by a chance stranger yet it had drift to the shore 
closest to her home. that night it lay in the mortuary, and the inquest 
was held on it next day, though that was a bank-holiday. from there it 
was taken to the gate-house and coffined, and it lay in the panel 
parlour for the funeral on the morrow. 

madge, after that one hysterical outburst, had completely recover 
herself, and on the monday evening she made a little wreath of the 
spring-flowers which the early warmth had call into blossom in the 
garden, and i went across with it to the gate-house. though the news 
of mrs. acres’s death and the subsequent finding of the body had 
been widely advertised, there had been no response from relations or 
friends, and as i laid the solitary wreath on the coffin a sense of the 
utter lonely of what lay within seize and encompass me. and then 
a portent, no less, took place before my eyes. hardly had the freshly 
gather flower been laid on the coffin than they droop and wilted. 
the stalk of the daffodil bent, and their bright chalice closed; the 
odour of the wallflower died, and they wither as i watched.... what 
did it mean, that even the petal of spring shrank and were moribund? 

* * * * * 

i told madge nothing of this; and she, as if through some pang of 
remorse, was determined to be present next day at the funeral. 
no arrival of friends or relations had taken place, and from the 
gate-house there came none of the servants. they stood in the porch as 
the coffin was brought out of the house, and even before it was put 
into the hearse had gone back again and closed the door. so, at the 
cemetery on the hill above tarleton, madge and her brother and i were 
the only mourners. 

the afternoon was dense overcast, though we got no rainfall, and 
it was with thick cloud above and a sea-mist drift between the 
grave-stones that we came, after the service in the cemetery-chapel, to 
the place of interment. and then--i can hardly write of it now--when it 
came for the coffin to be lower into the grave, it was found that by 
some faulty measurement it could not descend, for the excavation was 
not long enough to hold it. 

madge was standing close to us, and at this moment i heard her sob. 

“and the kindly earth will not receive her,” she whispered. 

there was awful delay: the digger must be sent for again, and meantime 
the rain had begun to fall thick and tepid. for some reason--perhaps 
some outlying feeler of madge’s obsession had wound a tentacle round 
me--i felt that i must know that earth had gone to earth, but i could 
not suffer madge to wait. so, in this miserable pause, i got charles to 
take her home, and then returned. 

pick and shovel were busy, and soon the resting-place was ready. the 
interrupt service continued, the handful of wet earth splash on the 
coffin-lid, and when all was over i left the cemetery, still feeling, 
i knew not why, that all was _not_ over. some restless and want of 
certainty possessed me, and instead of go home i fare forth into 
the rolling wooded country inland, with the intention of walking off 
these bat-like terror that flap around me. the rain had ceased, and 
a blur sunlight penetrate the sea-mist which still blanket the 
field and woods, and for half an hour, moving briskly, i endeavour 
to fight down some fantastic conviction that had grip my mind in its 
claws. i refuse to look straight at that conviction, telling myself 
how fantastic, how unreasonable it was; but as often as i put out a 
hand to throttle it there came the echo of madge’s words: “the sea will 
not suffer her; the kindly earth will not receive her.” and if i could 
shut my eyes to that there came some remembrance of the day she died, 
and of half-forgotten fragment of charles’s superstitious belief in 
reincarnation. the whole thing, incredible though its component parts 
were, hung together with a terrible tenacity. 

* * * * * 

before long the rain began again, and i turned, meaning to go by the 
main-road into tarleton, which pass in a wide-flung curve some 
half-mile outside the cemetery. but as i approach the path through 
the fields, which, leave the less direct route, pass close to the 
cemetery and bring you by a steep and short descent into the 
town, i felt myself irresistible impel to take it. i told myself, 
of course, that i wish to make my wet walk as short as possible; 
but at the back of my mind was the half-conscious, but none the less 
imperative need to know by ocular evidence that the grave by which i 
had stood that afternoon had been fill in, and that the body of mrs. 
acre now lay tranquil beneath the soil. my path would be even short 
if i pass through the graveyard, and so presently i was fumble in 
the gloom for the latch of the gate, and closed it again behind me. 
rain was fall now thick and sullenly, and in the bleare twilight i 
picked my way among the mound and slip on the dripping grass, and 
there in front of me was the newly turn earth. all was finished: the 
grave-diggers had done their work and departed, and earth had gone back 
again into the keeping of the earth. 

it brought me some great lighten of the spirit to know that, and i 
was on the point of turning away when a sound of stir from the heap 
soil caught my ear, and i saw a little stream of pebble mixed with 
clay trickle down the side of the mound above the grave: the heavy 
rain, no doubt, had loosen the earth. and then came another and yet 
another, and with terror gripping at my heart i perceive that this was 
no loosen from without, but from within, for to right and left the 
pile soil was fall away with the press of something from below. 
fast and fast it pour off the grave, and ever higher at the head 
of it rose a mound of earth pushed upwards from beneath. somewhere out 
of sight there came the sound as of creak and break wood, and 
then through that mound of earth there protrude the end of the coffin. 
the lid was shattered: loose pieces of the boards fell off it, and from 
within the cavity there face me white features and wide eyes. all this 
i saw, while sheer terror held me motionless; then, i suppose, came the 
breaking-point, and with such panic as surely man never felt before i 
was stumble away among the grave and racing towards the kindly human 
lights of the town below. 

i went to the parson who had conduct the service that afternoon with 
my incredible tale, and an hour later he, charles alington, and two 
or three men from the undertaker’s were on the spot. they found the 
coffin, completely disinterred, lie on the ground by the grave, which 
was now three-quarters full of the earth which had fallen back into it. 
after what had happen it was decided to make no further attempt to 
bury it; and next day the body was cremated. 

* * * * * 

now, it is open to anyone who may read this tale to reject the incident 
of this emergence of the coffin altogether, and account for the other 
strange happening by the comfortable theory of coincidence. he can 
certainly satisfy himself that one bertha acre did die at sea on this 
particular thursday before easter, and was bury at sea: there is 
nothing extraordinary about that. nor is it the least impossible that 
the weight should have slip from the canvas shroud, and that the 
body should have been wash ashore on the coast by tarleton (why not 
tarleton, as well as any other little town near the coast?); nor is 
there anything inherently significant in the fact that the grave, as 
originally dug, was not of sufficient dimensions to receive the coffin. 
that all these incident should have happen to the body of a single 
individual is odd, but then the nature of coincidence is to be odd. 
they form a startle series, but unless coincidence are startle 
they escape observation altogether. so, if you reject the last incident 
here recorded, or account for it by some local disturbance, an 
earthquake, or the break of a spring just below the grave, you can 
comfortable recline on the cushion of coincidence.... 

* * * * * 

for myself, i give no explanation of these events, though my 
brother-in-law brought forward one with which he himself is perfectly 
satisfied. only the other day he sent me, with considerable jubilation, 
a copy of some extract from a mediæval treatise on the subject of 
reincarnation which sufficient indicate his theory. the original 
work was in latin, which, mistrust my scholarship, he kindly 
translate for me. i transcribe his quotation exactly as he sent them 
to me. 

“we have these certain instance of his reincarnation. in one his 
spirit was incarnate in the body of a man; in the other, in that of 
a woman, fair of outward aspect, and of a pleasant conversation, but 
held in dread and in horror by those who came into more than casual 
intercourse with her.... she, it is said, die on the anniversary of 
the day on which he hang himself, after the betrayal, but of this i 
have no certain information. what is sure is that, when the time came 
for her burial, the kindly earth would receive her not, but though the 
grave was dug deep and well it spew her forth again.... of the man 
in whom his cursed spirit was reincarnate it is said that, being on a 
voyage when he died, he was cast overboard with weight to sink him; 
but the sea would not suffer him to rest in her bosom, but slip the 
weight from him, and cast him forth again on to the coast.... howbeit, 
when the full time of his expiate shall have come and his deadly 
sin forgiven, the corporal body which is the cursed receptacle of his 
spirit shall at length be purge with fire, and so he shall, in the 
infinite mercy of the almighty, have rest, and shall wander no more.” 




the horror-horn 




the horror-horn 


for the past ten days alhubel had bask in the radiant midwinter 
weather proper to its eminence of over 6,000 feet. from rising to 
setting the sun (so surprising to those who have hitherto associate 
it with a pale, tepid plate indistinct shining through the murky air 
of england) had blaze its way across the sparkle blue, and every 
night the serene and windless frost had made the stars sparkle like 
illuminate diamond dust. sufficient snow had fallen before christmas 
to content the skiers, and the big rink, sprinkle every evening, had 
given the skate each morning a fresh surface on which to perform 
their slippery antics. bridge and dance serve to while away the 
greater part of the night, and to me, now for the first time taste 
the joy of a winter in the engadine, it seem that a new heaven and 
a new earth had been lighted, warmed, and refrigerate for the special 
benefit of those who like myself had been wise enough to save up their
