days of holiday for the winter. 

but a break came in these ideal conditions: one afternoon the sun grew 
vapour-veiled and up the valley from the north-west a wind frozen with 
mile of travel over ice-bound hill-sides began scout through the 
calm hall of the heavens. soon it grew dust with snow, first in 
small flake driven almost horizontal before its congeal breath 
and then in large tuft as of swansdown. and though all day for a 
fortnight before the fate of nation and life and death had seem 
to me of far less importance than to get certain tracing of the 
skate-blades on the ice of proper shape and size, it now seem that 
the one paramount consideration was to hurry back to the hotel for 
shelter: it was wise to leave rocking-turns alone than to be frozen in 
their quest. 

i had come out here with my cousin, professor ingram, the celebrated 
physiologist and alpine climber. during the serene of the last 
fortnight he had made a couple of notable winter ascents, but this 
morning his weather-wisdom had mistrust the sign of the heavens, and 
instead of attempt the ascent of the piz passug he had wait to see 
whether his misgiving justified themselves. so there he sat now in the 
hall of the admirable hotel with his feet on the hot-water pipes and 
the latest delivery of the english post in his hands. this contained 
a pamphlet concerning the result of the mount everest expedition, of 
which he had just finished the peruse when i entered. 

“a very interesting report,” he said, passing it to me, “and they 
certainly deserve to succeed next year. but who can tell, what that 
final six thousand feet may entail? six thousand feet more when you 
have already accomplished twenty-three thousand do not seem much, 
but at present no one know whether the human frame can stand exertion 
at such a height. it may affect not the lung and heart only, but 
possibly the brain. delirious hallucination may occur. in fact, if i 
did not know better, i should have said that one such hallucination had 
occur to the climber already.” 

“and what was that?” i asked. 

“you will find that they thought they came across the track of some 
naked human foot at a great altitude. that looks at first sight like 
an hallucination. what more natural than that a brain excited and 
exhilarate by the extreme height should have interpret certain mark 
in the snow as the footprint of a human being? every bodily organ 
at these altitude is exert itself to the utmost to do its work, 
and the brain seize on those mark in the snow and say ‘yes, i’m 
all right, i’m do my job, and i perceive mark in the snow which 
i affirm are human footprints.’ you know, even at this altitude, how 
restless and eager the brain is, how vividly, as you told me, you dream 
at night. multiply that stimulus and that consequent eager and 
restless by three, and how natural that the brain should harbour 
illusions! what after all is the delirium which often accompany high 
fever but the effort of the brain to do its work under the pressure 
of feverish conditions? it is so eager to continue perceive that it 
perceive things which have no existence!” 

“and yet you don’t think that these naked human footprint were 
illusions,” said i. “you told me you would have thought so, if you had 
not known better.” 

he shift in his chair and look out of the window a moment. the air 
was thick now with the density of the big snow-flakes that were driven 
along by the squeal north-west gale. 

“quite so,” he said. “in all probability the human footprint were 
real human footprints. i expect that they were the footprints, anyhow, 
of a being more nearly a man than anything else. my reason for saying 
so is that i know such being exist. i have even seen quite near at 
hand--and i assure you i did not wish to be near in spite of my 
intense curiosity--the creature, shall we say, which would make such 
footprints. and if the snow was not so dense, i could show you the 
place where i saw him.” 

he pointed straight out of the window, where across the valley lie 
the huge tower of the ungeheuerhorn with the carve pinnacle of rock 
at the top like some gigantic rhinoceros-horn. on one side only, as i 
knew, was the mountain practicable, and that for none but the finest 
climbers; on the other three a succession of ledge and precipice 
render it unscalable. two thousand feet of sheer rock form the tower; 
below are five hundred feet of fallen boulders, up to the edge of which 
grow dense wood of larch and pine. 

“upon the ungeheuerhorn?” i asked. 

“yes. up till twenty years ago it had never been ascended, and i, like 
several others, spent a lot of time in try to find a route up it. 
my guide and i sometimes spent three nights together at the hut beside 
the blumen glacier, prowl round it, and it was by luck really that 
we found the route, for the mountain looks even more impracticable 
from the far side than it do from this. but one day we found a long, 
transverse fissure in the side which led to a negotiable ledge; then 
there came a slant ice couloir which you could not see till you got 
to the foot of it. however, i need not go into that.” 

the big room where we sat was filling up with cheerful group driven 
indoors by this sudden gale and snowfall, and the cackle of merry 
tongue grew loud. the band, too, that invariable appanage of tea-time 
at switzerland resorts, had begun to tune up for the usual potpourri from the 
works of puccini. next moment the sugary, sentimental melody began. 

“strange contrast!” said ingram. “here are we sitting warm and cosy, 
our ear pleasant tickle with these little baby tune and outside is 
the great storm grow more violent every moment, and swirl round 
the austere cliff of the ungeheuerhorn: the horror-horn, as indeed it 
was to me.” 

“i want to hear all about it,” i said. “every detail: make a short 
story long, if it’s short. i want to know why it’s _your_ horror-horn?” 

“well, chanton and i (he was my guide) use to spend days prowl 
about the cliffs, making a little progress on one side and then being 
stopped, and gain perhaps five hundred feet on another side and then 
being confront by some insuperable obstacle, till the day when by 
luck we found the route. chanton never like the job, for some reason 
that i could not fathom. it was not because of the difficulty or danger 
of the climbing, for he was the most fearless man i have ever met 
when dealing with rocks and ice, but he was always insistent that we 
should get off the mountain and back to the blumen hut before sunset. 
he was scarcely easy even when we had got back to shelter and lock 
and barred the door, and i well remember one night when, as we ate our 
supper, we heard some animal, a wolf probably, howling somewhere out in 
the night. a positive panic seize him, and i don’t think he closed his 
eyes till morning. it struck me then that there might be some grisly 
legend about the mountain, connected possibly with its name, and next 
day i ask him why the peak was call the horror-horn. he put the 
question off at first, and said that, like the schreckhorn, its name 
was due to its precipice and fall stones; but when i pressed him 
further he acknowledge that there was a legend about it, which his 
father had told him. there were creatures, so it was supposed, that 
live in its caves, things human in shape, and covered, except for 
the face and hands, with long black hair. they were dwarf in size, 
four feet high or thereabouts, but of prodigious strength and agility, 
remnant of some wild primeval race. it seem that they were still 
in an upward stage of evolution, or so i guessed, for the story ran 
that sometimes girl had been carry off by them, not as prey, and 
not for any such fate as for those capture by cannibals, but to be 
bred from. young men also had been rape by them, to be mate with 
the female of their tribe. all this look as if the creatures, as i 
said, were tend towards humanity. but naturally i did not believe a 
word of it, as applied to the conditions of the present day. century 
ago, conceivably, there may have been such beings, and, with the 
extraordinary tenacity of tradition, the news of this had been hand 
down and was still current round the hearth of the peasants. as for 
their numbers, chanton told me that three had been once seen together 
by a man who owe to his swift on ski had escape to tell the 
tale. this man, he averred, was no other than his grandfather, who 
had been benighted one winter evening as he pass through the dense 
wood below the ungeheuerhorn, and chanton supposed that they had 
been driven down to these lower altitude in search of food during 
severe winter weather, for otherwise the record sight of them 
had always taken place among the rocks of the peak itself. they had 
pursue his grandfather, then a young man, at an extraordinarily swift 
canter, running sometimes upright as men run, sometimes on all-fours 
in the manner of beasts, and their howl were just such as that we had 
heard that night in the blumen hut. such at any rate was the story 
chanton told me, and, like you, i regard it as the very moonshine 
of superstition. but the very next day i had reason to reconsider my 
judgment about it. 

“it was on that day that after a week of exploration we hit on the 
only route at present known to the top of our peak. we start as soon 
as there was light enough to climb by, for, as you may guess, on very 
difficult rocks it is impossible to climb by lantern or moonlight. 
we hit on the long fissure i have spoken of, we explore the ledge 
which from below seem to end in nothingness, and with an hour’s 
step-cutting ascend the couloir which led upwards from it. from there 
onwards it was a rock-climb, certainly of considerable difficulty, but 
with no heart-breaking discovery ahead, and it was about nine in the 
morning that we stood on the top. we did not wait there long, for that 
side of the mountain is rake by fall stone loosened, when the sun 
grow hot, from the ice that hold them, and we made haste to pass the 
ledge where the falls are most frequent. after that there was the long 
fissure to descend, a matter of no great difficulty, and we were at 
the end of our work by midday, both of us, as you may imagine, in the 
state of the highest elation. 

“a long and tiresome scramble among the huge boulder at the foot of 
the cliff then lay before us. here the hill-side is very porous and 
great cave extend far into the mountain. we had unroped at the base of 
the fissure, and were pick our way as seem good to either of us 
among these fallen rocks, many of them big than an ordinary house, 
when, on coming round the corner of one of these, i saw that which 
made it clear that the story chanton had told me were no figment of 
traditional superstition. 

“not twenty yard in front of me lay one of the being of which he 
had spoken. there it sprawl naked and bask on its back with face 
turn up to the sun, which its narrow eyes regard unwinking. in form 
it was completely human, but the growth of hair that cover limb and 
trunk alike almost completely hid the sun-tanned skin beneath. but its 
face, save for the down on its cheek and chin, was hairless, and i 
look on a countenance the sensual and malevolent bestiality of which 
froze me with horror. had the creature been an animal, one would have 
felt scarcely a shudder at the gross animalism of it; the horror lay in 
the fact that it was a man. there lay by it a couple of gnaw bones, 
and, its meal finished, it was lazy licking its protuberant lips, 
from which came a purr murmur of content. with one hand it scratch 
the thick hair on its belly, in the other it held one of these bones, 
which presently split in half beneath the pressure of its finger and 
thumb. but my horror was not base on the information of what happen 
to those men whom these creature caught, it was due only to my 
proximity to a thing so human and so infernal. the peak, of which the 
ascent had a moment ago fill us with such elated satisfaction, became 
to me an ungeheuerhorn indeed, for it was the home of being more awful 
than the delirium of nightmare could ever have conceived. 

“chanton was a dozen pace behind me, and with a backward wave of 
my hand i cause him to halt. then withdraw myself with infinite 
precaution, so as not to attract the gaze of that bask creature, 
i slip back round the rock, whisper to him what i had seen, and 
with blanch face we made a long detour, peer round every corner, 
and crouch low, not knowing that at any step we might not come upon 
another of these beings, or that from the mouth of one of these cave 
in the mountain-side there might not appear another of those hairless 
and dreadful faces, with perhaps this time the breast and insignia of 
womanhood. that would have been the worst of all. 

“luck favoured us, for we made our way among the boulder and shift 
stones, the rattle of which might at any moment have betray us, 
without a repetition of my experience, and once among the tree we ran 
as if the fury themselves were in pursuit. well now did i understand, 
though i dare say i cannot convey, the qualm of chanton’s mind when he 
spoke to me of these creatures. their very humanity was what made them 
so terrible, the fact that they were of the same race as ourselves, but 
of a type so abysmal degrade that the most brutal and inhuman of men 
would have seem angel in comparison.” 

the music of the small band was over before he had finished the 
narrative, and the chatter group round the tea-table had dispersed. 
he pause a moment. 

“there was a horror of the spirit,” he said, “which i experienced 
then, from which, i verily believe, i have never entire recovered. 
i saw then how terrible a living thing could be, and how terrible, in 
consequence, was life itself. in us all i suppose lurk some inherit 
germ of that ineffable bestiality, and who know whether, sterile as it 
has apparently become in the course of centuries, it might not fructify 
again. when i saw that creature sun itself, i look into the abyss 
out of which we have crawled. and these creature are try to crawl 
out of it now, if they exist any longer. certainly for the last twenty 
years there has been no record of their being seen, until we come to 
this story of the footprint seen by the climber on everest. if that 
is authentic, if the party did not mistake the footprint of some bear, 
or what not, for a human tread, it seem as if still this bestrande 
remnant of mankind is in existence.” 

now, ingram, had told his story well; but sitting in this warm 
and civilise room, the horror which he had clearly felt had not 
communicate itself to me in any very vivid manner. intellectually, i 
agreed, i could appreciate his horror, but certainly my spirit felt no 
shudder of interior comprehension. 

“but it is odd,” i said, “that your keen interest in physiology did 
not disperse your qualms. you were looking, so i take it, at some form 
of man more remote probably than the earliest human remains. did not 
something inside you say ‘this is of absorbing significance’?” 

he shook his head. 

“no: i only want to get away,” said he. “it was not, as i have told 
you, the terror of what according to chanton’s story, might await us if 
we were captured; it was sheer horror at the creature itself. i quake 
at it.” 

* * * * * 

the snowstorm and the gale increase in violence that night, and i 
slept uneasily, pluck again and again from slumber by the fierce 
battle of the wind that shook my window as if with an imperious 
demand for admittance. it came in billowy gusts, with strange noise 
intermingle with it as for a moment it abated, with fluting and 
moan that rose to shriek as the fury of it returned. these noises, 
no doubt, mingle themselves with my drowse and sleepy consciousness, 
and once i tore myself out of nightmare, imagine that the creature 
of the horror-horn had gain footing on my balcony and were rattling 
at the window-bolts. but before morning the gale had die away, and i 
awoke to see the snow fall dense and fast in a windless air. for 
three days it continued, without intermission, and with its cessation 
there came a frost such as i have never felt before. fifty degree were 
register one night, and more the next, and what the cold must have 
been on the cliff of the ungeheuerhorn i cannot imagine. sufficient, 
so i thought, to have made an end altogether of its secret inhabitants: 
my cousin, on that day twenty years ago, had miss an opportunity for 
study which would probably never fall again either to him or another. 

i received one morning a letter from a friend saying that he had 
arrive at the neighbouring winter resort of st. luigi, and propose 
that i should come over for a morning’s skate and lunch afterwards. 
the place was not more than a couple of mile off, if one took the path 
over the low, pine-clad foot-hills above which lay the steep wood 
below the first rocky slope of the ungeheuerhorn; and accordingly, 
with a knapsack contain skate on my back, i went on ski over the 
wooded slope and down by an easy descent again on to st. luigi. the 
day was overcast, cloud entire obscure the higher peak though the 
sun was visible, pale and unluminous, through the mists. but as the 
morning went on, it gain the upper hand, and i slid down into st. 
luigi beneath a sparkle firmament. we skate and lunched, and then, 
since it look as if thick weather was coming up again, i set out 
early about three o’clock for my return journey. 

hardly had i got into the wood when the cloud gather thick above, 
and streamer and skein of them began to descend among the pine 
through which my path thread its way. in ten minutes more their 
opacity had so increase that i could hardly see a couple of yard in 
front of me. very soon i became aware that i must have got off the 
path, for snow-cowled shrub lay directly in my way, and, casting 
back to find it again, i got altogether confuse as to direction. 
but, though progress was difficult, i knew i had only to keep on 
the ascent, and presently i should come to the brow of these low 
foot-hills, and descend into the open valley where alhubel stood. so 
on i went, stumble and slide over obstacles, and unable, owe to 
the thickness of the snow, to take off my skis, for i should have sunk 
over the knee at each step. still the ascent continued, and look at 
my watch i saw that i had already been near an hour on my way from st. 
luigi, a period more than sufficient to complete my whole journey. but 
still i stuck to my idea that though i had certainly stray far from 
my proper route a few minutes more must surely see me over the top of 
the upward way, and i should find the ground decline into the next 
valley. about now, too, i notice that the mists were grow suffuse 
with rose-colour, and, though the inference was that it must be close 
on sunset, there was consolation in the fact that they were there and 
might lift at any moment and disclose to me my whereabouts. but the 
fact that night would soon be on me made it needful to bar my mind 
against that despair of lonely which so eats out the heart of a man 
who is lost in wood or on mountain-side, that, though still there is 
plenty of vigour in his limbs, his nervous force is sapped, and he can 
do no more than lie down and abandon himself to whatever fate may await 
him.... and then i heard that which made the thought of lonely seem 
bliss indeed, for there was a worse fate than loneliness. what i heard 
resemble the howl of a wolf, and it came from not far in front of me 
where the ridge--was it a ridge?--still rose higher in vestment of 
pines. 

from behind me came a sudden puff of wind, which shook the frozen snow 
from the droop pine-branches, and swept away the mists as a broom 
sweep the dust from the floor. radiant above me were the unclouded 
skies, already charged with the red of the sunset, and in front i 
saw that i had come to the very edge of the wood through which i had 
wander so long. but it was no valley into which i had penetrated, 
for there right ahead of me rose the steep slope of boulder and rocks 
soar upwards to the foot of the ungeheuerhorn. what, then, was that 
cry of a wolf which had made my heart stand still? i saw. 

not twenty yard from me was a fallen tree, and leaning against the 
trunk of it was one of the denizen of the horror-horn, and it was a 
woman. she was envelop in a thick growth of hair grey and tufted, 
and from her head it stream down over her shoulder and her bosom, 
from which hung wither and pendulous breasts. and look on her 
face i comprehend not with my mind alone, but with a shudder of my 
spirit, what ingram had felt. never had nightmare fashion so terrible 
a countenance; the beauty of sun and stars and of the beast of the 
field and the kindly race of men could not atone for so hellish an 
incarnation of the spirit of life. a fathomless bestiality model the 
slaver mouth and the narrow eyes; i look into the abyss itself 
and knew that out of that abyss on the edge of which i lean the 
generation of men had climbed. what if that ledge crumble in front of 
me and pitch me headlong into its nethermost depths?... 

in one hand she held by the horn a chamois that kick and struggled. 
a blow from its hindleg caught her wither thigh, and with a grunt 
of anger she seize the leg in her other hand, and, as a man may pull 
from its sheath a stem of meadow-grass, she pluck it off the body, 
leave the torn skin hanging round the gape wound. then putt the 
red, bleeding member to her mouth she suck at it as a child suck a 
stick of sweetmeat. through flesh and gristle her short, brown teeth 
penetrated, and she lick her lip with a sound of purring. then 
drop the leg by her side, she look again at the body of the prey 
now quiver in its death-convulsion, and with finger and thumb gouge 
out one of its eyes. she snap her teeth on it, and it cracked like a 
soft-shelled nut. 

it must have been but a few seconds that i stood watch her, in 
some indescribable catalepsy of terror, while through my brain there 
peal the panic-command of my mind to my stricken limb “begone, 
begone, while there is time.” then, recover the power of my joint 
and muscles, i try to slip behind a tree and hide myself from this 
apparition. but the woman--shall i say?--must have caught my stir of 
movement, for she raise her eyes from her living feast and saw me. she 
crane forward her neck, she drop her prey, and half rising began to 
move towards me. as she did this, she open her mouth, and gave forth 
a howl such as i had heard a moment before. it was answer by another, 
but faint and distantly. 

slide and slipping, with the toe of my ski tripping in the 
obstacle below the snow, i plunge forward down the hill between 
the pine-trunks. the low sun already sink behind some rampart of 
mountain in the west redden the snow and the pine with its ultimate 
rays. my knapsack with the skate in it swung to and fro on my back, 
one ski-stick had already been twitch out of my hand by a fallen 
branch of pine, but not a second’s pause could i allow myself to 
recover it. i gave no glance behind, and i knew not at what pace my 
pursuer was on my track, or indeed whether any pursue at all, for my 
whole mind and energy, now working at full power again under the stress 
of my panic, was devoted to get away down the hill and out of the 
wood as swift as my limb could bear me. for a little while i heard 
nothing but the hiss snow of my headlong passage, and the rustle of 
the cover undergrowth beneath my feet, and then, from close at hand 
behind me, once more the wolf-howl sound and i heard the plunge of 
footstep other than my own. 

the strap of my knapsack had shifted, and as my skate swung to and fro 
on my back it chafe and pressed on my throat, hinder free passage 
of air, of which, god knew, my labour lung were in dire need, and 
without pause i slip it free from my neck, and held it in the hand 
from which my ski-stick had been jerked. i seem to go a little more 
easily for this adjustment, and now, not so far distant, i could see 
below me the path from which i had strayed. if only i could reach that, 
the smooth go would surely enable me to out-distance my pursuer, 
who even on the rough ground was but slow overhaul me, and at 
the sight of that riband stretch unimpeded downhill, a ray of hope 
pierce the black panic of my soul. with that came the desire, keen 
and insistent, to see who or what it was that was on my tracks, and 
i spare a backward glance. it was she, the hag whom i had seen at 
her gruesome meal; her long grey hair flew out behind her, her mouth 
chatter and gibbered, her finger made grab movements, as if 
already they closed on me. 

but the path was now at hand, and the near of it i suppose made me 
incautious. a hump of snow-covered bush lay in my path, and, thinking 
i could jump over it, i trip and fell, smother myself in snow. i 
heard a maniac noise, half scream, half laugh, from close behind, and 
before i could recover myself the grab finger were at my neck, as 
if a steel vice had closed there. but my right hand in which i held my 
knapsack of skate was free, and with a blind back-handed movement i 
whirl it behind me at the full length of its strap, and knew that my 
desperate blow had found its billet somewhere. even before i could look 
round i felt the grip on my neck relax, and something subside into the 
very bush which had entangle me. i recover my feet and turned. 

there she lay, twitch and quivering. the heel of one of my skate 
piercing the thin alpaca of the knapsack had hit her full on the 
temple, from which the blood was pouring, but a hundred yard away i 
could see another such figure coming downwards on my tracks, leap 
and bounding. at that panic rose again within me, and i sped off down 
the white smooth path that led to the lights of the village already 
beckoning. never once did i pause in my headlong going: there was no 
safety until i was back among the haunt of men. i flung myself against 
the door of the hotel, and scream for admittance, though i had but to 
turn the handle and enter; and once more as when ingram had told his 
tale, there was the sound of the band, and the chatter of voices, and 
there, too, was he himself, who look up and then rose swift to his 
feet as i made my clatter entrance. 

“i have seen them too,” i cried. “look at my knapsack. is there not 
blood on it? it is the blood of one of them, a woman, a hag, who tore 
off the leg of a chamois as i looked, and pursue me through the 
accursed wood. i----” 

whether it was i who spun round, or the room which seem to spin 
round me, i knew not, but i heard myself falling, collapse on the 
floor, and the next time that i was conscious at all i was in bed. 
there was ingram there, who told me that i was quite safe, and another 
man, a stranger, who prick my arm with the nozzle of a syringe, and 
reassure me.... 

a day or two later i gave a coherent account of my adventure, and three 
or four men, armed with guns, went over my traces. they found the bush 
in which i had stumbled, with a pool of blood which had soaked into 
the snow, and, still following my ski-tracks, they came on the body 
of a chamois, from which had been torn one of its hindleg and one 
eye-socket was empty. that is all the corroboration of my story that i 
can give the reader, and for myself i imagine that the creature which 
pursue me was either not kill by my blow or that her fellow remove 
her body.... anyhow, it is open to the incredulous to prowl about 
the cave of the ungeheuerhorn, and see if anything occur that may 
convince them. 




machaon 




machaon 


i was return at the close of the short winter day from my visit to 
st. james’s hospital, where my old servant parkes, who had been in my 
service for twenty years, was lying. i had sent him there three days 
before, not for treatment, but for observation, and this afternoon i 
had gone up to london, to hear the doctor’s report on the case. he told 
me that park was suffering from an internal tumour, the nature of 
which could not be diagnose for certain, but all the symptom pointed 
directly to its being cancerous. that, however, must not be regard 
as proved; it could only be prove by an exploratory operation to 
reveal the nature and the extent of the growth, which must then, if 
possible, be excised. it might involve, so my old friend godfrey syme 
told me, certain tissue and would be found to be inoperable, but he 
hope this would not be the case, and that it would be possible to 
remove it: removal gave the only chance of recovery. it was fortunate 
that the patient had been sent for examination in an early stage, for 
thus the chance of success were much greater than if the growth had 
been one of long standing. park was not, however, in a fit state to 
stand the operation at once; a recuperative week or ten days in bed was 
advisable. in these circumstances syme recommend that he should not 
be told at once what lay in front of him. 

“i can see that he is a nervous fellow,” he said, “and to lie in bed 
thinking of what he has got to face will probably undo all the good 
that lie in bed will bring to him. you don’t get use to the idea of 
being cut open; the more you think about it, the more intolerable it 
becomes. if that sort of adventure face me, i should infinite prefer 
not to be told about it until they came to give me the anæsthetic. 
naturally, he will have to consent to the operation, but i shouldn’t 
tell him anything about it till the day before. he’s not married, i 
think, is he?” 

“no: he’s alone in the world,” said i. “he’s been with me twenty years.” 

“yes, i remember park almost as long as i remember you. but that’s 
all i can recommend. of course, if the pain became severe, it might be 
better to operate sooner, but at present he suffer hardly at all, and 
he sleep well, so the nurse tell me.” 

“and there’s nothing else that you can try for it?” i asked. 

“i’ll try anything you like, but it will be perfectly useless. i’ll 
let him have any quack nostrum you and he wish, as long as it doesn’t 
injure his health, or make you put off the operation. there are x-rays 
and ultra-violet rays, and violet leaves and radium; there are fresh 
cure for cancer discover every day, and what’s the result? they 
only make people put off the operation till it’s no long possible to 
operate. naturally, i will welcome any further opinion you want.” 

now godfrey syme is easily the first authority on this subject, and 
has a far higher percentage of cure to his credit than anyone else. 

“no, i don’t want any fresh opinion,” said i. 

“very well, i’ll have him careful watched. by the way, can’t you stop 
in town and dine with me? there are one or two people coming, and among 
them a perfectly mad spiritualist who has more message from the other 
world than i ever get on my telephone. trunk-calls, eh? i wonder where 
the exchange is. do come! you like cranks, i know!” 

“i can’t, i’m afraid,” said i. “i’ve a couple of guest coming to stay 
with me to-day down in the country. they are both cranks: one’s a 
medium.” 

he laughed. 

“well, i can only offer you one crank, and you’ve got two,” he said. “i 
must get back to the wards. i’ll write to you in about a week’s time 
or so, unless there’s any urgent which i don’t foresee, and i should 
suggest your coming up to tell parkes. good-bye.” 

i caught my train at char cross with about three seconds to spare, 
and we slid clank out over the bridge through the cold, dense air. 
snow had been fall intermittent since morning, and when we got 
out of the grime and fog of london, it was lie thick on field 
and hedgerow, retard by its reflection of such light as linger 
the oncoming of darkness, and give to the landscape an aloof and 
lonely austerity. all day i had felt that drowsy which accompany 
snowfall, and sometimes, half lose myself in a doze, my mind crept, 
like a thing crawl about in the dark, over what godfrey syme had 
told me. for all these years parkes, as much friend as servant, had 
given me his faithful and devotion, and now, in return for that, 
all that apparently i could do was to tell him of his plight. it was 
clear, from what the surgeon had said, that he expect a serious 
disclosure, and i knew from the experience of two friends of mine who 
had been in his condition what might be expect of this “exploratory 
operation.” exactly similar had been these cases; there was clear 
evidence of an internal growth possibly not malignant, and in each case 
the same dismal sequence had followed. the growth had been removed, and 
within a couple of month there had been a recrudescence of it. indeed, 
surgery had prove no more than a pruning-knife, which had stimulate 
that which the surgeon had hope to extirpate into swift activity. 
and that apparently was the best chance that syme held out: the rest 
of the treatment were but rubbish or quackery.... 

my mind crawl away towards another subject: probably the two visitor 
whom i expected, charles hope and the medium whom he was bring with 
him, were in the same train as i, and i ran over in my mind all that 
he had told me of mrs. forrest. it was certainly an odd story he had 
brought me two days before. mrs. forrest was a medium of considerable 
reputation in psychic circles, and had produce some very 
extraordinary book-tests which, by all accounts, seem inexplicable, 
except on a spiritualistic hypothesis, and no imputation of trickery 
had, at any rate as yet, come near her. when in trance, she spoke and 
wrote, as is invariable the case with mediums, under the direction 
of a certain “control”--that is to say, a spiritual and discarnate 
intelligence which for the time was in possession of her. but lately 
there had been sign that a fresh control had inspired her, the nature 
of whom, his name, and his identity was at present unknown. and then 
came the following queer incident. 

last week only when in trance, and apparently under the direction 
of this new control, she began describe in considerable detail a 
certain house where the control said that he had work to do. at first 
the description arouse no association in charles hope’s mind, but as 
it went on, it sudden struck him that mrs. forrest was speak of 
my house in tilling. she gave its general features, its position in a 
small town on a hill, its walled-in garden, and then went on to speak 
with great minute of a rather peculiar feature in the house. she 
describe a big room built out in the garden a few yard away from 
the house itself at right-angles to its front, and approach by half 
a dozen stone steps. there was a railing, so she said, on each side 
of them, and into the railing were twisted, like snake coils, the 
stem of a tree which bore pale mauve flowers. this was all a correct 
description of my garden room and the wistaria which writhe in and out 
of the railing which line the steps. she then went on to speak of the 
interior of the room. at one end was a fireplace, at the other a big 
bow-window look out on to the street and the front of the house, and 
there were two other window opposite each other, in one of which was a 
table, while the other, look out on to the garden, was shadow by 
the tree that twist itself about the railings. book-cases line the 
walls, and there was a big sofa at right-angles to the fire.... 

now all this, though it was a perfectly accurate description of a place 
that, as far as could be ascertained, mrs. forrest had never seen, 
might conceivable have been derive from charles hope’s mind, since he 
knew the room well, have often stay with me. but the medium add a 
detail which could not conceivable have been thus derived, for charles 
believe it to be incorrect. she said that there was a big piano near 
the bow-window, while he was sure that there was not. but oddly enough 
i had hire a piano only a week or so ago, and it stood in the place 
that she mentioned. the “control” then repeated that there was work 
for him to do in that house. there was some situation or complication 
there in which he could help, and he could “get through” better (that 
is, make a clear communication) if the medium could hold a séance 
there. charles hope then told the control that he believe he knew 
the house that he had been speak of, and promise to do his best. 
shortly afterwards mrs. forrest came out of trance, and, as usual, had 
no recollection of what had passed. 

so charles came to me with the story exactly as i have given it here, 
and though i could not think of any situation or complication in which 
an unknown control of a medium i had never seen could be of assistance, 
the whole thing (and in especial that detail about the piano) was so 
odd that i ask him to bring the medium down for a sitting or a series 
of sittings. the day of their arrival was arranged, but when three days 
ago park had to go into hospital, i was inclined to put them off. but 
a neighbour away for a week obliging lent me a parlour-maid, and i 
let the engagement stand. with regard to the situation in which the 
control would be of assistance, i can but assure the reader that as far 
as i thought about it at all, i only wonder whether it was concerned 
with a book on which i was engaged, which dealt (if i could ever 
succeed in writing it) with psychic affairs. but at present i could 
not get on with it at all. i had made half a dozen beginning which had 
all gone into the waste-paper basket. 

my guest prove not to have come by the same train as i, but arrive 
shortly before dinner-time, and after mrs. forrest had gone to her 
room, i had a few words with charles, who told me exactly how the 
situation now stood. 

“i know your caution and your captious in these affairs,” he said, 
“so i have told mrs. forrest nothing about the description she gave 
of this house, or of the reason why i ask her to come here. i said 
only, as we settled, that you were a great friend of mine and immensely 
interested in psychic affairs, but a country-mouse whom it was 
difficult to get up to town. but you would be delight if she would 
come down for a few days and give some sitting here.” 

“and do she recognise the house, do you think?” i asked. 

“no sign of it. as i told you, when she come out of trance she never 
seem to have the faintest recollection of what she has said or 
written. we shall have a séance, i hope, to-night after dinner.” 

“certainly, if she will,” said i. “i thought we had better hold it in 
the garden-room, for that was the place that was so minutely described. 
it’s quite warm there, central-heating and a fire, and it’s only half a 
dozen yard from the house. i’ve had the snow swept from the steps.” 

mrs. forrest turn out to be a very intelligent woman, well spice 
with humour, gifted with a sane appreciation of the comfort of life, 
and most agreeably furnish with the small change of talk. she 
was inclined to be stout, but carry herself with briskness, and 
neither in body nor mind did she suggest that she was one who held 
communication with the unseen: there was nothing wan or occult about 
her. her general outlook on life appear to be rather materialist 
than otherwise, and she was very interesting on the topic when, about 
half-way through dinner, the subject of her mediumship came on the 
conversational board. 

“my gifts, such as they are,” she said, “have nothing to do with this 
person who sit eat and drink and talk to you. she, as mr. 
hope may have told you, is quite expunge before the subconscious part 
of me--that is the latest notion, is it not?--gets into touch with 
discarnate intelligences. until that happens, the door is shut, and 
when it is over, the door is shut again, and i have no recollection of 
what i have said or written. the control use my hand and my voice, but 
that is all. i know no more about it than a piano on which a tune has 
been played.” 

“and there is a new control who has lately been use you?” i asked. 

she laughed. 

“you must ask mr. hope about that,” she said. “i know nothing 
whatever of it. he tell me it is so, and he tell me--don’t you, mr. 
hope?--that he hasn’t any idea who or what the new control is. i look 
forward to its development; my idea is that the control has to get use 
to me, as in learning a new instrument. i assure you i am as eager as 
anyone that he should gain facility in communication through me. i 
hope, indeed, that we are to have a séance to-night.” 

the talk veer again, and i learned that mrs. forrest had never been 
in till before, and was enchant with the snowy moonlit glance she 
had had of its narrow street and ancient residences. she liked, too, 
the atmosphere of the house: it seem tranquil and kindly; especially 
so was the little drawing-room where we had assemble before dinner. 

i glance at charles. 

“i had thought of propose that we should sit in the garden-room,” i 
said, “if you don’t mind half a dozen steps in the open. it adjoin the 
house.” 

“just as you wish,” she said, “though i think we have excellent 
conditions in here without go there.” 

this confirmed her statement that she had no idea after she had come 
out of trance what she had said, for otherwise she must have recognise 
at the mention of the garden-room her own description of it, and when 
soon after dinner we adjourn there, it was clear that, unless she 
was acting an inexplicable part, the sight of it twang no chord of 
memory. there we made the very simple arrangement to which she was 
accustomed. 

as the procedure in such sitting is possibly unfamiliar to the reader, 
i will describe quite shortly what our arrangement were. we had no 
idea what form these manifestations--if there were any--might take, 
and therefore we, charles and i, were prepared to record them on the 
spot. we three sat round a small table about a couple of yard from 
the fire, which was burning brightly; mrs. forrest seat herself in 
a big armchair. exactly in front of her on the table were a pencil 
and a block of paper in case, as often happened, the manifestation 
took the form of automatic script--writing, that is, while in a state 
of trance. charles and i sat on each side of her, also provided with 
pencil and paper in order to take down what she said if and when (as 
lawyer say) the control took possession of her. in case materialise 
spirits appeared, a phenomenon not as yet seen at her séances, our idea 
was to jot down as quick as possible whatever we saw or thought we 
saw. should there be rap or movements of furniture, we were to 
make similar note of our impressions. the lamp was then turn down, 
so that just a ring of flame encircle the wick, but the firelight was 
of sufficient brightness, as we test before the séance began, to 
enable us to write and to see what we had written. the red glow of it 
illuminate the room, and it was settled that charles should note by 
his watch the time at which anything occurred. occasionally, throughout 
the séance a bubble of coal-gas caught fire, and then the whole room 
start into strong light. i had given orders that my servant should 
not interrupt the sitting at all, unless somebody rang the bell from 
the garden-room. in that case it was to be answered. finally, before 
the séance began, we bolt all the window on the inside and lock 
the door. we took no other precaution against trickery, though, as a 
matter of fact, mrs. forrest suggest that she should be tie into her 
chair. but in the firelight any movement of hers would be so visible 
that we did not adopt this precaution. charles and i had settled to 
read to each other the note we made during the sitting, and cut out 
anything that both of us had not recorded. the accounts, therefore, of 
this sitting and of that which follow next day are found on our 
joint evidence. the sitting began. 

mrs. forrest was leaning back at ease with her eyes open and her hand 
on the arms of her chair. then her eyes closed and a violent tremble 
seize her. that passed, and shortly afterwards her head fell forward 
and her breathing became very rapid. presently that quiet to normal 
pace again, and she began to speak at first in a scarcely audible 
whisper and then in a high shrill voice, quite unlike her usual tones. 

i do not think that in all england there was a more disappointed man 
than i during the next half-hour. “starlight,” it appeared, was in 
control, and starlight was a personage of platitudes. she had been a 
nun in the time of henry vii, and her work was to help those who had 
lately pass over. she was very busy and very happy, and was in the 
third sphere where they had a great deal of beautiful music. we must 
all be good, said starlight, and it didn’t matter much whether we were 
clever or not. love was the great thing; we had to love each other 
and help each other, and death was no more than the gate of life, and 
everything would be tremendous jolly.... starlight, in fact, might be 
better describe as clap-trap, and i began thinking about parkes.... 

and then i cease to think about parkes, for the shrill morality 
of starlight ceased, and mrs. forrest’s voice change again. the 
stale facility of her utterance stop and she began to speak, quite 
unintelligibly, in a voice of low baritone range. charles lean across 
the table and whisper to me. 

“that’s the new control,” he said. 

the voice that was speak stumble and hesitated: it was like that 
of a man try to express himself in some language which he knew very 
imperfectly. sometimes it stop altogether, and in one of these 
pause i asked: 

“can you tell us your name?” 

there was no reply, but presently i saw mrs. forrest’s hand reach 
out for the pencil. charles put it into her finger and placed the 
writing-pad more handy for her. i watch the letters, in capitals, 
being traced. they were made hesitatingly, but were perfectly legible. 
“swallow,” she wrote, and again “swallow,” and stopped. 

“the bird?” i asked. 

the voice spoke in answer; now i could hear the words, utter in that 
low baritone voice. 

“no, not a bird,” it said. “not a bird, but it flies.” 

i was utterly at sea; my mind could form no conjecture whatever 
as to what was meant. and then the pencil began writing again. 
“swallow, swallow,” and then with a sudden brisk of movement, as 
if the guide intelligence had got over some difficulty, it wrote 
“swallow-tail.” 

this seem more abstruse senseless than ever. the only connection 
with swallow-tail in my mind was a swallow-tailed coat, but whoever 
heard of a swallow-tailed coat flying? 

“i’ve got it,” said charles. “swallow-tail butterfly. is it that?” 

there came three sudden rap on the table, loud and startling. these 
raps, i may explain, in the usual code mean “yes.” as if to confirm 
it the pencil began to write again, and spell out “swallow-tail 
butterfly.” 

“is that your name?” i asked. 

there was one rap, which signify “no,” follow by three, which 
means “yes.” i had not the slightest idea of what it all signify 
(indeed it seem to signify nothing at all), but the sitting had 
become extraordinarily interesting if only for its very unexpectedness. 
the control was try to establish himself by three method 
simultaneously--by the voice, by the automatic writing, and by rapping. 
but how a swallow-tail butterfly could assist in some situation which 
was now existing in my house was utterly beyond me.... then an idea 
struck me: the swallow-tail butterfly no doubt had a scientific name, 
and that we could easily ascertain, for i knew that there was on my 
shelves a copy of newman’s _butterflies and moth of great britain_, a 
sumptuous volume bound in morocco, which i had won as an entomology 
prize at school. a moment’s search gave me the book, and by the 
firelight i turn up the description of this butterfly in the index. 
its scientific name was _papilio machaon_. 

“is machaon your name?” i asked. 

the voice came clear now. 

“yes, i am machaon,” it said. 

with that came the end of the séance, which had last not more than 
an hour. whatever the power was that had made mrs. forrest speak 
in that male voice and struggle, through that roundabout method of 
“swallow, swallow-tail, machaon,” to establish its identity, it now 
began to fail. mrs. forrest’s pencil made a few illegible scribbles, 
she whisper a few inaudible words, and presently with a stretch and a 
sigh she came out of trance. we told her that the name of the control 
was established, but apparently machaon meant nothing to her. she was 
much exhausted, and very soon i took her across to the house to go to 
bed, and presently rejoin charles. 

“who was machaon, anyhow?” he asked. “he sound classical: more in your 
line than mine.” 

i remember enough greece mythology to supply elementary facts, while i 
hunt for a particular book about athens. 

“machaon was the son of asclepios,” i said, “and asclepio was the 
greece god of healing. he had precincts, hydropathic establishments, 
where people went to be cured. the roman call him aesculapius.” 

“what can he do for you then?” ask charles. “you’re fairly fit, 
aren’t you?” 

not till he spoke did a light dawn on me. though i had been thinking so 
much of park that day, i had not conscious made the connection. 

“but park isn’t,” said i. “is that possible?” 

“by jove!” said he. 

i found my book, and turn to the account of the precinct of 
asclepio in athens. 

“yes, asclepio had two sons,” i said--“machaon and podaleirios. in 
homeric times he wasn’t a god, but only a physician, and his son were 
physician too. the myth of his godhead is rather a late one----” 

i shut the book. 

“best not to read any more,” i said. “if we know all about asclepios, 
we shall possibly be suggest things to the medium’s mind. let’s 
see what machaon can tell us about himself, and we can verify it 
afterwards.” 

it was therefore with no further knowledge than this on the subject 
of machaon that we propose to hold another séance the next day. all 
morning the bitter air had been laden with snow, and now the street 
in front of my house, a by-way at the best in the slender traffic of 
the town, lay white and untrodden, save on the pavement where a few 
passenger had gone by. mrs. forrest had not appear at breakfast, and 
from then till lunch-time i sat in the bow-window of the garden-room, 
for the warmth of the central heating, of which a stack of pipes was 
there installed, and for secure the utmost benefit of light that 
penetrate this cowl of snow-laden sky, busy with belated letters. the 
drowsy that accompany snowfall weigh heavy on my faculties, 
but as far as i can assert anything, i can assert that i did not 
sleep. from one letter i went on to another, and then for the sixth 
or seventh time i try to open my story. it promise better now 
than before, and searching for a word that would not come to my pen, 
i happen to look up along the street which lay in front of me. i 
expect nothing: i was thinking of nothing but my work; probably i 
had look up like that a dozen times before, and had seen the empty 
street, with snow lie thick on the roadway. 

but now the roadway was not untenanted. someone was walking down the 
middle of it, and his aspect, incredible though it seemed, was not 
startling. why i was not startle i have no idea: i can only say that 
the vision appear perfectly natural. the figure was that of a young 
man, whose hair, black and curly, lay crisp over his forehead. a 
large white cloak reach down to his knee envelop him, and he 
had thrown the end of it over his shoulder. below his knee his leg 
and feet were bare, so too was the arm up to the elbow, with which he 
pressed his cloak to him, and there he was walking brisk down the 
snowy street. as he came directly below the window where i sat, he 
raise his head and look at me directly, and smiled. and now i saw 
his face: there was the low brow, the straight nose, the curve and 
sunny mouth, the short chin, and i thought to myself that this was none 
other than the herme of praxiteles, he whose statue at olympia make 
all those who look on it grow young again. there, anyhow, was a boyish 
greece god, step blithe and with gay, incomparable grace along 
the street, and raise his face to smile at this stolid, middle-aged 
man who blank regard him. then with the certainty of one return 
home, he mount the steps outside the front door, and seem to pass 
into and through it. certainly he was no long in the street, and, so 
real and solid-seeming had he been to my vision, that i jump up, ran 
across the few steps of garden, and went into the house, and i should 
not have been amaze if i had found him standing in the hall. but there 
was no one there, and i open the front door: the snow lay smooth 
and untrodden down the centre of the road where he had walk and on 
my doorstep. and at that moment the memory of the séance the evening 
before, about which up till now i had somehow felt distrustful and 
suspicious, pass into the realm of sober fact, for had not machaon 
just now enter my house, with a smile as of recognition on some 
friendly mission? 

we sat again that afternoon by daylight, and now, i must suppose, the 
control was more active and powerful present, for hardly had mrs. 
forrest pass into trance than the voice began, loud than it had 
been the night before, and far more distinct. he--machaon i must call 
him--seemed to be anxious to establish his identity beyond all doubt, 
like some newcomer present his credentials, and he began to speak of 
the precinct of asclepio in athens. often he hesitate for a word in 
english, often he put in a word in greek, and as he spoke, fragment of 
things i had learned when an archæological student in athens came back 
into my mind, and i knew that he was accurate describe the portico 
and the temple and the well. all this i toss to the sceptic to growl 
and worry over and tear to bits; for certainly it seem possible that 
my mind, holding these fact in its subconsciousness, was suggest 
them to the medium’s mind, who thereupon spoke of them and, convey 
them back to me, made me aware that i had known them.... my forgotten 
knowledge of these things and of the greece language came flood back 
on me, as he told us, now half in greek, and half in english, of the 
patient who came to consult the god, how they wash in the sacred 
well for purification, and lay down to sleep in the portico. they often 
dreamed, and in the interpretation of their dreams, which they told to 
the priest next day, lay the indication of the cure. or sometimes the 
god heal more directly, and accompany by the sacred snake walk 
among the sleeper and by his touch made them whole. his temple was 
hung with _ex-votos_, the gift of those whom he had cured. and at 
epidaurus, where was another shrine of his, there were great mural 
tablet recording the same.... 

then the voice stopped, and as if to prove identity by another means, 
the medium drew the pencil and paper to her, and in greece characters, 
unknown apparently to her, she trace the words “machaon, son of 
asclepios....” 

there was a pause, and i ask a direct question, which now had been 
long simmer in my mind. 

“have you come to help me about parkes?” i asked. “can you tell me what 
will cure him?” 

the pencil began to move again, tracing out character in greek. it 
wrote [greek: phengo x], and repeated it. i did not at once guess 
what it meant, and ask for an explanation. there was no answer, and 
presently the medium stirred, stretch herself and sighed, and came 
out of trance. she took up the paper on which she had written. 

“did that come through?” she asked. “and what do it mean? i don’t 
even know the characters....” 

then sudden the possible significance of [greek: phengo x] flash 
on me, and i marvel at my slowness. [greek: phengos], a beam of 
light, a ray, and the letter [greek: x], the equivalent of the english 
_x_. that had come in direct answer to my question as to what would 
cure parkes, and it was without hesitation or delay that i wrote to 
symes. i remind him that he had said that he had no objection to 
any possible remedy, provided it was not harmful, being try on his 
patient, and i ask him to treat him with x-rays. the whole sequence 
of event had been so frankly amazing, that i believe the veriest 
sceptic would not have done otherwise than i did. 

our sitting continued, but after this day we had no further evidence 
of this second control. it look as if the intelligence (even the 
most incredulous will allow me, for the sake of convenience, to call 
that intelligence machaon) that had describe this room, and told mrs. 
forrest that he had work to do here, had finished his task. machaon had 
said, or so my interpretation was, that x-rays would cure parkes. in 
justification of this view it is proper to quote from a letter which i 
got from syme a week later. 

“there is no need for you to come up to break to park that an 
operation lie in front of him. in answer to your request, and 
without a grain of faith in its success, i treat him with x-rays, 
which i assured you were useless. to-day, to speak quite frankly, 
i don’t know what to think, for the growth has been steady 
diminish in size and hardness, and it is perfectly evident that it 
is being absorb and is disappearing. 

“the treatment through which i put park is that of ----. here in 
this hospital we have had patient to whom it brought no shadow of 
benefit. often it had been continue on these delude wretch till 
any operation which might possibly have been successful was out of 
the question owe to the encroachment of the growth. but from the 
first dose of the x-rays, park began to get better, the growth was 
first arrested, and then diminished. 

“i am try to put the whole thing before you with as much 
impartial as i can command. so, on the other side, you must 
remember that parkes’s was never a prove case of cancer. i told 
you that it could not be prove till the exploratory operation took 
place. all the symptom pointed to cancer--you see, i am try to 
save my own face--but my diagnosis, though confirmed by ----, may 
have been wrong. if he only had what we call a benign tumour, the 
case is not so extraordinary; there have been plenty of case when 
a benign tumour has disappear by absorption or what not. it is 
unusual, but by no means unknown. for instance.... 

“but parkes’s case was quite different. i certainly believe he had 
a cancerous growth, and thought that an operation was inevitable if 
his life was to be saved. even then, the most i hope for was an 
alleviate of pain, as the disease progressed, and a year or two 
more, at the most, of life. instead, i apply another remedy, at 
your suggestion, and if he go on as he has been doing, the growth 
will be a nodule in another week or two, and i should expect it to 
disappear altogether. taking everything into consideration, if you 
ask me the question whether this x-ray treatment was the cause of 
the cure, i should be oblige to say ‘yes.’ i don’t believe in such 
a treatment, but i believe it is cure him. i suppose that it was 
suggest to you by a fraudulent, spiritualistic medium in a feign 
trance, who was inspired by aesculapiu or some exploded heathen 
deity, for i remember you said you were go down into the country 
for some spiritual business.... 

“well, park is get better, and i am so old-fashioned a fellow 
that i would soon a patient of mine got better by incredible 
methods, than die under my skilful knife.... of course, we train 
people know nothing, but we have to act according to the best chance 
of our ignorance. i entire believe that the knife was the only 
means of saving the man, and now, when i stand confuted, the only 
thing that i can save is my honesty, which i hereby have done. let me 
know, at your leisure, whether you just thought you would, on your 
own idea, like me to try x-rays, or whether some fake voice from the 
grave suggest it. 
“ever yours, 
“godfrey symes. 

“p.s.--if it was some beastly voice from the grave, you might tell me 
in confidence who the medium was. i want to be fair....” 

that is the story; the reader will explain it according to his 
temperament. and as i have told parkes, who is now back with me again, 
to look into the garden-room before post-time and take a register 
packet to the office, it is time that i got it ready for him. so here 
is the complete packet in manuscript, to be sent to the printer’s. 
from my window i shall see him go brisk along the street down which 
machaon walk on a snowy morning. 




negotium perambulans.... 




negotium perambulans.... 


the casual tourist in west cornwall may just possibly have noticed, 
as he bowl along over the bare high plateau between penzance and 
the land’s end, a dilapidated signpost point down a steep lane 
and bearing on its batter finger the fade inscription “polearn 
2 miles,” but probably very few have had the curiosity to traverse 
those two mile in order to see a place to which their guide-books 
award so cursory a notice. it is describe there, in a couple of 
unattractive lines, as a small fishing village with a church of no 
particular interest except for certain carve and paint wooden panel 
(originally belong to an early edifice) which form an altar-rail. 
but the church at st. creed (the tourist is reminded) has a similar 
decoration far superior in point of preservation and interest, and thus 
even the ecclesiastical disposed are not lure to polearn. so meagre 
a bait is scarce worth swallowing, and a glance at the very steep lane 
which in dry weather presents a carpet of sharp-pointed stones, and 
after rain a muddy watercourse, will almost certainly decide him not 
to expose his motor or his bicycle to risk like these in so sparse 
populate a district. hardly a house has met his eye since he left 
penzance, and the possible trundle of a puncture bicycle for half 
a dozen weary mile seem a high price to pay for the sight of a few 
paint panels. 

polearn, therefore, even in the high noon of the tourist season, is 
little liable to invasion, and for the rest of the year i do not 
suppose that a couple of folk a day traverse those two mile (long one 
at that) of steep and stony gradient. i am not forget the postman 
in this exiguous estimate, for the days are few when, leave his 
pony and cart at the top of the hill, he go as far as the village, 
since but a few hundred yard down the lane there stand a large 
white box, like a sea-trunk, by the side of the road, with a slit for 
letters and a lock door. should he have in his wallet a register 
letter or be the bearer of a parcel too large for insertion in the 
square lip of the sea-trunk, he must needs trudge down the hill and 
deliver the troublesome missive, leave it in person on the owner, and 
receiving some small reward of coin or refreshment for his kindness. 
but such occasion are rare, and his general routine is to take out 
of the box such letters as may have been deposit there, and insert 
in their place such letters as he has brought. these will be call 
for, perhaps that day or perhaps the next, by an emissary from the 
polearn post-office. as for the fishermen of the place, who, in their 
export trade, constitute the chief link of movement between polearn and 
the outside world, they would not dream of taking their catch up the 
steep lane and so, with six mile farther of travel, to the market at 
penzance. the sea route is short and easier, and they deliver their 
wares to the pier-head. thus, though the sole industry of polearn is 
sea-fishing, you will get no fish there unless you have bespoken your 
requirement to one of the fishermen. back come the trawler as empty 
as a haunt house, while their spoils are in the fish-train that is 
speeding to london. 

such isolation of a little community, continued, as it has been, for 
centuries, produce isolation in the individual as well, and nowhere 
will you find greater independence of character than among the people 
of polearn. but they are link together, so it has always seem 
to me, by some mysterious comprehension: it is as if they had all 
been initiate into some ancient rite, inspired and frame by forces 
visible and invisible. the winter storm that batter the coast, the 
vernal spell of the spring, the hot, still summers, the season of rains 
and autumnal decay, have made a spell which, line by line, has been 
communicate to them, concerning the powers, evil and good, that rule 
the world, and manifest themselves in ways benignant or terrible.... 

i came to polearn first at the age of ten, a small boy, weak and 
sickly, and threaten with pulmonary trouble. my father’s business 
kept him in london, while for me abundance of fresh air and a mild 
climate were considered essential conditions if i was to grow to 
manhood. his sister had married the vicar of polearn, richard bolitho, 
himself native to the place, and so it came about that i spent three 
years, as a pay guest, with my relations. richard bolitho own 
a fine house in the place, which he inhabit in preference to the 
vicarage, which he let to a young artist, john evans, on whom the spell 
of polearn had fallen, for from year’s beginning to year’s end he never 
left it. there was a solid roof shelter, open on one side to the 
air, built for me in the garden, and here i live and slept, passing 
scarcely one hour out of the twenty-four behind wall and windows. 
i was out on the bay with the fisher-folk, or wandering along the 
gorse-clad cliff that climb steeple to right and left of the deep 
combe where the village lay, or potter about on the pier-head, or 
bird’s-nesting in the bush with the boys of the village. except on 
sunday and for the few daily hour of my lessons, i might do what i 
pleased so long as i remain in the open air. about the lesson there 
was nothing formidable; my uncle conduct me through flowering bypath 
among the thicket of arithmetic, and made pleasant excursion into the 
elements of latin grammar, and above all, he made me daily give him an 
account, in clear and grammatical sentences, of what had been occupy 
my mind or my movements. should i select to tell him about a walk along 
the cliffs, my speech must be orderly, not vague, slip-shod note of 
what i had observed. in this way, too, he train my observation, for 
he would bid me tell him what flower were in bloom, and what bird 
hover fishing over the sea or were building in the bushes. for that 
i owe him a perennial gratitude, for to observe and to express my 
thought in the clear spoken word became my life’s profession. 

but far more formidable than my weekday task was the prescribed 
routine for sunday. some dark ember compound of calvinism and 
mysticism smoulder in my uncle’s soul, and made it a day of terror. 
his sermon in the morning scorch us with a foretaste of the eternal 
fire reserved for unrepentant sinners, and he was hardly less 
terrify at the children’s service in the afternoon. well do i 
remember his exposition of the doctrine of guardian angels. a child, 
he said, might think himself secure in such angel care, but let him 
beware of commit any of those numerous offence which would cause 
his guardian to turn his face from him, for as sure as there were 
angel to protect us, there were also evil and awful presence which 
were ready to pounce; and on them he dwelt with peculiar gusto. well, 
too, do i remember in the morning sermon his commentary on the carve 
panel of the altar-rails to which i have already alluded. there was 
the angel of the annunciation there, and the angel of the resurrection, 
but not less was there the witch of endor, and, on the fourth panel, 
a scene that concerned me most of all. this fourth panel (he came 
down from his pulpit to trace its time-worn features) represent 
the lych-gate of the church-yard at polearn itself, and indeed the 
resemblance when thus pointed out was remarkable. in the entry stood 
the figure of a robe priest holding up a cross, with which he face 
a terrible creature like a gigantic slug, that rear itself up in 
front of him. that, so ran my uncle’s interpretation, was some evil 
agency, such as he had spoken about to us children, of almost infinite 
malignity and power, which could alone be combat by firm faith and a 
pure heart. below ran the legend “_negotium perambulan in tenebris_” 
from the ninety-first psalm. we should find it translate there, “the 
pestilence that walketh in darkness,” which but feeble render the 
latin. it was more deadly to the soul than any pestilence that can 
only kill the body: it was the thing, the creature, the business that 
trafficke in the outer darkness, a minister of god’s wrath on the 
unrighteous.... 

i could see, as he spoke, the looks which the congregation exchange 
with each other, and knew that his words were evoke a surmise, a 
remembrance. nod and whisper pass between them, they understood 
to what he alluded, and with the inquisitive of boyhood i could 
not rest till i had worm the story out of my friends among the 
fisher-boys, as, next morning, we sat bask and naked in the sun 
after our bathe. one knew one bit of it, one another, but it piece 
together into a truly alarm legend. in bald outline it was as 
follows: 

a church far more ancient than that in which my uncle terrify us 
every sunday had once stood not three hundred yard away, on the shelf 
of level ground below the quarry from which its stone were hewn. the 
owner of the land had pull this down, and erect for himself a house 
on the same site out of these materials, keeping, in a very ecstasy of 
wickedness, the altar, and on this he dine and play dice afterwards. 
but as he grew old some black melancholy seize him, and he would have 
lights burning there all night, for he had deadly fear of the darkness. 
on one winter evening there sprang up such a gale as was never before 
known, which broke in the window of the room where he had supped, and 
extinguish the lamps. yell of terror brought in his servants, who 
found him lie on the floor with the blood stream from his throat. 
as they enter some huge black shadow seem to move away from him, 
crawl across the floor and up the wall and out of the broken window. 

“there he lay a-dying,” said the last of my informants, “and him 
that had been a great burly man was wither to a bag o’ skin, for 
the critter had drain all the blood from him. his last breath was 
a scream, and he holler out the same words as parson read off the 
screen.” 

“_negotium perambulan in tenebris_,” i suggest eagerly. 

“thereabouts. latin anyhow.” 

“and after that?” i asked. 

“nobody would go near the place, and the old house rot and fell in 
ruins till three years ago, when along come mr. dooliss from penzance, 
and built the half of it up again. but he don’t care much about such 
critters, nor about latin neither. he take his bottle of whisky a 
day and get drunk’s a lord in the evening. eh, i’m gwine home to my 
dinner.” 

whatever the authenticity of the legend, i had certainly heard the 
truth about mr. dooliss from penzance, who from that day became 
an object of keen curiosity on my part, the more so because the 
quarry-house adjoin my uncle’s garden. the thing that walk in 
the dark fail to stir my imagination, and already i was so use to 
sleep alone in my shelter that the night had no terror for me. but 
it would be intense exciting to wake at some timeless hour and hear 
mr. dooliss yelling, and conjecture that the thing had got him. 

but by degree the whole story fade from my mind, overscore by 
the more vivid interests of the day, and, for the last two years of 
my out-door life in the vicarage garden, i seldom thought about mr. 
dooliss and the possible fate that might await him for his temerity in 
living in the place where that thing of dark had done business. 
occasional i saw him over the garden fence, a great yellow lump of 
a man, with slow and staggering gait, but never did i set eyes on him 
outside his gate, either in the village street or down on the beach. 
he interfere with none, and no one interfere with him. if he want 
to run the risk of being the prey of the legendary nocturnal monster, 
or quiet drink himself to death, it was his affair. my uncle, so i 
gathered, had made several attempt to see him when first he came to 
live at polearn, but mr. dooliss appear to have no use for parsons, 
but said he was not at home and never return the call. 

* * * * * 

after three years of sun, wind, and rain, i had completely outgrown 
my early symptom and had become a tough, strapping youngster of 
thirteen. i was sent to eton and cambridge, and in due course ate my 
dinner and became a barrister. in twenty years from that time i was 
earn a yearly income of five figures, and had already laid by in 
sound security a sum that brought me dividend which would, for 
one of my simple taste and frugal habits, supply me with all the 
material comfort i need on this side of the grave. the great prize 
of my profession were already within my reach, but i had no ambition 
beckon me on, nor did i want a wife and children, being, i must 
suppose, a natural celibate. in fact there was only one ambition which 
through these busy years had held the lure of blue and far-off hill to 
me, and that was to get back to polearn, and live once more isolated 
from the world with the sea and the gorse-clad hill for play-fellows, 
and the secret that lurk there for exploration. the spell of it had 
been woven about my heart, and i can truly say that there had hardly 
pass a day in all those years in which the thought of it and the 
desire for it had been wholly absent from my mind. though i had been in 
frequent communication with my uncle there during his lifetime, and, 
after his death, with his widow who still live there, i had never 
been back to it since i embark on my profession, for i knew that if 
i went there, it would be a wrench beyond my power to tear myself away 
again. but i had made up my mind that when once i had provided for my 
own independence, i would go back there not to leave it again. and yet 
i did leave it again, and now nothing in the world would induce me to 
turn down the lane from the road that leads from penzance to the land’s 
end, and see the side of the combe rise steep above the roof of the 
village and hear the gull chide as they fish in the bay. one of the 
things invisible, of the dark powers, leap into light, and i saw it 
with my eyes. 

the house where i had spent those three years of boyhood had been left 
for life to my aunt, and when i made known to her my intention of 
coming back to polearn, she suggest that, till i found a suitable 
house or found her proposal unsuitable, i should come to live with her. 

“the house is too big for a lone old woman,” she wrote, “and i have 
often thought of quit and taking a little cottage sufficient for me 
and my requirements. but come and share it, my dear, and if you find 
me troublesome, you or i can go. you may want solitude--most people in 
polearn do--and will leave me. or else i will leave you: one of the 
main reason of my stopping here all these years was a feeling that i 
must not let the old house starve. house starve, you know, if they 
are not live in. they die a lingering death; the spirit in them grow 
weak and weaker, and at last fade out of them. isn’t this nonsense 
to your london notions?...” 

naturally i accept with warmth this tentative arrangement, and on an 
evening in june found myself at the head of the lane leading down to 
polearn, and once more i descended into the steep valley between the 
hills. time had stood still apparently for the combe, the dilapidated 
signpost (or its successor) pointed a rickety finger down the lane, 
and a few hundred yard farther on was the white box for the exchange 
of letters. point after remember point met my eye, and what i saw 
was not shrunk, as is often the case with the revisite scene of 
childhood, into a small scale. there stood the post-office, and there 
the church and close beside it the vicarage, and beyond, the tall 
shrubbery which separate the house for which i was bound from the 
road, and beyond that again the grey roof of the quarry-house damp and 
shining with the moist evening wind from the sea. all was exactly as i 
remember it, and, above all, that sense of seclusion and isolation. 
somewhere above the tree-tops climb the lane which join the main 
road to penzance, but all that had become immeasurable distant. the 
years that had pass since last i turn in at the well-known gate 
fade like a frosty breath, and vanish in this warm, soft air. there 
were law-courts somewhere in memory’s dull book which, if i care to 
turn the pages, would tell me that i had made a name and a great income 
there. but the dull book was closed now, for i was back in polearn, and 
the spell was woven around me again. 

and if polearn was unchanged, so too was aunt hester, who met me at the 
door. dainty and china-white she had always been, and the years had 
not age but only refined her. as we sat and talk after dinner she 
spoke of all that had happen in polearn in that score of years, and 
yet somehow the change of which she spoke seem but to confirm the 
immutable of it all. as the recollection of name came back to me, i 
ask her about the quarry-house and mr. dooliss, and her face gloom 
a little as with the shadow of a cloud on a spring day. 

“yes, mr. dooliss,” she said, “poor mr. dooliss, how well i remember 
him, though it must be ten years and more since he died. i never wrote 
to you about it, for it was all very dreadful, my dear, and i did not 
want to darken your memory of polearn. your uncle always thought that 
something of the sort might happen if he went on in his wicked, drunken 
ways, and worse than that, and though nobody knew exactly what took 
place, it was the sort of thing that might have been anticipated.” 

“but what more or less happened, aunt hester?” i asked. 

“well, of course i can’t tell you everything, for no one knew it. but 
he was a very sinful man, and the scandal about him at newlyn was 
shocking. and then he lived, too, in the quarry-house.... i wonder 
if by any chance you remember a sermon of your uncle’s when he got 
out of the pulpit and explain that panel in the altar-rails, the 
one, i mean, with the horrible creature rear itself up outside the 
lych-gate?” 

“yes, i remember perfectly,” said i. 

“ah. it made an impression on you, i suppose, and so it did on all who 
heard him, and that impression got stamp and brand on us all when 
the catastrophe occurred. somehow mr. dooliss got to hear about your 
uncle’s sermon, and in some drunken fit he broke into the church and 
smashed the panel to atoms. he seem to have thought that there was 
some magic in it, and that if he destroy that he would get rid of 
the terrible fate that was threaten him. for i must tell you that 
before he committed that dreadful sacrilege he had been a haunt man: 
he hate and fear darkness, for he thought that the creature on the 
panel was on his track, but that as long as he kept lights burning 
it could not touch him. but the panel, to his disorder mind, was 
the root of his terror, and so, as i said, he broke into the church 
and attempted--you will see why i said ‘attempted’--to destroy it. it 
certainly was found in splinter next morning, when your uncle went 
into church for matins, and knowing mr. dooliss’s fear of the panel, 
he went across to the quarry-house afterwards and tax him with its 
destruction. the man never deny it; he boast of what he had done.
