there he sat, though it was early morning, drink his whisky. 

“‘i’ve settled your thing for you,’ he said, ‘and your sermon too. a 
fig for such superstitions.’ 

“your uncle left him without answer his blasphemy, meaning to go 
straight into penzance and give information to the police about this 
outrage to the church, but on his way back from the quarry-house he 
went into the church again, in order to be able to give detail about 
the damage, and there in the screen was the panel, untouched and 
uninjured. and yet he had himself seen it smashed, and mr. dooliss had 
confessed that the destruction of it was his work. but there it was, 
and whether the power of god had mend it or some other power, who 
knows?” 

this was polearn indeed, and it was the spirit of polearn that made me 
accept all aunt hester was telling me as attested fact. it had happen 
like that. she went on in her quiet voice. 

“your uncle recognise that some power beyond police was at work, and 
he did not go to penzance or give information about the outrage, for 
the evidence of it had vanished.” 

a sudden spate of scepticism swept over me. 

“there must have been some mistake,” i said. “it hadn’t been broken....” 

she smiled. 

“yes, my dear, but you have been in london so long,” she said. “let 
me, anyhow, tell you the rest of my story. that night, for some reason, 
i could not sleep. it was very hot and airless; i dare say you will 
think that the sultry conditions account for my wakefulness. once and 
again, as i went to the window to see if i could not admit more air, i 
could see from it the quarry-house, and i notice the first time that 
i left my bed that it was blazing with lights. but the second time i 
saw that it was all in darkness, and as i wonder at that, i heard a 
terrible scream, and the moment afterwards the steps of someone coming 
at full speed down the road outside the gate. he yell as he ran; 
‘light, light!’ he call out. ‘give me light, or it will catch me!’ it 
was very terrible to hear that, and i went to rouse my husband, who was 
sleep in the dressing-room across the passage. he waste no time, 
but by now the whole village was arouse by the screams, and when he 
got down to the pier he found that all was over. the tide was low, and 
on the rocks at its foot was lie the body of mr. dooliss. he must 
have cut some artery when he fell on those sharp edge of stone, for 
he had bled to death, they thought, and though he was a big burly man, 
his corpse was but skin and bones. yet there was no pool of blood round 
him, such as you would have expected. just skin and bone as if every 
drop of blood in his body had been suck out of him!” 

she lean forward. 

“you and i, my dear, know what happened,” she said, “or at least 
can guess. god has his instrument of vengeance on those who bring 
wicked into place that have been holy. dark and mysterious are his 
ways.” 

now what i should have thought of such a story if it had been told me 
in london i can easily imagine. there was such an obvious explanation: 
the man in question had been a drunkard, what wonder if the demon of 
delirium pursue him? but here in polearn it was different. 

“and who is in the quarry-house now?” i asked. “years ago the 
fisher-boys told me the story of the man who first built it and of his 
horrible end. and now again it has happened. surely no one has venture 
to inhabit it once more?” 

i saw in her face, even before i ask that question, that somebody had 
done so. 

“yes, it is live in again,” said she, “for there is no end to the 
blindness.... i don’t know if you remember him. he was tenant of the 
vicarage many years ago.” 

“john evans,” said i. 

“yes. such a nice fellow he was too. your uncle was pleased to get so 
good a tenant. and now----” 

she rose. 

“aunt hester, you shouldn’t leave your sentence unfinished,” i said. 

she shook her head. 

“my dear, that sentence will finish itself,” she said. “but what a time 
of night! i must go to bed, and you too, or they will think we have to 
keep lights burning here through the dark hours.” 

* * * * * 

before get into bed i drew my curtains wide and open all the 
window to the warm tide of the sea air that flow soft in. look 
out into the garden i could see in the moonlight the roof of the 
shelter, in which for three years i had lived, gleam with dew. that, 
as much as anything, brought back the old days to which i had now 
returned, and they seem of one piece with the present, as if no gap 
of more than twenty years sunder them. the two flow into one like 
globule of mercury unite into a soft shining globe, of mysterious 
lights and reflections. then, raise my eyes a little, i saw against 
the black hill-side the window of the quarry-house still alight. 

morning, as is so often the case, brought no shatter of my illusion. 
as i began to regain consciousness, i fancy that i was a boy again 
waking up in the shelter in the garden, and though, as i grew more 
widely awake, i smile at the impression, that on which it was base 
i found to be indeed true. it was sufficient now as then to be here, 
to wander again on the cliffs, and hear the pop of the ripen 
seed-pods on the gorse-bushes; to stray along the shore to the 
bathing-cove, to float and drift and swim in the warm tide, and bask 
on the sand, and watch the gull fishing, to lounge on the pier-head 
with the fisher-folk, to see in their eyes and hear in their quiet 
speech the evidence of secret things not so much known to them as 
part of their instinct and their very being. there were powers and 
presence about me; the white poplar that stood by the stream that 
babble down the valley knew of them, and show a glimpse of their 
knowledge sometimes, like the gleam of their white underleaves; the 
very cobble that paved the street were soaked in it.... all that i 
want was to lie there and grow soaked in it too; unconsciously, as 
a boy, i had done that, but now the process must be conscious. i must 
know what stir of forces, fruitful and mysterious, seethe along the 
hill-side at noon, and sparkle at night on the sea. they could be 
known, they could even be control by those who were master of the 
spell, but never could they be spoken of, for they were dwell in the 
innermost, graft into the eternal life of the world. there were dark 
secret as well as these clear, kindly powers, and to these no doubt 
belong the _negotium perambulan in tenebris_ which, though of deadly 
malignity, might be regard not only as evil, but as the avenge of 
sacrilegious and impious deeds.... all this was part of the spell of 
polearn, of which the seed had long lain dormant in me. but now they 
were sprouting, and who knew what strange flower would unfold on their 
stems? 

it was not long before i came across john evans. one morning, as i lay 
on the beach, there came shamble across the sand a man stout and 
middle-aged with the face of silenus. he pause as he drew near and 
regard me from narrow eyes. 

“why, you’re the little chap that use to live in the parson’s garden,” 
he said. “don’t you recognise me?” 

i saw who it was when he spoke: his voice, i think, instruct me, and 
recognise it, i could see the features of the strong, alert young man 
in this gross caricature. 

“yes, you’re john evans,” i said. “you use to be very kind to me: you 
use to draw pictures for me.” 

“so i did, and i’ll draw you some more. been bathing? that’s a risky 
performance. you never know what lives in the sea, nor what lives on 
the land for that matter. not that i heed them. i stick to work and 
whisky. god! i’ve learned to paint since i saw you, and drink too for 
that matter. i live in the quarry-house, you know, and it’s a powerful 
thirsty place. come and have a look at my things if you’re passing. 
stay with your aunt, are you? i could do a wonderful portrait of 
her. interesting face; she know a lot. people who live at polearn get 
to know a lot, though i don’t take much stock in that sort of knowledge 
myself.” 

i do not know when i have been at once so repel and interested. 
behind the mere gross of his face there lurk something which, 
while it appalled, yet fascinate me. his thick lisp speech had the 
same quality. and his paintings, what would they be like?... 

“i was just go home,” i said. “i’ll gladly come in, if you’ll allow 
me.” 

he took me through the untended and overgrown garden into the house 
which i had never yet entered. a great grey cat was sun itself in 
the window, and an old woman was lay lunch in a corner of the cool 
hall into which the door opened. it was built of stone, and the carve 
moulding let into the walls, the fragment of gargoyle and sculpture 
images, bore testimony to the truth of its have been built out of 
the demolish church. in one corner was an oblong and carve wooden 
table litter with a painter’s apparatus and stack of canvas lean 
against the walls. 

he jerk his thumb towards a head of an angel that was built into the 
mantelpiece and giggled. 

“quite a sanctify air,” he said, “so we tone it down for the 
purpose of ordinary life by a different sort of art. have a drink? no? 
well, turn over some of my pictures while i put myself to rights.” 

he was justified in his own estimate of his skill: he could paint (and 
apparently he could paint anything), but never have i seen pictures 
so inexplicably hellish. there were exquisite study of trees, and 
you knew that something lurk in the flicker shadows. there was a 
drawing of his cat sun itself in the window, even as i had just now 
seen it, and yet it was no cat but some beast of awful malignity. there 
was a boy stretch naked on the sands, not human, but some evil thing 
which had come out of the sea. above all there were pictures of his 
garden overgrown and jungle-like, and you knew that in the bush were 
presence ready to spring out on you.... 

“well, do you like my style?” he said as he came up, glass in hand. 
(the tumbler of spirits that he held had not been diluted.) “i try to 
paint the essence of what i see, not the mere husk and skin of it, but 
its nature, where it come from and what gave it birth. there’s much 
in common between a cat and a fuchsia-bush if you look at them close 
enough. everything came out of the slime of the pit, and it’s all go 
back there. i should like to do a picture of you some day. i’d hold the 
mirror up to nature, as that old lunatic said.” 

after this first meeting i saw him occasional throughout the month 
of that wonderful summer. often he kept to his house and to his 
painting for days together, and then perhaps some evening i would find 
him lounge on the pier, always alone, and every time we met thus the 
repulsion and interest grew, for every time he seem to have gone 
farther along a path of secret knowledge towards some evil shrine 
where complete initiation await him.... and then sudden the end 
came. 

i had met him thus one evening on the cliff while the october sunset 
still burn in the sky, but over it with amazing rapid there spread 
from the west a great black of cloud such as i have never seen for 
denseness. the light was suck from the sky, the dusk fell in ever 
thick layers. he sudden became conscious of this. 

“i must get back as quick as i can,” he said. “it will be dark in a few 
minutes, and my servant is out. the lamp will not be lit.” 

he step out with extraordinary brisk for one who shamble and 
could scarcely lift his feet, and soon broke out into a stumble run. 
in the gathering dark i could see that his face was moist with the 
dew of some unspoken terror. 

“you must come with me,” he panted, “for so we shall get the lights 
burning the sooner. i cannot do without light.” 

i had to exert myself to the full to keep up with him, for terror 
wing him, and even so i fell behind, so that when i came to the 
garden gate, he was already half-way up the path to the house. i saw 
him enter, leave the door wide, and found him fumble with matches. 
but his hand so tremble that he could not transfer the light to the 
wick of the lamp. 

“but what’s the hurry about?” i asked. 

sudden his eyes focus themselves on the open door behind me, and he 
jump from his seat beside the table which had once been the altar of 
god, with a gasp and a scream. 

“no, no!” he cried. “keep it off!...” 

i turn and saw what he had seen. the thing had enter and now was 
swift slide across the floor towards him, like some gigantic 
caterpillar. a stale phosphorescent light came from it, for though the 
dusk had grown to black outside, i could see it quite distinct in 
the awful light of its own presence. from it too there came an odour 
of corruption and decay, as from slime that has long lain below water. 
it seem to have no head, but on the front of it was an orifice of 
pucker skin which open and shut and slaver at the edges. it was 
hairless, and slug-like in shape and in texture. as it advanced its 
fore-part rear itself from the ground, like a snake about to strike, 
and it fasten on him.... 

at that sight, and with the yell of his agony in my ears, the panic 
which had struck me relax into a hopeless courage, and with palsied, 
impotent hand i try to lay hold of the thing. but i could not: 
though something material was there, it was impossible to grasp it; 
my hand sunk in it as in thick mud. it was like wrestle with a 
nightmare. 

i think that but a few seconds elapse before all was over. the scream 
of the wretched man sank to moan and mutter as the thing fell 
on him: he pant once or twice and was still. for a moment long 
there came gurgle and suck noises, and then it slid out even as 
it had entered. i lit the lamp which he had fumble with, and there 
on the floor he lay, no more than a rind of skin in loose fold over 
project bones. 




at the farmhouse 




at the farmhouse 


the dusk of a november day was fall fast when john aylsford came 
out of his lodging in the cobble street and start to walk brisk 
along the road which led eastward by the shore of the bay. he had been 
at work while the daylight serve him, and now, when the gathering 
dark wean him from his easel, he was accustomed to go out for air 
and exercise and cover half a dozen mile before he return to his 
solitary supper. 

to-night there were but few folk abroad, and those scud along before 
the strong south-westerly gale which had roar and rage all day, or, 
leaning forward, beat their way against it. no fishing-boats had put 
forth on that madden sea, but had lain moor behind the quay-wall, 
toss uneasy with the backwash of the great breaker that swept 
by the pier-head. the tide was low now, and they rest on the sandy 
beach, black blot against the smooth wet surface which sombre 
reflect the last flame in the west. the sun had gone down in a wrack 
of broken and flying clouds, angry and menace with promise of a wild 
night to come. 

for many days past, at this hour john aylsford had start eastward 
for his tramp along the rough coast road by the bay. the last high 
tide had swept shingle and sand over section of it, and fragment of 
seaweed, driven by the wind, bowl along the ruts. the heavy boom of 
the breaker sound sullen in the dusk, and white tower of foam 
appear and disappear show how high they leap over the reef 
of rock beyond the headland. for half a mile or so, slant himself 
against the gale he pursue this road, then turn up a narrow muddy 
lane sunk deep between the bank on either side of it. it ran steeple 
uphill, dip down again, and join the main road inland. have 
arrive at the junction, john aylsford went eastward no more, but 
turn his steps to the west, arriving, half an hour after he had set 
out, on the top of the hill above the village he had quitted, though 
five minutes’ ascent would have taken him from his lodgings to the spot 
where he now stood look down on the scattered lights below him. the 
wind had blown all wayfarer indoors, and now in front of him the road 
that cross this high and desolate table-land, sprinkle here and 
there with lonely cottage and solitary farms, lay empty and grey 
glimmer in the wind-swept darkness, not more than faint visible. 

many times during this past month had john aylsford made this long 
detour, start eastward from the village and coming back by a wide 
circuit, and now, as on these other occasions, he pause in the black 
shelter of the hedge through which the wind hiss and whistled, 
crouch there in the shadow as if to make sure that none had follow 
him, and that the road in front lay void of passengers, for he had no 
mind to be observe by any on these journeyings. and as he pause he 
let his hate blaze up, warm him for the work the accomplishment of 
which alone could enable him to recapture any peace or profit from 
life. to-night he was determined to release himself from the millstone 
which for so many years had hung round his neck, drown him in bitter 
waters. from long brood over the idea of the deed, he had quite 
cease to feel any horror of it. the death of that drunken slut was not 
a matter for qualm or uneasiness; the world would be well rid of her, 
and he more than well. 

no spark of tender for the handsome fisher-girl who once had been 
his model and for twenty years had been his wife pierce the black 
of his purpose. just here it was that he had seen her first when on a 
summer holiday he had lodge with a couple of friends in the farmhouse 
towards which his way now lay. she was coming up the hill with the 
late sunset gilding her face, and, breathing quick from the ascent, 
had lean on the wall close by with a smile and a glance for the 
young man. she had sat to him, and the autumn brought the sequel to 
the summer in his marriage. he had bought from her uncle the little 
farmhouse where he had lodged, add to its modest accommodation a 
studio and a bedroom above it, and there he had seen the flicker of 
what had never been love, die out, and over the cold ashes of its 
ember the poisonous lichen of hatred spread fast. early in their 
married life she had taken to drink, and had sunk into a degrade of 
soul and body that seem bottomless, drag him with her, down and 
down, in the grip of a force that was hardly human in its malignity. 

often during the wretched years that follow he had try to leave 
her; he had offer to settle the farm on her and make adequate 
provision for her, but she had clung to the possession of him, not, 
it would seem, from any affection for him, but for a reason exactly 
opposite, namely, that her hatred of him fed and glut itself on the 
sight of his ruin. it was as if, in obedience to some hellish power, 
she set herself to spoil his life, his powers, his possibilities, by 
tie him to herself. and by the aid of that power, so sometimes he had 
thought, she enforce her will on him, for, plan as he might to cut the 
whole dreadful business and leave the wreck behind him, he had never 
been able to consolidate his resolve into action. there, but a few 
mile away, was the station from which ran the train that would bear 
him out of this ancient western kingdom, where the belief in spell 
and superstition grew rank as the herbage in that soft enervate air, 
and set him in the dry hard light of cities. the way lay open, but he 
could not take it; something unseen and potent, of grim inflexibility, 
held him back.... 

he had pass no one on his way here, and satisfy now that in the 
dark he could proceed without fear of being recognise if a chance 
wayfarer came from the direction in which he was going, he left the 
shelter of the hedge, and struck out into the stormy sea of that 
stupendous gale. even as a man in the grip of imminent death see his 
past life spread itself out in front of him for his final survey before 
the book is closed, so now, on the brink of the new life from which the 
deed on which he was determined alone separate him, john aylsford, as 
he battle his advance through this great tempest, turn over page 
after page of his own wretched chronicles, feeling already strange 
detached from them; it was as if he read the sordid and enslave annals 
of another, wonder at them, half-pitying, half-despising him who had 
allow himself to be bound so long in this ruinous noose. 

yes; it had been just that, a noose drawn ever tight round his neck, 
while he choke and struggle all unavailingly. but there was another 
noose which should very soon now be drawn rapid and finally tight, 
and the drawing of that in his own strong hand would free him. as he 
dwelt on that for a moment, his finger stroke and pat the hank of 
whipcord that lay white and tough in his pocket. a noose, a knot drawn 
quick taut, and he would have paid her back with justice and swift 
mercy for the long strangle which he had suffered. 

voluntary and eager at the beginning had he allow her to slip the 
noose about him, for ellen trenair’s beauty in those days, so long past 
and so everlastingly regretted, had been enough to ensnare a man. he 
had been warn at the time, by hint and half-spoken suggestion, that 
it was ill for a man to mate with a girl of that dark and ill-famed 
family, or for a woman to wed a boy in whose vein ran the blood of 
jona trenair, once methodist preacher, who learned on one all-hallows’ 
eve a dark gospel than he had ever preach before. what had happen 
to the girl who had married into that dwindle family, now all 
but extinct? one, before her marriage was a year old, had gone off 
her head, and now, a wither and ancient crone, mow and gibber 
about the street of the village, pick garbage from the gutter and 
munch it in her toothless jaws. another, ellen’s own mother, had 
been found hanging from the banister of her stairs, stark and grim. 
then there was young frank pencarris, who had wed ellen’s sister. 
he had sunk into an awful melancholy, and sat tracing on sheet of 
paper the vision that beset his eyes, headless shapes, and foam 
mouths, and the image of the spawn of hell.... john aylsford, in those 
early days, had laugh to scorn these old-wife tale of spell and 
sorceries: they belong to age long past, whereas fair ellen trenair 
was of the lovely present, and had lit desire in his heart which she 
alone could assuage. he had no use, in the bright of her eye, for 
such shadow and superstitions; her beam dispel them. 

bitter and black as midnight had his enlightenment been, darken 
through dubious dusk till the mirk of the pit itself envelop him. 
his laughter at the notion that in this twentieth century spell and 
sorcery could survive, grew silent on his lips. he had seen the 
cattle of a neighbour who had offend one whom it was wise not to 
cross, dwindle and pine, though there were rich pasture for their 
grazing, till the rib-bones stuck out like the timber of stranded 
wrecks. he had seen the spring on another farm run dry at lambing-time 
because the owner, sceptic like himself, had refuse that bounty, which 
all prudent folk paid to the wizard of mareuth, who, like ellen, was of 
the blood of jona trenair. from scorn and laughter he had waver to 
an uneasy wonder, and from wonder his mind had pass to the conviction 
that there were powers occult and terrible which strove in dark and 
prevailed, secret and spell that could send disease on man and beast, 
dark incantations, known to few, which could maim and cripple, and of 
these few his wife was one. his reason revolted, but some conviction, 
deep than reason, held its own. to such a view it seem that the 
deed he contemplate was no crime, but rather an act of obedience to 
the ordinance “thou shalt not suffer a witch to live.” and the sense of 
detachment was over that, even as over the memory that ooze up in 
his mind. somebody--not he--who had plan everything very careful 
was in the next hour go to put an end to his bondage. 

so the years had passed, he flounder ever deep in the slough into 
which he was plunged, out of which while she live he could never 
emerge. for the last year, she, weary of his perpetual presence at 
the farm, had allow him to take a lodging in the village. she did 
not loose her hold over him, for the days were few on which she did 
not come with demand for a handful of shilling to procure her the 
raw spirits which alone could slake her thirst. sometimes as he sat 
at work there in the north room look on to the small garden-yard, 
she would come lurch up the path, with her bloated crimson face set 
on the wither neck, and tap at his window with finger shrivel 
like bird’s claws. body and limb were no more than bone over which 
the wrinkle skin was stretched, but her face bulge monstrous with 
layer of fat. he would give her whatever he had about him, and if 
it was not enough, she would plant herself there, grin at him 
and wheedle him, or with scream and curse threaten him with 
such fate as he had known to overtake those who cross her will. but 
usually he gave her enough to satisfy her for that day and perhaps the 
next, for thus she would the more quick drink herself to death. yet 
death seem long in coming.... 

he remember well how first the notion of killing her came into his 
head, just a little seed, small as that of mustard, which lay long 
in barrenness. only the bare idea of it was there, like an abstract 
proposition. then imperceptible in the fruitful dark of his mind, 
it must have begun to sprout, for presently a tendril, still soft and 
white, prod out into the daylight. he almost pushed it back again, 
for fear that she, by some divine art, should probe his purpose. 
but when next she came for supplies, he saw no gleam of surmise in 
her red-rimmed eyes, and she took her money and went her way, and 
his purpose put forth another leaf, and the stem of it grew sappy. 
all autumn through it had flourished, and grown tree-like, and fresh 
ideas, fresh details, fresh precautions, flock there like building 
bird and made it gay with singing. he sat under the shadow of it and 
listen with brighten hope to their song; never had there been 
such peerless melody. they knew their tune now, there was no need for 
any further rehearsal. 

he began to wonder how soon he would be back on the road again, with 
face turn from this buffet wind, and on his way home. his business 
would not take him long; the central deed of it would be over in a 
couple of minutes, and he did not anticipate delay about the setting 
to work on it, for by seven o’clock of the evening, as well he knew, 
she was usually snore in the oblivion of complete drunkenness, 
and even if she was not as far gone as that, she would certainly be 
incapable of any serious resistance. after that, a quarter of an hour 
more would finish the job, and he would leave the house secure already 
from any chance of detection. night after night during these last ten 
days he had been up here, peer from the dark into the light 
room where she sat, then listen for her step on the stairs as 
she stumble up to bed, or hearing her snore as she slept in her 
chair below. the out-house, he knew, was well stock with paraffin; 
he need no further apparatus than the whipcord and the match he 
carry with him. then back he would go along the exact route by which 
he had come, re-entering the village again from the eastwards, in which 
direction he had set out. 

this walk of his was now a known and establish habit; half the 
village during the last week or two had seen him every evening set 
forth along the coast road, for a tramp in the dusk when the light 
fail for his painting, and had seen him come back again as they hung 
about and smoke in the warm dusk, a couple of hour later. none knew 
of his detour to the main road which took him westwards again above 
the village and so to the stretch of bleak upland along which now he 
fought his way against the gale. always round about the hour of eight 
he had enter the village again from the other side, and had stop 
and chat with the loiterers. to-night, no later than was usual, he 
would come up the cobble road again, and give “good night” to any who 
linger there outside the public-house. in this wild wind it was not 
likely that there would be such, and if so, no matter; he had been seen 
already setting forth on his usual walk by the coast of the bay, and 
if none outside saw him return, none could see the true chart of his 
walk. by eight he should be back to his supper, there would be a soused 
herring for him, and a cut of cheese, and the kettle would be singing 
on the hob for his hot whisky-toddy. he would have a keen edge for the 
enjoyment of them to-night; he would drink long health to the damn 
and the dead. not till to-morrow, probably, would the news of what had 
happen reach him, for the farmhouse lay lonely and sheltered by the 
wood of firs. however high might mount the beacon of its blazing, it 
would scarcely, screen by the tall trees, light up the western sky, 
and be seen from the village nestling below the steep hill-crest. 

by now john aylsford had come to the fir wood which border the road 
on the left, and, as he pass into its shelter, cut off from him the 
violence of the gale. all its branch were astir with the sound of 
some vexed, overhead sea, and the trunks that upheld them creak and 
groan in the fury of the tempest. somewhere behind the thick scud 
of flying cloud the moon must have risen, for the road glimmer more 
visibly, and the toss black of the branch was clear enough 
against the grey tumult overhead. behind the tempest she rode in serene 
skies, and in the murderous clarity of his mind he liken himself to 
her. just for half an hour more he would still grope and scheme and 
achieve in this hurly-burly, and then, like a balloon released, soar 
through the cloud and find serenity. a couple of hundred yard now 
would take him round the corner of the wood; from there the miry lane 
led from the high-road to the farm. 

he hasten rather than retarded his go as he drew near, for the 
wood, though it roar with the gale, began to whisper to him of 
memories. often in that summer before his marriage had he stray 
out at dusk into it, certain that before he had gone many pace he 
would see a shadow flit towards him through the firs, or hear the 
crack of dry twig in the stillness. here was their tryst; she would 
come up from the village with the excuse of bring fish to the 
farmhouse, after the boat had come in, and desert the high-road 
make a short cut through the wood. like some distant blink of lightning 
the memory of those evenings quiver distantly on his mind, and he 
quicken his step. the years that follow had kill and bury 
those recollections, but who knew what stirring of corpse and dry 
bone might not yet come to them if he linger there? he finger the 
whipcord in his pocket, and launch out, beyond the trees, into the 
full fury of the gale. 

the farmhouse was near now and in full view, a black blot against 
the clouds. a beam of light shone from an uncurtained window on the 
ground-floor, and the rest was dark. even thus had he seen it for many 
nights past, and well knew what sight would greet him as he stole up 
nearer. and even so it was to-night, for there she sat in the studio he 
had built, betwixt table and fireplace with the bottle near her, and 
her wither hand stretch out to the blaze, and the huge bloated 
face sway on her shoulders. beside her to-night were the wreck 
remains of a chair, and the first sight that he caught of her was to 
show her feed the fire with the broken pieces of it. it had been too 
troublesome to bring fresh log from the store of wood; to break up a 
chair was the easy task. 

she stir and sat more upright, then reach out for the bottle that 
stood beside her, and drank from the mouth of it. she drank and lick 
her lip and drank again, and stagger to her feet, tripping on the 
edge of the hearthrug. for the moment that seem to anger her, and 
with clench teeth and point finger she mumble at it; then once 
more she drank, and lurch forward, took the lamp from the table. 
with it in her hand she shuffle to the door, and the room was left to 
the flicker firelight. a moment afterwards, the bedroom window above 
sprang into light, an oblong of bright illumination. 

as soon as that appear he crept round the house to the door. he 
gently turn the handle of it, and found it unlocked. inside was a 
small passage entrance, on the left of which ascend the stairs to 
the bedroom above the studio. all was silent there, but from where he 
stood he could see that the door into the bedroom was open, for a shaft 
of light from the lamp she had carry up with her was shed on to the 
landing there.... everything was smooth itself out to render his 
course most easy. even the gale was his friend, for it would be bellows 
for the fire. he slip off his shoes, leave them on the mat, and 
drew the whipcord from his pocket. he made a noose in it, and began to 
ascend the stairs. they were well-built of season oak, and no creak 
betray his advance footfall. 

at the top he paused, listen for any stir of movement within, but 
there was nothing to be heard but the sound of heavy breathing from 
the bed that lay to the left of the door and out of sight. she had 
thrown herself down there, he guessed, without undressing, leave 
the lamp to burn itself out. he could see it through the open door 
already beginning to flicker; on the wall behind it were a couple of 
water-colours, pictures of his own, one of the little wall garden by 
the farm, the other of the pinewood of their tryst. well he remember 
painting them: she would sit by him as he work with prattle and 
singing. he look at them now quite detachedly; they seem to him 
wonderful good, and he envy the artist that fresh, clean skill. 
perhaps he would take them down presently and carry them away with him. 

very soft now he advanced into the room, and look round the corner 
of the door, he saw her, sprawl and fully dress on the broad 
bed. she lay on her back, eyes closed and mouth open, her dull grey 
hair spread over the pillow. evidently she had not made the bed that 
day, for she lay stretch on the crumple back-turned blankets. a 
hair-brush was on the floor beside her; it seem to have fallen from 
her hand. he move quick towards her. 

* * * * * 

he put on his shoe again when he came to the foot of the stairs, 
carry the lamp with him and the two pictures which he had taken down 
from the wall, and went into the studio. he set the lamp on the table 
and drew down the blinds, and his eye fell on the half-empty whisky 
bottle from which he had seen her drinking. though his hand was quite 
steady and his mind compose and tranquil, there was yet at the back 
of it some impression that was slow developing, and a good dose of 
spirits would no doubt expunge that. he drank half a tumbler of it raw 
and undiluted, and though it seem no more than water in his mouth, 
he soon felt that it was do its work and sponge away from his 
mind the picture that had been outline itself there. in a couple 
of minutes he was quite himself again, and could afford to wonder 
and laugh at the illusion, for it was no less than that, which had 
been gain on him. for though he could distinct remember drawing 
the noose tight, and seeing the face grow black, and struggle with 
the convulsive movements of those wither limb that soon lay quiet 
again, there had sprung up in his mind some unaccountable impression 
that what he had left there huddle on the bed was not just the bundle 
of wither limb and strangle neck, but the body of a young girl, 
smooth of skin and golden of hair, with mouth that smile drowsily. 
she had been asleep when he came in, and now was half-awake, and was 
stirring and stretch herself. in what dim region of his mind that 
image had form itself, he had no idea; all he care about now was 
that his drink had shatter it again, and he could proceed with order 
and method to make all secure. just one drop more first: how lucky it 
was that this morning he had been liberal with his money when she came 
to the village, for he would have been sorry to have gone without that 
fillip to his nerves. 

he look at his watch, and saw to his satisfaction that it was still 
only a little after seven o’clock. half an hour’s walking, with this 
gale to speed his steps, would easily carry him from door to door, 
round the detour which approach the village from the east, and a 
quarter of an hour, so he reckoned, would be sufficient to accomplish 
thorough what remain to be done here. he must not hurry and thus 
overlook some precaution needful for his safety, though, on the other 
hand, he would be glad to be gone from the house as soon as might 
be, and he proceed to set about his work without delay. there was 
brushwood and fire-kindling to be brought in from the wood-shed in 
the yard, and he made three journeys, return each time with his 
arms full, before he had brought in what he judge to be sufficient. 
most of this he pile in a loose heap in the studio; with the rest he 
ascend once more to the bedroom above and made a heap of it there in 
the middle of the floor. he took the curtains down from the windows, 
for they would make a fine wick for the paraffin, and stuff them into 
the pile. before he left, he look once more at what lay on the bed, 
and marvel at the illusion which the whisky had dispelled, and as 
he looked, the sense that he was free mount and bubble in his head. 
the thing seem scarcely human at all; it was a monster from which he 
had deliver himself, and now, with the thought of that to warm him, 
he was no long eager to get through with his work and be gone, for 
it was all part of that act of riddance which he had accomplished, and 
he glory in it. soon, when all was ready, he would come back once 
more and soak the fuel and set light to it, and purge with fire the 
corruption that lay hump on the bed. 

the fury of the gale had increase with nightfall, and as he went 
downstairs again he heard the rattle of loosen tile on the roof, and 
the crash as they shatter themselves on the cobble of the yard. at 
that a sudden misgiving made his breath to catch in his throat, as he 
picture to himself some maniac blast fall on the house and crashing 
in the wall that now tremble and shuddered. supposing the whole 
house fell, even if he escape with his life from the topple ruin, 
what would his life be worth? there would be search made in the fallen 
débris to find the body of her who lay strangle with the whipcord 
round her neck, and he picture to himself the slow, relentless march 
of justice. he had bought whipcord only yesterday at a shop in the 
village, insist on its strength and tough ... would it be wise 
now, this moment, to untie the noose and take it back with him or add 
it to his brushwood?... he pause on the staircase, ponder that; but 
his flesh quake at the thought, and master of himself though he had 
been during those few struggle minutes, he distrust his power of 
making himself handle once more that which could struggle no longer. 
but even as he try to screw his courage to the point, the violence 
of the squall passed, and the shudder house brace itself again. 
he need not fear that; the gale was his friend that would blow on the 
flames, not his enemy. the blast that trumpet overhead were the 
voice of the ally who had come to aid him. 

all was arrange then upstairs for the pour of the paraffin and the 
lighting of the pyre; it remain but to make similar disposition in 
the studio. he would stay to feed the flame till they rage beyond all 
power of extinction; and now he began to plan the line of his retreat. 
there were two door in the studio: one by the fireplace which open 
on to the little garden; the other gave into the passage entrance from 
which mount the stairs and so to the door through which he had come 
into the house. he decided to use the garden-door for his exit; but 
when he came to open it, he found that the key was stiff in the rusty 
lock, and did not yield to his efforts. there was no use in waste 
time over that; it made no difference through which door he finally 
emerged, and he began pile up his heap of wood at that end of the 
room. the lamp was burning low; but the fire, which only so few minutes 
ago she had fed with a broken chair, shone brightly, and a flaming 
ember from it would serve to set light to his conflagration. there was 
a straw mat in front of it, which would make fine kindling, and with 
these two fires, one in the bedroom upstairs and the other here, there 
would be no mistake about the incinerate of the house and all that 
it contained. his own crime, if crime it was, would perish, too, and 
all evidence thereof, victim and whipcord, and the very wall of the 
house of sin and hate. it was a great deed and a fine adventure, and 
as the liquor he had drunk began to circulate more buoyant through 
his veins, he glory at the thought of the approach consummation. 
he would slip out of the sordid tragedy of his past life, as from a 
discard garment that he threw into the bonfire he would soon kindle. 

all was ready now for the soaking of the fuel he had pile with the 
paraffin, and he went out to the shed in the yard where the barrel 
stood. a big tin ewer stood beside it, which he fill and carry 
indoors. that would be sufficient for the soaking of the pile upstairs, 
and fetching the smoky and flicker lamp from the studio, he went 
up again, and like a careful garden water some bed of choice 
blossoms, he sprinkle and pour till his ewer was empty. he gave 
but one glance to the bed behind him, where the huddle thing lay 
so quietly, and as he turned, lamp in hand, to go down again, the 
draught that came in through the window against which the gale blew, 
extinguish it. a little blue flame of burning vapour rose in the 
chimney and went out; so, have no further use for it, he pitch it 
on to the pile of soaked material. as he left the room he thought he 
heard some small stir of movement behind him, but he told himself that 
it was but something slip in the heap he had built there. 

again he went out into the storm. the cloud that scud overhead were 
thinner now, though the gale blew not less fiercely, and the blurred, 
watery moonlight was brighter. once for a moment, as he approach 
the shed, he caught sight of the full orb plunge madly among the 
stream vapours; then she was hidden again behind the wrack. close in 
front of him were the fir tree of the wood where those sweet tryst 
had been held, and once again the vision of her as she had been broke 
into his mind and the queer conviction that it was no wither and 
bloated hag, who lay on the bed upstairs but the fair, comely limb 
and the golden head. it was even more vivid now, and he made haste to 
get back to the studio, where he would find the trusty medicine that 
had dispel that vision before. he would have to make two journey 
at least with his tin ewer before he transport enough oil to feed 
the large pyre below, and so, to save time, he took the barrel off 
its stand, and roll it along the path and into the house. he pause 
at the foot of the stairs, listen to hear if anything stirred, but 
all was silent. whatever had slip up there was steady again; from 
outside only came the squeal and bellow of the wind. 

the studio was bright but fitful lit by the flame on the hearth; 
at one moment a noonday blaze there, the next but the last smoulder of 
some red sunset. it was easy to decant from the barrel into his ewer 
than carry the heavy keg and sprinkle from it, and once and once again 
he fill and empty it. one more application would be sufficient, 
and after that he could let what remain trickle out on to the floor. 
but by some awkward movement he manage to spill a splash of it down 
the front of his trousers: he must be sure, therefore (how quick his 
brain respond with counsel of precautions), to have some accident 
with his lamp when he came in to his supper, which should account for 
this little misadventure. or, probably, the wind through which he would 
presently be walking would dry it before he reach the village. 

so, for the last time with match ready in his hand, he mount the 
stairs to set light to the fuel pile in the room above. his second 
dose of whisky sang in his head, and he said to himself, smile at 
the humour of the notion, “she always like a fire in her bedroom; she 
shall have it now.” that seem a very comical idea, and it dwelt in 
his head as he struck the match which should light it for her. then, 
still grinning, he gave one glance to the bed, and the smile die on 
his face, and the wild cymbal of panic crash in his brain. the bed 
was empty; no huddle shape lay there. 

distraught with terror, he thrust the match into the soaked pile and 
the flame flared up. perhaps the body had roll off the bed. it must, 
in any case, be here somewhere, and when once the room was alight there 
would be nothing more to fear. high rose the smoky flame, and bang 
the door, he leap down the stairs to set light to the pile below and 
be gone from the house. yet, whatever monstrous miracle his eye had 
assured him of, it could not be that she still live and had left the 
place where she lay, for she had cease to breathe when the noose was 
tight round her neck, and her fight for life and air had long been 
stilled. but, if by some hideous witchcraft, she was not dead, it would 
soon be over now with her in the stupefaction of the smoke and the 
scorching flames. let be; the door was shut and she within, for him it 
remain to be finished with the business, and flee from the house of 
terror, lest he leave the sanity of his soul behind him. 

the red glare from the hearth in the studio lit his steps down the 
passage from the stairway, and already he could hear from above the 
dry crack and snap from the fire that prosper there. as he shuffle 
in, he held his hand to his head, as if pressing the brain back into 
its cool case, from which it seem eager to fly out into the welter 
of storm and fire and hideous imagination. if he could only control 
himself for a few moments more, all would be done and he would escape 
from this disorder haunt place into the night and the gale, leave 
behind him the blaze that would burn away all perilous stuff. again 
the flame broke out in the ember on the hearth, brave burning, 
and he took from the heart of the glare a fragment on which the fire 
was burst into yellow flowers. he heed not the scorching of his 
hand, for it was but for a moment that he held it, and then plunge it 
into the pile that drip with the oil he had pour on it. a tower 
of flame mounted, licking the rafter of the low ceiling, then die 
away as if suffocate by its own smoke, but crept onwards, nose its 
way along till it reach the straw mat, which blaze fiercely. that 
blaze kindle the courage in him; whatever trick his imagination had 
play on him just now, he had nothing to fear except his own terror, 
which now he master again, for nothing real could ever escape from 
the conflagration, and it was only the real that he feared. spell and 
witchcraft and superstitions, such as for the last twenty years had 
batten on him, were all enclose in that tight-drawn noose. 

it was time to be gone, for all was safe now, and the room was grow 
to oven-heat. but as he picked his way across the floor over which 
runnel of flame from the split barrel were beginning to spread this 
way and that, he heard from above the sound of a door unlatched, and 
footstep light and firm tap on the stairs. for one second the sheer 
catalepsy of panic seize him, but he recover his control, and with 
hand that grope through the thick smoke he found the door. at that 
moment the fire shot up in a blaze of blind flame, and there in the 
doorway stood ellen. it was no wither body and bloated face that 
confront him, but she with whom he had tryst in the wood, with the 
bloom of eternal youth upon her, and the smooth soft hand, on which 
was her wedding-ring, pointed at him. 

it was in vain that he call on himself to rush forward out of that 
torrid and suffocate air. the front door was open, he had but to pass 
her and speed forth safe into the night. but no power from his will 
reach his limbs; his will scream to him, “go, go! push by her: it 
is but a phantom which you fear!” but muscle and sinew were in mutiny, 
and step by step he retreat before that point finger and the 
radiant shape that advanced on him. the flame that flicker over the 
floor had discover the paraffin he had spilt, and leap up his leg. 

just one spot in his brain retain lucid from the encompass 
terror. somewhere behind that barrier of fire there was the second 
door into the garden. he had but cursory attempt to unlock its 
rusty wards; now, surely, the knowledge that there alone was escape 
would give strength to his hand. he leap backwards through the 
flames, still with eyes fixed on her who ever advanced in time with 
his retreat, and turning, wrestle and strove with the key. something 
snap in his hand, and there still in the keyhole was the bare shaft. 

holding his breath, for the heat scorch his throat, he grope towards 
where he knew was the window through which he had first seen her that 
night. the flame lick fierce round it, but there, beneath his 
hand, was the hasp, and he threw it open. at that the wind pour in as 
through the nozzle of a ply bellows, and death rose high and bright 
around him. through the flames, as he sank to the floor, a face radiant 
with revenge smile on him. 




inscrutable decree 




inscrutable decree 


i had found nothing momentous in the more august page of _the times_ 
that morning, and so, just because i was lazy and unwill to embark 
on a host of business that were wait for me, i turn to the first 
page and, beginning with the seventh column, ponder profound over 
“situations vacant,” and hope that the “gentlewoman fond of games,” 
who desire the position of governess, would find the very thing to 
suit her. i glance at the notice of lecture to be deliver under 
the auspices of various learned societies, and was thankful that i had 
not got to give or to listen to any of them. i debate over “business 
opportunities”; i vain try to conjecture clue to mysterious 
“personal” paragraphs, and, still pursue my sideways, crab-fashion 
course, came to “deaths continued.” 

there, with a shock of arrest, i saw that sybil rorke, widow of the 
late sir ernest rorke, had die at torquay, suddenly, at the age of 
thirty-two. it seem strange that there should be only this bare 
announcement concerning a woman who at one time had been so well-known 
and dazzle a figure; and turning to the obituary notices, i found 
that my inattentive skim had overlook a paragraph there of 
appreciation and regret. she had die during her sleep, and it was 
announce that an inquest would be held. my lazy then had been 
of some use, for archie rorke, distant cousin but successor to sir 
ernest’s estate and title, was arrive that evening to spend a few 
country days with me, and i was glad to have known this before he came. 
how it would affect him, or whether, indeed, it would affect him at 
all, i had no idea. 

what a mysterious affair it had been! no one, i supposed, knew the 
history of it except he, now that lady rorke was dead. if anyone knew, 
it should have been myself, and yet archie, my oldest friend, whose 
best man i was to have been, had never open his lip to a syllable of 
explanation. i knew, in fact, no whit more than the whole world knew, 
namely, that a year after sir ernest rorke’s death the engagement of 
his widow to the new baronet, sir archibald rorke, was made public, 
and that within a fortnight of the date fixed for the wedding it was 
laconic announce that the marriage would not take place. when, 
on seeing that, i rang archie up on the telephone, i was told that 
he had already left london, and he wrote to me a few days later from 
lincote--the place in hampshire, which he had inherit from his 
cousin--saying that he had nothing to tell me about the break off 
of his engagement beyond the fact that it was true. the whole--he had 
written a word and careful erase it--episode was now an excise leaf 
from his life. he was propose to stay down at lincote alone for a 
month or so, and would then turn on to the new page. 

lady rorke, so i heard, had also left london immediately and pass 
the summer in italy. then she took a furnish house in torquay, where 
she live for the remainder of the year which intervene between 
the break off of her engagement and her death. she cut herself 
completely off from all her friends--and no woman, surely, ever 
command a large host of them--saw nobody, seldom went outside her 
house and garden, and observe the same unbroken silence as did archie 
about what had happened. and now, with all her youth and charm and 
beauty, she had gone down dumb into the great silence. 

with the prospect of seeing archie that evening it was no wonder that 
the thought of lady rorke ran all day in my head like a tune heard 
long ago which now recall itself to my mind in scattered staves of 
melody. meeting and talks with her, phrase by phrase, reconstruct 
themselves, and as these memory grew definite and complete i found 
that, even as before, when i was actually experience them, there 
lurk underneath the gay rhythm and joyous something _macabre_ 
and mysterious. to-day that was accentuated, whereas before when i 
listen for it, try to isolate it from the rest and so perhaps 
dispel it, it was always overscore by some triumphant crescendo: her 
presence divert eye and ear alike. yet such a simile halts; perhaps, 
still in simile, i shall more accurate define this underlying 
“something” by saying that her presence was like some gorgeous 
rose-bush, full of flowers, and sun, and sweetness; then, even as one 
admire and applaud and inhaled, one saw that among its bud and 
blossom there emerge the spike of some other plant, bitter and 
poisonous, but grow from the same soil as the rose, and intertwine 
with it. but immediately a fresh glory met your eye, a fresh fragrance 
enchant you. 

as i rummage among my memory of her, certain scene which 
significant illustrate this curious vivid impression stir and 
made themselves manifest to me, and now they were not broken in upon 
by her presence. one such occur on the first evening that i ever 
met her, which was in the summer before the death of her husband. the 
moment that she enter the room where we were wait before dinner 
for her arrival, the stale, sultry air of a june evening grew fresh 
and effervescent; never have i come across so radiant and infectious a 
vitality. she was tall and big, with the splendour of the juno-type, 
and though she was then close on thirty, the iridescence of girlhood 
was still hers. without effort she pied-pipered a rather stodgy party 
to dance to her flutings, she cause everyone to become silly and 
pleased and full of laughter. at her bidding we indulge in ridiculous 
games, dumb-crambo, and what not, and after that the carpet was roll 
up and we caper to the strain of a gramophone. and then the incident 
occurred. 

i was standing with her, for a breath of air, on the balcony outside 
the drawing-room window which face the park. she had just made a 
great curtsey to a slip of the moon that rose above the tree and had 
borrow a shilling of me in order to turn it. 

“no, i can’t swear that i believe in moon-luck,” she said, “but after 
all it do no harm, and, in case it’s true, you can’t afford to make 
an enemy of her. ah, what’s that?” 

a thrush, attract by the lights inside, had flown between us, dashed 
itself against the window, and now lay flutter on the ground at our 
feet. instantly she was all pity and tenderness. she picked up the 
bird, examine it, and found that its wing was broken. 

“ah, poor thing!” she said. “look, its wing-bone is snapped; the end 
protrudes. and how terrify it is! what are we to do?” 

it was clear that the kindest thing to do would be to put the bird 
out of its pain, but when i suggest that, she took a step back from 
me, and cover it with her other hand. her eyes gleamed, her mouth 
smiled, and i saw the tip of her tongue swift pass over her lip as 
if licking them. 

“no, that would be a terrible thing to do,” she said. “i shall take it 
home with me ever so carefully, and watch over it. i am afraid it is 
badly hurt. but it may live.” 

suddenly--perhaps it was that swift licking of her lip that suggest 
the thought to me--i felt instinctive that she was not so much 
pitiful as pleased. she stood there with eyes fixed on it, as it feeble 
struggle in her hands. 

and then her face clouded; over its bright there came a look of 
displeasure, of annoyance. 

“i’m afraid it is dying,” she said. “its poor frightened eyes are 
closing.” 

the bird flutter once more, then its leg stretch themselves 
stiff out, and it lay still. she toss it out of her hand on to the 
paved balcony, with a little shrug of her shoulders. 

“what a fuss over a bird,” she said. “it was silly of it to fly 
against the glass. but i have too soft a heart; i cannot bear that the 
poor creature should die. let us go in and have one more romp. oh, 
here is your shilling; i hope it will have brought me good luck. and 
then i must get home. my husband--do you know him?--always sit up till 
i get back, and he will scold me for being so late!” 

there, then, was my first meeting with her, and there, too, were the 
spike of the poisonous plant push up among the magnificence of her 
roses. and yet, so i thought to myself then, and so i think to myself 
now, i perhaps was utterly wrong about it all, in thus attribute to 
her a secret glee of which she was wholly incapable. so, with a certain 
effort i wipe the impression i had received off my mind, determine 
to consider myself quite mistaken. but, involuntarily, my mind as if 
to justify itself in have delineate such a picture, proceed to 
delineate another. 

very shortly after that first meeting i received from her a charming 
note, ask me to dine with her on a date not far distant. i 
telephone a delight acceptance, for, indeed, i want then, even 
as i did this morning, to convince myself that i was wholly in error 
concerning my interpretation of that incident concerning the thrush. 
though i hold that no man has the right to accept the hospitality 
offer by one he do not like, in all points except one i admire 
and like lady rorke immensely and wish to get rid of that one. so 
i grateful accepted, and then hurried out on a dismal and overdue 
visit to the dentist’s. in the waiting-room was a girl of about twelve, 
with a hand nursing a rueful face, and from time to time she stifle 
a sob of pain or apprehension. i was just wonder whether it would 
be a breach of waiting-room etiquette to attempt to administer comfort 
or supply diversion, when the door open and in came lady rorke. she 
laugh delightful when she saw me. 

“hurrah! you’re another occupant of the condemn cell,” she said, “and 
very soon we shall both be sent for to the scaffold. i can’t describe 
to you what a coward i am about it. why haven’t we got beak like 
birds?----” 

her glance fell on the forlorn little figure by the window, with the 
rueful face and the wet eyes. 

“why, here’s another of us,” she said. “and have they sent you to the 
dentist’s all alone, my dear?” 

“y--yes.” 

“how horrid of them!” said lady rorke. “they’ve sent me alone, too, 
and i think it’s most unfeeling. but you shan’t be alone, anyhow, i’ll 
come in with you, and sit by you, if you like that, and box the man’s 
ear for him if he hurt you. or shall you and i set on him, as soon 
as we’ve got him by himself, and take out all his teeth one after the 
other? just to teach him to be a dentist.” 

a faint smile began the break through the clouds. 

“oh, will you come in with me?” she asked. “i shan’t mind nearly so 
much, then. it’s--it’s got to come out, you know, and i mayn’t have 
gas.” 

just the same gleam of a smile as i had seen on lady rorke’s face once 
before quiver there now, a light not of pity, surely. 

“ah, but it won’t ache any more after that,” she said, “and after all, 
it is so soon over. you’ll just open your mouth as if you were go to 
put the largest of all strawberry into it, and you’ll hold tight on 
to my hand, and the dentist take up something which you needn’t look 
at----” 

there was a want of tact in the vivid of this picture, and the 
child began to sob again. 

“oh, don’t, don’t!” she cried. 

again the door opened, and she clung to lady rorke. 

“oh, i know it’s for me!” she wailed. 

lady rorke bent over her, scan her terrify face. 

“come along, my dear,” she said, “and it will be over in no time. 
you’ll be back here again before this gentleman can count a hundred, 
and he’ll have all his trouble in front of him still.” 

again this morning i try to expunge from that picture, so trivial and 
yet so vivid to me, the sinister something which seem to connect it 
with the incident about the thrush, and, leave it, my mind stray 
on over other reminiscences of lady rorke. before the season was over 
i had got to know her well, and the better i knew her the more i 
marvel at that many-petalled vitality, which never cease unfold 
itself. she entertain largely, and had that crown gift of a good 
hostess, namely, that she enjoy her own party quite enormously. she 
was a very fine horsewoman, and after being up till dawn at some dance, 
she would be in the row by half-past eight on a peculiarly vicious mare 
to whom she seem to pay only the most cursory attention. she had a 
good knowledge of music, she dress amazingly, she was charming to her 
meagre little husband, play piquet with him by the hour (which was 
the only thing, apart from herself, that he care about), and if in 
this modern democratic london there could be said to be a queen, there 
is no doubt who that season would have worn the crown. less publicly, 
she was a great student of the psychic and occult, and i remember 
hearing that she was herself possessed of very remarkable mediumistic 
gifts. but to me that was a matter of hearsay, for i never was present 
at any séance of hers. 

yet through the triumphant music of her pageant, there sounded, to 
my ear at least, fragment of a very ugly tune. it was not only in 
these two instance of its emergence that i heard it, it was chiefly 
and most persistent audible in her treatment of archie rorke, her 
husband’s cousin. everyone knew, for none could help knowing, that 
he was desperate in love with her, and it is impossible to imagine 
that she alone was ignorant of it. it is, no doubt, the instinct 
of many women to fan a passion which they do not share, and which 
they have no intention of indulging, just as the male instinct is to 
gratify a passion that he do not really feel, but there are limit 
to mercilessness. she was not “cruel to be kind”; she was kind to be 
demoniacal cruel. she had him always by her; she gave him those 
little touch and comrade-like licence which meant nothing to her, 
but craze him with thirst; she held the glass close to his lip 
and then tilt it up and show it him empty. the more charitable 
explanation was that she, perhaps, knew that her husband could not 
live long, and that she intended to marry archie, and such, so it 
subsequent appeared, her intentions were. but when i saw her feed 
him with husk and putt an empty glass to his lips, nothing, to 
my mind, could account for her treatment of him except a rapture of 
cruelty at the sight of his aching. and somehow, awfully and aptly, 
that seem to fit in with the affair of the thrush, and the meeting 
with the forlorn child in the dentist’s waiting-room. yet ever, through 
that gruesome twilight, there blaze forth her charm and her beauty and 
the beam of her joyous vitality, and i would cudgel myself for my nasty 
interpretations. 

it was early in the spring of next year that i was spend a week-end 
with her and her husband at lincote. she had suggest my coming down 
on saturday morning before the party assemble later in the day, and 
at lunch i was alone with her husband and her. sir ernest was very 
silent; he look ill and haggard, and, in fact, hardly spoke a word 
except when sudden he turn to the butler and said, “has anything 
been heard of the child yet?” he was told that there was no news, and 
subside into silence again. i thought that some queer shadow as of 
suspense or anxiety cross lady rorke’s face at the question; but 
on the answer, it clear off again, and, as if to sweep the subject 
wholly away, she ask me if i could tolerate a saunter with her 
through the wood till her guest arrived. 

out she came like some splendid diana of the forests, and like the 
goddess’s was the swift, swinging pace of her saunter. spring all round 
was riotous in blossom and bird-song; it was just that ecstatic moment 
of the year when the hound of spring have run winter to death, and as 
we gain the high ridge of down above the wood she stop and threw 
her arms wide. 

“oh, the sense of spring!” she cried. “the daffodils, and the west 
wind, and the shadow of the clouds. how i wish i could take the 
whole lot into my arms and hug them. miracle are flowering every 
moment now in the country, while the only miracle in london is the 
mud. what sunshine, what air! drink them in, for they are the one 
divine medicine. one wants that medicine sometimes, for there are sad 
things and terrible things all round us, pain and anguish, and decay. 
yet i suppose that even those call out the splendour of fortitude 
or endurance. even when one looks on a struggle which one know is 
hopeless, it warm the heart to see it.” 

the gleam that shone from her paled, her arms dropped, and she move 
on. then, soft of voice and soft of eye, she spoke again. 

“such a sad thing happen here two days ago,” she said. “a small 
girl--now what was her name? yes--ellen davenport--brought a note from 
the village up to the house. i was out, so she left it, and started, it 
is supposed, to go back home. she has not been seen since. description 
of her were circulate in all the village for mile round; but, as 
you heard at lunch, there has been no news of her, and the copse and 
covert in the park have been searched, but with no result. and yet out 
of that come splendour. i went to see her mother yesterday, bowed down 
with grief, but she won’t give up hope. ‘if it is god’s will,’ she said 
to me, ‘we shall find my ellen alive; and if we find her dead, it will 
be god’s will, too.’” 

she paused. 

“but i didn’t ask you down here to moan over tragedies,” she said. 
“i want you after all your week in town to come and have a 
spring-cleaning. doesn’t the wind take the dust out of you, like one of 
those sucking-machines which you put on to carpets? and the sun! make a 
sponge of yourself and soak it up till you’re dripping with it.” 

for a couple of miles, at the least, we kept along this high ridge of 
down, and the lark were spring from the grass, vocal with song 
uncongealed, as they aspire and sank again, drop at last dumb 
and spent with rapture. then we descended steeply, through the wood 
and glade of the park, past thicket of catkin sallows, and of 
willow with soft moleskin buttons, and in the hollow the daffodil 
were dancing, and the herb of the springtime were push up through 
the brittle wither stuff of the winter. then, passing along the one 
street of the red-tiled village, in which my companion pointed me out 
the house where the poor vanish girl had lived, we turn homewards 
across the grass and join the road again at the bottom of the great 
lake that lie below the terrace garden of the house. 

this lake was artificial, made a hundred years ago by the erection of a 
huge dam across the dip of the valley, so that the stream which flow 
down it was thereby confine and must needs form this sheet of water 
before it found outlet again through the sluices. at the centre the dam 
is some twenty-five feet in height, and by the side of the road which 
cross it clump of rhododendron lean out over the deep water. the 
margin on the side towards the lake is reinforce with concrete, now 
mossy and overgrown with herbage, and the face of it, burrow down 
to the level of the bottom of the dam through four fathom of dusky 
water. the lake was high and the overflow pour sonorous through the 
sluices, and the sun in the west made broken rainbow in the foam of 
its outpouring. 

as we pause there a moment, my companion seem the incarnation of the 
sight and sound that went to the spell of the spring; singing lark 
and dance daffodils, west wind and rain-bowed foam and, no less, the 
dark, deep water, were all distill into her radiant vitality. 

“and now for the house again,” she said, go brisk up the steep 
slope. “is it inhospitable of me to wish that no one was coming except, 
of course, our delightful archie? a houseful bring london into the 
country, and we shall talk scandal and stir up mud instead of watch 
miracles.” 

another faint memory of her linger somewhere in the dusk, and i 
grope for it, as one grope in slime for the roots of a water-plant, 
and pull it out. a notorious murder had been guillotine that 
morning in france, and in some sunday paper next day there was a 
brutal, brilliant, inexcusable little sketch of his being led out 
between guard for the final scene at dawn outside the prison at 
versailles. and, as i wrote my name in lady rorke’s visitors’ book 
on monday morning, i spilt a blot of ink on the page and hasty 
had recourse to the blotting-pad on her writing-table in order to 
minimize the disfigurement. inside it was this unpardonable picture, 
cut out and put away, and i thought of the thrush and the dentist’s 
waiting-room---- 

a month afterwards her husband died, after three week of intolerable 
torment. the doctor insist on his have two train nurses, but 
lady rorke never left him. she was present at the painful dressing 
of the wound from the operation that only prolonged the misery of his 
existence, and even slept on the sofa of the room where he lay. 

* * * * * 

archie rorke arrive that evening. he let me know at once that he had 
seen the announcement of lady rorke’s death, and said no more about 
it till later, when he and i were left alone over the fire in the 
smoking-room. he look round to see that the door was shut behind the 
last bedgoer of my little party, and then turn to me. 

“i’ve got to tell you something,” he said. “it’ll take half an hour, so 
to-morrow will do if you want to be off.” 

“but i don’t,” said i. 

he pull himself together from his sprawl sunken in his chair. 

“very well,” he said. “what i want to tell you is the story of the 
breaking-off of my engagement with sybil. i have often want to do so 
before, but while she was alive, as you will presently see, i could 
tell nobody. i shall ask you, when you know everything, whether you 
think i could have done otherwise. and please do not interrupt me till 
i have finished, unless there is something you don’t understand, for it 
won’t be very easy to get through with it. but i think i can make it 
intelligible.” 

he was silent a moment, and i saw his face working and twitching. 

“i must tell somebody,” he said, “and i choose you, unless you mind it 
awfully. but i simply can’t bear it alone any more.” 

“go on, then, old boy,” i said. “i’m glad you chose me, do you know. 
and i won’t interrupt.” 

archie spoke. 

“a week or two only before our marriage was to have taken place,” he 
said, “i went down to lincote for a couple of days. i had had the house 
done up and re-decorated, and now the work was finished and i want to 
see that all was in order. nothing could be worthy of sybil, but--well, 
you can guess, more or less, what my feelings were. 

“for a week before there had been very heavy rains, and the lake--you 
know it--below the garden was very high, higher than i had ever seen 
it: the water pour over the road across the dam which leads to the 
village. under the weight and press of it a great crack had appear 
in the concrete with which it is faced, and there was danger of the 
dam being carry away. if that happen the whole lake would have 
been sudden release and no end of damage might have been done. it 
was therefore necessary to draw off the water as fast as possible to 
relieve the pressure and repair the crack. this was done by means of 
big siphons. for two days we had them working, but the crack seem 
to extend right to the foundations of the dam, and before it could be 
repair all the water in the lake would have to be drawn off. i was 
just leave for town, when the foreman came up to the house to tell me 
that they had found something there. in the ooze and mud at the base 
of the dam, twenty-five feet below water-level, they had come upon the 
body of a young girl.” 

he grip the arms of his chair tight. little did he know that i was 
horrible aware of what he was go to tell me next. 

“about a month before my cousin ernest’s death,” he said, “a mysterious 
affair happen in the village. a girl name ellen davenport had 
disappeared. she came up one afternoon to the house with a note, 
and was never seen again, dead or alive. her disappearance was now 
explained. a chain of bead round the neck and various fragment of 
clothing established, beyond any doubt, the identity of what they had 
found at the bottom of the lake. i wait for the inquest, telegraph 
to sybil that business had detain me, and then return to town, not 
intend to tell her what that business was, for our marriage was 
close at hand and it was not a topic one would choose. she was very 
superstitious, you know, and i thought that it would shock her. that 
she would feel it to be unlucky and ill-omened. so i said nothing to 
her. 

“sybil had extraordinary mediumistic powers. she did not often exercise 
them and she never would give a séance to any one she did not know 
extremely well, for she believe that people brought with them the 
spiritual influence with which they were surrounded, and that there 
was the possibility of very evil intelligence being set free. but she 
had sat several times with me, and i had witness some very remarkable 
manifestations. her procedure was to put herself, by abstraction of 
her mind, into a state of trance, and spirits of the dead who were 
connected with the sitter could then communicate through her. on 
one occasion my mother, whom she had never seen, and who die many 
years ago, spoke through her and told me certain fact which sybil 
could not have known, and which i did not know. but an old friend of 
my mother’s, still alive, told me that they were correct. they were 
of an exceedingly private nature. sybil also, so she told me, could 
produce materialisations, but up till now i had never seen any. a 
remarkable thing about her mediumship was that she would sometimes 
regain consciousness from her trance while still these communications 
were being made, and she knew what was go on. she could hear herself 
speak and be mental aware of what she was saying. on the occasion, 
for instance, of which i have told you, when my mother spoke to me she 
was in this state. the same thing occur at the sitting of which i 
shall now speak. 

“that night, on my return to london, she and i dine alone. i felt a 
very strong desire, for which i could not account, that she should 
hold a sitting--just herself and me--and she consented. we sat in her 
room, with a shade lamp, but there was sufficient illumination for me 
to see her quite distinctly, for her face was towards the light. there 
was a small table in front of us cover with a dark cloth. she sat 
close to it, in a high chair, compose herself, and almost immediately 
went into trance. her head fell forward and by her slow breathing and 
her absolute immobile i knew she was unconscious. for a long time 
we sat there in silence, and i began to think that we should get no 
manifestation at all, and that the sitting, as sometimes was the case, 
would be a failure; but then i saw that something was happening.” 

his hands, with which he grip the arms of his chair, were trembling. 
twice he try to speak, but it was not till the third attempt that he 
master himself. 

“there was form a mist above the table,” he said. “it was slightly 
luminous and it spread upwards, pillar-shaped, in height between 
two and three feet. then i saw that below the outlying skein of it 
something was materialising. it mould itself into human shape, rising 
waist-high from the table, and presently shoulder and arms and neck 
and head were visible, and features began to outline themselves. for 
some time it remain vague and fluid, sway backwards and forward a 
little; then very quick it solidified, and there, close in front of 
me, was the half-figure of a young girl. the eyes were still closed, 
but now they opened. round her neck was a chain of bead just such as i 
had seen laid by the body that had been found in the lake. and then i 
spoke to her, ask her who she was, though i already knew. 

“her answer was no more than a whisper, but quite distinct. 

“‘ellen davenport,’ she said. 

“a disorder terror seize me. yet perhaps this little white figure, 
with its wide-gazing eyes, was some hallucination, something that had 
no objective existence at all. all day the thought of the poor kiddie 
whose remains i had seen taken out of the ooze at the bottom of the 
lake had been vivid in my mind, and i try to think that what i saw 
was no more than some strange projection of my thought. and yet i 
felt it was not so; it was independent of myself. and why was it made 
manifest, and on what errand had it come? i had pressed sybil to give 
me this séance, and god know what i would have given not to have done 
so! for one thing i was thankful, namely, that she was in unconscious 
trance. perhaps the phantom would fade again before she came out of it. 

“and then i heard a stir of movement from the chair where she sat, and, 
turning, i saw that she had raise her head. her eyes were open and on 
her face such a mask of terror as i have never known human being could 
wear. recognition was there, too; i saw that sybil knew who the phantom 
was. 

“the figure that pale gleam above the table turn its head towards 
her, and once more the white lip opened. 

“‘yes, i am ellen davenport,’ she said. 

“the whisper grew louder. 

“‘you might have save me,’ she said, ‘or you might have try to save 
me; but you watch me struggle till i sank.’ 

“and then the apparition vanished. it did not die away; it was there 
clear and distinct one moment, at the next it was gone. sybil and i 
were sitting alone in her room with the low-burning lamp, and the 
silence sang in my ears. 

“i got up and turn on the switch that kindle the electric lights, 
and knew that something within me had grown cold and that something 
had snapped. she still sat where she was, not look at me at all, 
but blank in front of her. she said no word of denial in answer to 
the terrible accusation that had been uttered. and i think i was glad 
of that, for there are times when it is not only futility to deny, but 
blasphemy. for my part, i could neither look at her nor speak to her. i 
remember holding out my hand to the empty grate, as if there had been 
a fire burning there. and standing there i heard her rise, and dreary 
wonder what she would say and knew how useless it would be. and then 
i heard the whisper of her dress on the carpet and the noise of the 
door opening and shutting, and when i turn i found that i was alone 
in the room. presently i let myself out of the house.”
