there was a long pause, but i did not break it, for i felt he had not 
quite finished. 

“i had love her with my whole heart,” he said, “and she knew it. 
perhaps that was why i never attempt to see her again and why she did 
not attempt to see me. that little white figure would always have been 
with us, for she could not deny the reality of it and the truth of that 
which it had spoken. that’s my story, then. you needn’t even tell me if 
you think i could have done differently, for i knew i couldn’t. and she 
couldn’t.” 

he rose. 

“i see there is to be an inquest,” he said. “i hope they will find 
that she kill herself. it will mean, won’t it, that her remorse was 
unbearable. and that’s atonement.” 

he move towards the door. 

“inscrutable decrees,” he said. 




the garden 




the garden 


two friends of mine, hugh grainger and his wife, had taken for a 
month of christmas holiday the house in which we were to witness such 
strange manifestations, and when i received an invitation from them to 
spend a fortnight there i return them an enthusiast affirmative. 
well already did i know that pleasant heathery country-side, and most 
intimate was my acquaintance with the subtle hazard of its most 
charming golf-links. golf, i was given to understand, was to occupy the 
solid day for hugh and me, so that margaret should never be oblige to 
set her hand to the implement with which the game, so detest to 
her, was conducted.... 

i arrive there while yet the daylight lingered, and as my host were 
out, i took a ramble round the place. the house and garden stood on a 
plateau facing south; below it were a couple of acre of pasture that 
slope down to a vagrant stream cross by a foot-bridge, by the side 
of which stood a thatch cottage with a vegetable patch surrounding 
it. a path ran close past this across the pasture from a wicket-gate in 
the garden, conduct you over the foot-bridge, and, so my remember 
sense of geography told me, must constitute a short cut to the links 
that lay not half a mile beyond. the cottage itself was clearly on 
the land of the little estate, and i at once supposed it to be the 
gardener’s house. what went against so obvious and simple a theory was 
that it appear to be untenanted. no wreath of smoke, though the 
evening was chilly, curl from its chimneys, and, coming closer, i 
fancy it had that air of “waiting” about it which we so often conjure 
into unused habitations. there it stood, with no sign of life whatever 
about it, though ready, as its apparently perfect state of repair 
seem to warrant, for fresh tenant to put the breath of life into it 
again. its little garden, too, though the palings were neat and newly 
painted, told the same tale; the bed were untended and unweeded, and 
in the flower-border by the front door was a row of chrysanthemums, 
which had wither on their stems. but all this was but the impression 
of a moment, and i did not pause as i pass it, but cross the 
foot-bridge and went on up the heathery slope that lay beyond. my 
geography was not at fault, for presently i saw the club-house just 
in front of me. hugh no doubt would be just about coming in from his 
afternoon round, and so we would walk back together. on reach the 
club-house, however, the steward told me that not five minutes before 
mrs. grainger had call in her car for her husband, and i therefore 
retrace my steps by the path along which i had already come. but 
i made a detour, as a golf will, to walk up the fairway of the 
seventeenth and eighteenth hole just for the pleasure of recognition, 
and look respectful at the yawn sandpit which so inexorable 
guard the eighteenth green, wonder in what circumstances i should 
visit it next, whether with a step complacent and superior, knowing 
that my ball repose safe on the green beyond, or with the heavy 
footfall of one who know that laborious delve lie before him. 

the light of the winter evening had fade fast, and when i cross 
the foot-bridge on my return the dusk had gathered. to my right, just 
beside the path, lay the cottage, the whitewash wall of which 
gleam white in the gloaming; and as i turn my glance back from 
it to the rather narrow plank which bridge the stream i thought i 
caught out of the tail of my eye some light from one of its windows, 
which thus disprove my theory that it was untenanted. but when i 
look directly at it again i saw that i was mistaken: some reflection 
in the glass of the red lines of sunset in the west must have deceive 
me, for in the inclement twilight it look more desolate than ever. 
yet i linger by the wicket gate in its low palings, for though all 
exterior evidence bore witness to its emptiness, some inexplicable 
feeling assured me, quite irrationally, that this was not so, and that 
there was somebody there. certainly there was nobody visible, but, so 
this absurd idea informed me, he might be at the back of the cottage 
conceal from me by the intervene structure, and, still oddly, still 
unreasonably, it became a matter of importance to my mind to ascertain 
whether this was so or not, so clearly had my perception told me that 
the place was empty, and so firm had some conviction assured me 
that it was tenanted. to cover my inquisitiveness, in case there was 
someone there, i could inquire whether this path was a short cut to 
the house at which i was staying, and, rather rebel at what i was 
doing, i went through the small garden, and rap at the door. there 
was no answer, and, after wait for a response to a second summons, 
and have try the door and found it locked, i made the circuit of 
the house. of course there was no one there, and i told myself that i 
was just like a man who looks under his bed for a burglar and would be 
beyond measure astonish if he found one. 

my host were at the house when i arrived, and we spent a cheerful two 
hour before dinner in such desultory and eager conversation as is 
proper between friends who have not met for some time. between hugh 
grainger and his wife it is always impossible to light on a subject 
which do not vivid interest one or other of them, and golf, 
politics, the needs of russia, cooking, ghosts, the possible victory 
over mount everest, and the income tax were among the topic which we 
passionately discussed. with all these plate spinning, it was easy 
to whip up any one of them, and the subject of spook generally was 
light upon again and again. 

“margaret is on the high road to madness,” remark hugh on one of 
these occasions, “for she has begun use planchette. if you use 
planchette for six months, i am told, most careful doctor will 
conscientious certify you as insane. she’s got five month more 
before she go to bedlam.” 

“does it work?” i asked. 

“yes, it say most interesting things,” said margaret. “it say things 
that never enter my head. we’ll try it to-night.” 

“oh, not to-night,” said hugh. “let’s have an evening off.” 

margaret disregard this. 

“it’s no use ask planchette questions,” she went on, “because there 
is in your mind some sort of answer to them. if i ask whether it will 
be fine to-morrow, for instance, it is probably i--though indeed i 
don’t mean to push--who make the pencil say ‘yes.’” 

“and then it usually rains,” remark hugh. 

“not always: don’t interrupt. the interesting thing is to let the 
pencil write what it chooses. very often it only make loop and 
curves--though they may mean something--and every now and then a word 
comes, of the significance of which i have no idea whatever, so i 
clearly couldn’t have suggest it. yesterday evening, for instance, 
it wrote ‘gardener’ over and over again. now what did that mean? the 
garden here is a methodist with a chin-beard. could it have meant 
him? oh, it’s time to dress. please don’t be late, my cook is so 
sensitive about soup.” 

we rose, and some connection of idea about “gardener” link itself up 
in my mind. 

“by the way, what’s that cottage in the field by the foot-bridge?” i 
asked. “is that the gardener’s cottage?” 

“it use to be,” said hugh. “but the chin-beard doesn’t live there: in 
fact nobody lives there. it’s empty. if i was owner here, i should put 
the chin-beard into it, and take the rent off his wages. some people 
have no idea of economy. why did you ask?” 

i saw margaret was look at me rather attentively. 

“curiosity,” i said. “idle curiosity.” 

“i don’t believe it was,” said she. 

“but it was,” i said. “it was idle curiosity to know whether the 
house was inhabited. as i pass it, go down to the club-house, i 
felt sure it was empty, but coming back i felt so sure that there was 
someone there that i rap at the door, and indeed walk round it.” 

hugh had precede us upstairs, as she linger a little. 

“and there was no one there?” she asked. “it’s odd: i had just the same 
feeling as you about it.” 

“that explain planchette writing ‘gardener’ over and over again,” said 
i. “you had the gardener’s cottage on your mind.” 

“how ingenious!” said margaret. “hurry up and dress.” 

a gleam of strong moonlight between my drawn curtains when i went up 
to bed that night led me to look out. my room face the garden and 
the field which i had traverse that afternoon, and all was vivid 
illuminate by the full moon. the thatch cottage with its white wall 
close by the stream was very distinct, and once more, i suppose, the 
reflection of the light on the glass of one of its window made it 
appear that the room was lit within. it struck me as odd that twice 
that day this illusion should have been present to me, but now a yet 
odd thing happened. even as i look the light was extinguished. 

the morning did not at all bear out the fine promise of the clear 
night, for when i woke the wind was squealing, and sheet of rain from 
the south-west were dashed against my panes. golf was wholly out of the 
question, and, though the violence of the storm abate a little in the 
afternoon, the rain drip with a steady sullenness. but i weary 
of indoors, and, since the two other entire refuse to set foot 
outside, i went forth mackintosh to get a breath of air. by way of 
an object in my tramp, i took the road to the links in preference to 
the muddy short cut through the fields, with the intention of engaging 
a couple of caddie for hugh and myself next morning, and linger 
awhile over illustrate papers in the smoking-room. i must have read 
for long than i knew, for a sudden beam of sunset light sudden 
illuminate my page, and look up, i saw that the rain had ceased, 
and that evening was fast coming on. so instead of taking the long 
detour by the road again, i set forth homewards by the path across 
the fields. that gleam of sunset was the last of the day, and once 
again, just as twenty-four hour ago, i cross the foot-bridge in the 
gloaming. till that moment, as far as i was aware, i had not thought 
at all about the cottage there, but now in a flash the light i had 
seen there last night, sudden extinguished, recall itself to my 
mind, and at the same moment i felt that invincible conviction that 
the cottage was tenanted. simultaneous in these swift process of 
thought i look towards it, and saw standing by the door the figure of 
a man. in the dusk i could distinguish nothing of his face, if indeed 
it was turn to me, and only got the impression of a tallish fellow, 
thick built. he open the door, from which there came a dim light as 
of a lamp, entered, and shut it after him. 

so then my conviction was right. yet i had been distinct told that 
the cottage was empty: who, then, was he that enter as if return 
home? once more, this time with a certain qualm of fear, i rap on 
the door, intend to put some trivial question; and rap again, 
this time more drastically, so that there could be no question that my 
summons was unheard. but still i got no reply, and finally i try the 
handle of the door. it was locked. then, with difficulty master an 
increase terror, i made the circuit of the cottage, peer into each 
unshuttered window. all was dark within, though but two minutes ago i 
had seen the gleam of light escape from the open door. 

just because some chain of conjecture was beginning to form itself in 
my mind, i made no allusion to this odd adventure, and after dinner 
margaret, amid protest from hugh, got out the planchette which had 
persist in writing “gardener.” my surmise was, of course, utterly 
fantastic, but i want to convey no suggestion of any sort to 
margaret.... for a long time the pencil skate over her paper making 
loop and curve and peak like a temperature chart, and she had begun 
to yawn and weary over her experiment before any coherent word emerged. 
and then, in the oddest way, her head nod forward and she seem to 
have fallen asleep. 

hugh look up from his book and spoke in a whisper to me. 

“she fell asleep the other night over it,” he said. 

margaret’s eyes were closed, and she breathe the long, quiet breath 
of slumber, and then her hand began to move with a curious firmness. 
right across the big sheet of paper went a level line of writing, and 
at the end her hand stop with a jerk, and she woke. 

she look at the paper. 

“hullo,” she said. “ah, one of you has been play a trick on me!” 

we assured her that this was not so, and she read what she had written. 

“gardener, gardener,” it ran. “i am the gardener. i want to come in. i 
can’t find her here.” 

“o lord, that garden again!” said hugh. 

look up from the paper, i saw margaret’s eyes fixed on mine, and 
even before she spoke i knew what her thought was. 

“did you come home by the empty cottage?” she asked. 

“yes: why?” 

“still empty?” she said in a low voice. “or--or anything else?” 

i did not want to tell her just what i had seen--or what, at any rate, 
i thought i had seen. if there was go to be anything odd, anything 
worth observation, it was far better that our respective impression 
should not fortify each other. 

“i tap again, and there was no answer,” i said. 

presently there was a move to bed: margaret initiate it, and after she 
had gone upstairs hugh and i went to the front door to interrogate the 
weather. once more the moon shone in a clear sky, and we stroll out 
along the flag path that front the house. sudden hugh turn 
quick and pointed to the angle of the house. 

“who on earth is that?” he said. “look! there! he has gone round the 
corner.” 

i had but the glimpse of a tallish man of heavy build. 

“didn’t you see him?” ask hugh. “i’ll just go round the house, and 
find him; i don’t want anyone prowl round us at night. wait here, 
will you, and if he come round the other corner ask him what his 
business is.” 

hugh had left me, in our stroll, close by the front door which was 
open, and there i wait until he should have made his circuit. he had 
hardly disappear when i heard, quite distinctly, a rather quick but 
heavy footfall coming along the paved walk towards me from the opposite 
direction. but there was absolutely no one to be seen who made this 
sound of rapid walking. close and close to me came the steps of the 
invisible one, and then with a shudder of horror i felt somebody unseen 
push by me as i stood on the threshold. that shudder was not merely of 
the spirit, for the touch of him was that of ice on my hand. i try to 
seize this impalpable intruder, but he slip from me, and next moment 
i heard his steps on the parquet of the floor inside. some door within 
open and shut, and i heard no more of him. next moment hugh came 
running round the corner of the house from which the sound of steps had 
approached. 

“but where is he?” he asked. “he was not twenty yard in front of me--a 
big, tall fellow.” 

“i saw nobody,” i said. “i heard his step along the walk, but there was 
nothing to be seen.” 

“and then?” ask hugh. 

“whatever it was seem to brush by me, and go into the house,” said i. 

there had certainly been no sound of steps on the bare oak stairs, and 
we search room after room through the ground floor of the house. the 
dining-room door and that of the smoking-room were locked, that into 
the drawing-room was open, and the only other door which could have 
furnish the impression of an opening and a shut was that into the 
kitchen and servants’ quarters. here again our quest was fruitless; 
through pantry and scullery and boot-room and servants’ hall we 
searched, but all was empty and quiet. finally we came to the kitchen, 
which too was empty. but by the fire there was set a rocking-chair, and 
this was oscillate to and fro as if someone, lately sitting there, 
had just quit it. there it stood gently rocking, and this seem to 
convey the sense of a presence, invisible now, more than even the sight 
of him who surely had been sitting there could have done. i remember 
wanting to steady it and stop it, and yet my hand refuse to go forth 
to it. 

what we had seen, and in especial what we had not seen, would have been 
sufficient to furnish most people with a broken night, and assured i 
was not among the strong-minded exceptions. long i lay wide-eyed and 
open-eared, and when at last i doze i was pluck from the borderland 
of sleep by the sound, muffle but unmistakable, of someone moving 
about the house. it occur to me that the steps might be those of 
hugh conduct a lonely exploration, but even while i wonder a tap 
came at the door of communication between our rooms, and, in answer to 
my response, it appear that he had come to see whether it was i thus 
uneasy wandering. even as we spoke the step pass my door, and the 
stairs leading to the floor above creak to its ascent. next moment 
it sound directly above our head in some attic in the roof. 

“those are not the servants’ bedrooms,” said hugh. “no one sleep 
there. let us look once more: it must be somebody.” 

with lit candle we made our stealthy way upstairs, and just when we 
were at the top of the flight, hugh, a step ahead of me, utter a 
sharp exclamation. 

“but something is passing by me!” he said, and he clutch at the empty 
air. even as he spoke, i experienced the same sensation, and the moment 
afterwards the stairs below us creak again, as the unseen pass down. 

all night long that sound of steps move about the passages, as if 
someone was searching the house, and as i lay and listen that message 
which had come through the pencil of the planchette to margaret’s 
finger occur to me. “i want to come in. i cannot find her here.”... 
indeed someone had come in, and was sedulous in his search. he was the 
gardener, it would seem. but what garden was this invisible seeker, 
and for whom did he seek? 

even as when some bodily pain cease it is difficult to recall with 
any vivid what the pain was like, so next morning, as i dressed, i 
found myself vain try to recapture the horror of the spirit which 
had accompany these nocturnal adventures. i remember that something 
within me had sicken as i watch the movements of the rocking-chair 
the night before and as i heard the steps along the paved way outside, 
and by that invisible pressure against me knew that someone had enter 
the house. but now in the sane and tranquil morning, and all day 
under the serene winter sun, i could not realise what it had been. the 
presence, like the bodily pain, had to be there for the realisation of 
it, and all day it was absent. hugh felt the same; he was even disposed 
to be humorous on the subject. 

“well, he’s had a good look,” he said, “whoever he is, and whomever 
he was look for. by the way, not a word to margaret, please. she 
heard nothing of these perambulations, nor of the entry of--of whatever 
it was. not gardener, anyhow: who ever heard of a garden spend 
his time walking about the house? if there were steps all over the 
potato-patch, i might have been with you.” 

margaret had arrange to drive over to have tea with some friends of 
hers that afternoon, and in consequence hugh and i refresh ourselves 
at the club-house after our game, and it was already dusk when for the 
third day in succession i pass homewards by the whitewash cottage. 
but to-night i had no sense of it being subtle occupied; it stood 
mournful desolate, as is the way of untenanted houses, and no light 
nor semblance of such gleam from its windows. hugh, to whom i had 
told the odd impression i had received there, gave them a reception as 
flippant as that which he had accord to the memory of the night, 
and he was still being humorous about them when we came to the door of 
the house. 

“a psychic disturbance, old boy,” he said. “like a cold in the head. 
hullo, the door’s locked.” 

he rang and rapped, and from inside came the noise of a turn key and 
withdrawn bolts. 

“what’s the door lock for?” he ask his servant who open it. 

the man shift from one foot to the other. 

“the bell rang half an hour ago, sir,” he said, “and when i came to 
answer it there was a man standing outside, and----” 

“well?” ask hugh. 

“i didn’t like the looks of him, sir,” he said, “and i ask him his 
business. he didn’t say anything, and then he must have gone pretty 
smart away, for i never saw him go.” 

“where did he seem to go?” ask hugh, glancing at me. 

“i can’t rightly say, sir. he didn’t seem to go at all. something 
seem to brush by me.” 

“that’ll do,” said hugh rather sharply. 

* * * * * 

margaret had not come in from her visit, but when soon after the crunch 
of the motor wheels was heard hugh reiterate his wish that nothing 
should be said to her about the impression which now, apparently, a 
third person share with us. she came in with a flush of excitement on 
her face. 

“never laugh at my planchette again,” she said. “i’ve heard the most 
extraordinary story from maud ashfield--horrible, but so frightfully 
interesting.” 

“out with it,” said hugh. 

“well, there was a garden here,” she said. “he use to live at that 
little cottage by the foot-bridge, and when the family were up in 
london he and his wife use to be caretaker and live here.” 

hugh’s glance and mine met: then he turn away. i knew, as certainly 
as if i was in his mind, that his thought were identical with my own. 

“he married a wife much younger than himself,” continue margaret, “and 
gradual he became frightfully jealous of her. and one day in a fit 
of passion he strangle her with his own hands. a little while after 
someone came to the cottage, and found him sob over her, try to 
restore her. they went for the police, but before they came he had cut 
his own throat. isn’t it all horrible? but surely it’s rather curious 
that the planchette said gardener. ‘i am the gardener. i want to come 
in. i can’t find her here.’ you see i knew nothing about it. i shall do 
planchette again to-night. oh dear me, the post go in half an hour, 
and i have a whole budget to send. but respect my planchette for the 
future, hughie.” 

we talk the situation out when she had gone, but hugh, unwilling 
convinced and yet unwill to admit that something more than 
coincidence lay behind that “planchette nonsense,” still insist that 
margaret should be told nothing of what we had heard and seen in the 
house last night, and of the strange visitor who again this evening, so 
we must conclude, had made his entry. 

“she’ll be frightened,” he said, “and she’ll begin imagine things. as 
for the planchette, as likely as not it will do nothing but scribble 
and make loops. what’s that? yes: come in!” 

there had come from somewhere in the room one sharp, peremptory rap. i 
did not think it came from the door, but hugh, when no response reply 
to his words of admittance, jump up and open it. he took a few 
steps into the hall outside, and returned. 

“didn’t you hear it?” he asked. 

“certainly. no one there?” 

“not a soul.” 

hugh came back to the fireplace and rather irritable threw a cigarette 
which he had just lit into the fender. 

“that was rather a nasty jar,” he observed; “and if you ask me whether 
i feel comfortable, i can tell you i never felt less comfortable in my 
life. i’m frightened, if you want to know, and i believe you are too.” 

i hadn’t the smallest intention of deny this, and he went on. 

“we’ve got to keep a hand on ourselves,” he said. “there’s nothing so 
infectious as fear, and margaret mustn’t catch it from us. but there’s 
something more than our fear, you know. something has got into the 
house and we’re up against it. i never believe in such things before. 
let’s face it for a minute. _what_ is it anyhow?” 

“if you want to know what i think it is,” said i, “i believe it to be 
the spirit of the man who strangle his wife and then cut his throat. 
but i don’t see how it can hurt us. we’re afraid of our own fear 
really.” 

“but we’re up against it,” said hugh. “and what will it do? good 
lord, if i only knew what it would do i shouldn’t mind. it’s the not 
knowing.... well, it’s time to dress.” 

* * * * * 

margaret was in her highest spirits at dinner. knowing nothing of 
the manifestation of that presence which had taken place in the 
last twenty-four hours, she thought it absorbing interesting that 
her planchette should have “guessed” (so ran her phrase) about the 
gardener, and from that topic she flit to an equally interesting 
form of patience for three which her friend had show her, promising 
to initiate us into it after dinner. this she did, and, not knowing 
that we both above all things want to keep planchette at a distance, 
she was delight with the success of her game. but sudden she 
observe that the evening was burning rapid away, and swept the cards 
together at the conclusion of a hand. 

“now just half an hour of planchette,” she said. 

“oh, mayn’t we play one more hand?” ask hugh. “it’s the best game 
i’ve seen for years. planchette will be dismal slow after this.” 

“darling, if the garden will only communicate again, it won’t be 
slow,” said she. 

“but it is such drivel,” said hugh. 

“how rude you are! read your book, then.” 

margaret had already got out her machine and a sheet of paper, when 
hugh rose. 

“please don’t do it to-night, margaret,” he said. 

“but why? you needn’t attend.” 

“well, i ask you not to, anyhow,” said he. 

margaret look at him closely. 

“hughie, you’ve got something on your mind,” she said. “out with it. i 
believe you’re nervous. you think there is something queer about. what 
is it?” 

i could see hugh hesitate as to whether to tell her or not, and i 
gather that he chose the chance of her planchette inane scribbling. 

“go on, then,” he said. 

margaret hesitated: she clearly did not want to vex hugh, but his 
insistence must have seem to her most unreasonable. 

“well, just ten minutes,” she said, “and i promise not to think of 
gardeners.” 

she had hardly laid her hand on the board when her head fell forward, 
and the machine began moving. i was sitting close to her, and as it 
roll steady along the paper the writing became visible. 

“i have come in,” it ran, “but still i can’t find her. are you hiding 
her? i will search the room where you are.” 

what else was written but still conceal underneath the planchette i 
did not know, for at that moment a current of icy air swept round the 
room, and at the door, this time unmistakably, came a loud, peremptory 
knock. hugh sprang to his feet. 

“margaret, wake up,” he said, “something is coming!” 

the door opened, and there move in the figure of a man. he stood just 
within the door, his head bent forward, and he turn it from side to 
side, peering, it would seem, with eyes staring and infinite sad, 
into every corner of the room. 

“margaret, margaret,” cry hugh again. 

but margaret’s eyes were open too; they were fixed on this dreadful 
visitor. 

“be quiet, hughie,” she said below her breath, rising as she spoke. the 
ghost was now look directly at her. once the lip above the thick, 
rust-coloured beard moved, but no sound came forth, the mouth only 
move and slavered. he raise his head, and, horror upon horror, i saw 
that one side of his neck was laid open in a red, glisten gash.... 

for how long that pause continued, when we all three stood stiff and 
frozen in some deadly inhibition to move or speak, i have no idea: i 
suppose that at the utmost it was a dozen seconds. then the spectre 
turned, and went out as it had come. we heard his steps pass along the 
parquet floor; there was the sound of bolt withdrawn from the front 
door, and with a crash that shook the house it slam to. 

“it’s all over,” said margaret. “god have mercy on him!” 

* * * * * 

now the reader may put precisely what construction he please on 
this visitation from the dead. he need not, indeed, consider it to 
have been a visitation from the dead at all, but say that there had 
been impress on the scene, where this murder and suicide happened, 
some sort of emotional record, which in certain circumstances could 
translate itself into image visible and invisible. wave of ether, 
or what not, may conceivable retain the impress of such scenes; they 
may be held, so to speak, in solution, ready to be precipitated. 
or he may hold that the spirit of the dead man indeed made itself 
manifest, revisite in some sort of spiritual penance and remorse 
the place where his crime was committed. naturally, no materialist 
will entertain such an explanation for an instant, but then there is 
no one so obstinate unreasonable as the materialist. beyond doubt 
a dreadful deed was done there, and margaret’s last utterance is not 
inapplicable. 




mr. tilly’s séance 




mr. tilly’s séance 


mr. til had only the briefest moment for reflection, when, as he 
slip and fell on the greasy wood pavement at hyde park corner, which 
he was crossing at a smart trot, he saw the huge traction-engine with 
its groove ponderous wheels towering high above him. 

“oh, dear! oh, dear!” he said petulantly, “it will certainly crush me 
quite flat, and i shan’t be able to be at mrs. cumberbatch’s séance! 
most provoking! a-ow!” 

the words were hardly out of his mouth, when the first half of his 
horrid anticipation was thorough fulfilled. the heavy wheels pass 
over him from head to foot and flatten him completely out. then the 
driver (too late) reverse his engine and pass over him again, and 
finally lost his head, whistle loud and stopped. the policeman on 
duty at the corner turn quite faint at the sight of the catastrophe, 
but presently recover sufficient to hold up the traffic, and ran to 
see what on earth could be done. it was all so much “up” with mr. til 
that the only thing possible was to get the hysterical engine-driver 
to move clear. then the ambulance from the hospital was sent for, and 
mr. tilly’s remains, detached with great difficulty from the road (so 
firm had they been pressed into it), were reverent carry away 
into the mortuary.... 

mr. til during this had experienced one moment’s excruciating pain, 
resemble the severest neuralgia as his head was ground beneath the 
wheel, but almost before he realise it, the pain was past, and he 
found himself, still rather dazed, floating or standing (he did not 
know which) in the middle of the road. there had been no break in 
his consciousness; he perfectly recollect slipping, and wonder 
how he had manage to save himself. he saw the arrest traffic, the 
policeman with white wan face making suggestion to the gibber 
engine-driver, and he received the very puzzle impression that the 
traction engine was all mixed up with him. he had a sensation of 
red-hot coal and boil water and rivet all around him, but yet no 
feeling of scalding or burning or confinement. he was, on the contrary, 
extremely comfortable, and had the most pleasant consciousness of 
buoyancy and freedom. then the engine puffed and the wheels went round, 
and immediately, to his immense surprise, he perceive his own crush 
remains, flat as a biscuit, lie on the roadway. he identify them 
for certain by his clothes, which he had put on for the first time that 
morning, and one patent leather boot which had escape demolition. 

“but what on earth has happened?” he said. “here am i, and yet that 
poor pressed flower of arms and leg is me--or rather i--also. and how 
terribly upset the driver looks. why, i do believe that i’ve been run 
over! it did hurt for a moment, now i come to think of it.... my good 
man, where are you shove to? don’t you see me?” 

he address these two question to the policeman, who appear to walk 
right through him. but the man took no notice, and calm came out 
on the other side: it was quite evident that he did not see him, or 
apprehend him in any way. 

mr. til was still feeling rather at sea amid these unusual 
occurrences, and there began to steal into his mind a glimpse of the 
fact which was so obvious to the crowd which form an interested but 
respectful ring round his body. men stood with bare heads; women 
scream and look away and look back again. 

“i really believe i’m dead,” said he. “that’s the only hypothesis which 
will cover the facts. but i must feel more certain of it before i do 
anything. ah! here they come with the ambulance to look at me. i must 
be terribly hurt, and yet i don’t feel hurt. i should feel hurt surely 
if i was hurt. i must be dead.” 

certainly it seem the only thing for him to be, but he was far from 
realise it yet. a lane had been made through the crowd for the 
stretcher-bearers, and he found himself wince when they began to 
detach him from the road. 

“oh, do take care!” he said. “that’s the sciatic nerve protrude there 
surely, isn’t it? a-ow! no, it didn’t hurt after all. my new clothes, 
too: i put them on to-day for the first time. what bad luck! now you’re 
holding my leg upside down. of course all my money come out of my 
trouser pocket. and there’s my ticket for the séance; i must have that: 
i may use it after all.” 

he tweak it out of the finger of the man who had picked it up, and 
laugh to see the expression of amaze on his face as the card 
sudden vanished. that gave him something fresh to think about, and 
he ponder for a moment over some touch of association set up by it. 

“i have it,” he thought. “it is clear that the moment i came into 
connection with that card, it became invisible. i’m invisible myself 
(of course to the gross sense), and everything i hold become 
invisible. most interesting! that account for the sudden appearances 
of small object at a séance. the spirit has been holding them, and as 
long as he hold them they are invisible. then he let go, and there’s 
the flower or the spirit-photograph on the table. it accounts, too, for 
the sudden disappearance of such objects. the spirit has taken them, 
though the scoff say that the medium has secrete them about his 
person. it is true that when search he sometimes appear to have done 
so; but, after all, that may be a joke on the part of the spirit. now, 
what am i to do with myself.... let me see, there’s the clock. it’s 
just half-past ten. all this has happen in a few minutes, for it was 
a quarter past when i left my house. half-past ten now: what do that 
mean exactly? i use to know what it meant, but now it seem nonsense. 
ten what? hours, is it? what’s an hour?” 

this was very puzzling. he felt that he use to know what an hour and a 
minute meant, but the perception of that, naturally enough, had cease 
with his emergence from time and space into eternity. the conception 
of time was like some memory which, refuse to record itself on the 
consciousness, lie perdu in some dark corner of the brain, laugh at 
the effort of the owner to ferret it out. while he still interrogate 
his mind over this lapsed perception, he found that space as well as 
time, had similarly grown obsolete for him, for he caught sight of his 
friend miss ida soulsby, whom he knew was to be present at the séance 
for which he was bound, hurry with bird-like steps down the pavement 
opposite. forget for the moment that he was a disembodied spirit, 
he made the effort of will which in his past human existence would have 
set his leg in pursuit of her, and found that the effort of will alone 
was enough to place him at her side. 

“my dear miss soulsby,” he said, “i was on my way to mrs. cumberbatch’s 
house when i was knock down and killed. it was far from unpleasant, a 
moment’s headache----” 

so far his natural voluble had carry him before he recollect 
that he was invisible and inaudible to those still closed in by the 
muddy vesture of decay, and stop short. but though it was clear 
that what he said was inaudible to miss soulsby’s rather large 
intelligent-looking ears, it seem that some consciousness of his 
presence was convey to her fine sense, for she look sudden 
startled, a flush rose to her face, and he heard her murmur, “very odd. 
i wonder why i received so vivid an impression of dear teddy.” 

that gave mr. til a pleasant shock. he had long admire the lady, 
and here she was allude to him in her supposed privacy as “dear 
teddy.” that was follow by a momentary regret that he had been 
killed: he would have like to have been possessed of this information 
before, and have pursue the primrose path of dalliance down which it 
seem to lead. (his intentions, of course, would, as always, have 
been strict honourable: the path of dalliance would have conduct 
them both, if she consented, to the altar, where the primrose would 
have been exchange for orange blossom.) but his regret was quite 
short-lived; though the altar seem inaccessible, the primrose path 
might still be open, for many of the spiritualistic circle in which he 
live were on most affectionate terms with their spiritual guide and 
friends who, like himself, had pass over. from a human point of view 
these innocent and even elevate flirtation had always seem to him 
rather bloodless; but now, look on them from the far side, he saw 
how charming they were, for they gave him the sense of still have 
a place and an identity in the world he had just quitted. he pressed 
miss ida’s hand (or rather put himself into the spiritual condition of 
so doing), and could vague feel that it had some hint of warmth and 
solidity about it. this was gratifying, for it show that though he 
had pass out of the material plane, he could still be in touch with 
it. still more gratifying was it to observe that a pleased and secret 
smile overspread miss ida’s fine features as he gave this token of his 
presence: perhaps she only smile at her own thoughts, but in any case 
it was he who had inspired them. encourage by this, he indulge in 
a slightly more intimate token of affection, and permit himself a 
respectful salute, and saw that he had gone too far, for she said to 
herself, “hush, hush!” and quicken her pace, as if to leave these 
amorous thought behind. 

he felt that he was beginning to adjust himself to the new conditions 
in which he would now live, or, at any rate, was get some sort 
of inkling as to what they were. time exist no more for him, nor 
yet did space, since the wish to be at miss ida’s side had instantly 
transport him there, and with a view to test this further he 
wish himself back in his flat. as swift as the change of scene in 
a cinematograph show he found himself there, and perceive that the 
news of his death must have reach his servants, for his cook and 
parlour-maid with excited faces, were talk over the event. 

“poor little gentleman,” said his cook. “it seem a shame it does. he 
never hurt a fly, and to think of one of those great engine lay him 
out flat. i hope they’ll take him to the cemetery from the hospital: i 
never could bear a corpse in the house.” 

the great strapping parlour-maid toss her head. 

“well, i’m not sure that it doesn’t serve him right,” she observed. 
“always mess about with spirits he was, and the knock and 
concertina was awful sometimes when i’ve been lay out supper in 
the dining-room. now perhaps he’ll come himself and visit the rest of 
the loonies. but i’m sorry all the same. a less troublesome little 
gentleman never stepped. always pleasant, too, and wages paid to the 
day.” 

these regretful comment and encomium were something of a shock to mr. 
tilly. he had imagine that his excellent servant regard him with a 
respectful affection, as befit some sort of demigod, and the rôle of 
the poor little gentleman was not at all to his mind. this revelation 
of their true estimate of him, although what they thought of him could 
no long have the smallest significance, irritate him profoundly. 

“i never heard such impertinence,” he said (so he thought) quite out 
loud, and still intense earth-bound, was astonish to see that 
they had no perception whatever of his presence. he raise his voice, 
replete with extreme irony, and address his cook. 

“you may reserve your criticism on my character for your saucepans,” he 
said. “they will no doubt appreciate them. as regards the arrangement 
for my funeral, i have already provided for them in my will, and do not 
propose to consult your convenience. at present----” 

“lor’!” said mrs. inglis, “i declare i can almost hear his voice, poor 
little fellow. husky it was, as if he would do better by clearing his 
throat. i suppose i’d best be making a black bow to my cap. his lawyer 
and what not will be here presently.” 

mr. til had no sympathy with this suggestion. he was immensely 
conscious of being quite alive, and the idea of his servant behave 
as if he were dead, especially after the way in which they had spoken 
about him, was very vexing. he want to give them some striking 
evidence of his presence and his activity, and he bang his hand 
angry on the dining-room table, from which the breakfast equipage 
had not yet been cleared. three tremendous blow he gave it, and was 
rejoice to see that his parlour-maid look startled. mrs. inglis’s 
face remain perfectly placid. 

“why, if i didn’t hear a sort of rap sound,” said miss talton. 
“where did it come from?” 

“nonsense! you’ve the jumps, dear,” said mrs. inglis, pick up a 
remain rasher of bacon on a fork, and putt it into her capacious 
mouth. 

mr. til was delight at making any impression at all on either of 
these impercipient females. 

“talton!” he call at the top of his voice. 

“why, what’s that?” said talton. “almost hear his voice, do you say, 
mrs. inglis? i declare i did hear his voice then.” 

“a pack o’ nonsense, dear,” said mrs. ingli placidly. “that’s a prime 
bit of bacon, and there’s a good cut of it left. why, you’re all of a 
tremble! it’s your imagination.” 

sudden it struck mr. til that he might be employ himself much 
better than, with such extreme exertion, manage to convey so slight 
a hint of his presence to his parlour-maid, and that the séance at 
the house of the medium, mrs. cumberbatch, would afford him much 
easy opportunity of get through to the earth-plane again. he 
gave a couple more thump to the table and, wish himself at mrs. 
cumberbatch’s, nearly a mile away, scarcely heard the faint scream 
of talton at the sound of his blow before he found himself in west 
norfolk street. 

he knew the house well, and went straight to the drawing-room, which 
was the scene of the séances he had so often and so eager attended. 
mrs. cumberbatch, who had a long spoon-shaped face, had already pull 
down the blinds, leave the room in total dark except for the 
glimmer of the night-light which, under a shade of ruby-glass, stood 
on the chimney-piece in front of the coloured photograph of cardinal 
newman. round the table were seat miss ida soulsby, mr. and mrs. 
meriott (who paid their guinea at least twice a week in order to 
consult their spiritual guide abibel and received mysterious advice 
about their indigestion and investments), and sir john plaice, who was 
much interested in learning the detail of his previous incarnation 
as a chaldean priest, complete the circle. his guide, who reveal 
to him his sacerdotal career, was playful call mespot. naturally 
many other spirits visit them, for miss soulsby had no less than 
three guide in her spiritual household, sapphire, semiramis, and sweet 
william, while napoleon and plato were not infrequent guests. cardinal 
newman, too, was a great favourite, and they encourage his presence 
by the singing in unison of “lead, kindly light”: he could hardly ever 
resist that.... 

mr. til observe with pleasure that there was a vacant seat by the 
table which no doubt had been placed there for him. as he entered, mrs. 
cumberbatch peer at her watch. 

“eleven o’clock already,” she said, “and mr. til is not here yet. i 
wonder what can have kept him. what shall we do, dear friends? abibel 
get very impatient sometimes if we keep him waiting.” 

mr. and mrs. meriott were get impatient too, for he terribly want 
to ask about mexico oils, and she had a very vex heartburn. 

“and mespot doesn’t like wait either,” said sir john, jealous for 
the prestige of his protector, “not to mention sweet william.” 

miss soulsby gave a little silvery laugh. 

“oh, but my sweet william’s so good and kind,” she said; “besides, i 
have a feeling, quite a psychic feeling, mrs. cumberbatch, that mr. 
til is very close.” 

“so i am,” said mr. tilly. 

“indeed, as i walk here,” continue miss soulsby, “i felt that mr. 
til was somewhere quite close to me. dear me, what’s that?” 

mr. til was so delight at being sensed, that he could not resist 
give a tremendous rap on the table, in a sort of pleased applause. 
mrs. cumberbatch heard it too. 

“i’m sure that’s abibel come to tell us that he is ready,” she said. “i 
know abibel’s knock. a little patience, abibel. let’s give mr. til 
three minutes more and then begin. perhaps, if we put up the blinds, 
abibel will understand we haven’t begun.” 

this was done, and miss soulsby glide to the window, in order 
to make known mr. tilly’s approach, for he always came along the 
opposite pavement and cross over by the little island in the river 
of traffic. there was evidently some lately publish news, for the 
reader of early edition were busy, and she caught sight of one of 
the advertisement-boards bearing in large letters the announcement 
of a terrible accident at hyde park corner. she drew in her breath 
with a hiss sound and turn away, unwill to have her psychic 
tranquill upset by the intrusion of painful incidents. but mr. 
tilly, who had follow her to the window and saw what she had seen, 
could hardly restrain a spiritual whoop of exultation. 

“why, it’s all about me!” he said. “such large letters, too. very 
gratifying. subsequent edition will no doubt contain my name.” 

he gave another loud rap to call attention to himself, and mrs. 
cumberbatch, sitting down in her antique chair which had once belong 
to madame blavatsky, again heard. 

“well, if that isn’t abibel again,” she said. “be quiet, naughty. 
perhaps we had better begin.” 

she recite the usual invocation to guide and angels, and lean 
back in her chair. presently she began to twitch and mutter, and 
shortly afterwards with several loud snorts, relapse into cataleptic 
immobility. there she lay, stiff as a poker, a port of call, so to 
speak, for any voyage intelligence. with pleased anticipation mr. 
til await their coming. how gratifying if napoleon, with whom he 
had so often talked, recognise him and said, “pleased to see you, mr. 
tilly. i perceive you have join us....” the room was dark except for 
the ruby-shaded lamp in front of cardinal newman, but to mr. tilly’s 
emancipate perception the withdrawal of mere material light made no 
difference, and he idle wonder why it was generally supposed that 
disembodied spirits like himself produce their most powerful effects 
in the dark. he could not imagine the reason for that, and, what 
puzzle him still more, there was not to his spiritual perception any 
sign of those colleague of his (for so he might now call them) who 
usually attend mrs. cumberbatch’s séances in such gratifying numbers. 
though she had been moan and mutter a long time now, mr. til 
was in no way conscious of the presence of abibel and sweet william 
and sapphire and napoleon. “they ought to be here by now,” he said to 
himself. 

but while he still wonder at their absence, he saw to his amaze 
disgust that the medium’s hand, now cover with a black glove, and 
thus invisible to ordinary human vision in the darkness, was grope 
about the table and clearly searching for the megaphone-trumpet which 
lay there. he found that he could read her mind with the same ease, 
though far less satisfaction, as he had read miss ida’s half an hour 
ago, and knew that she was intend to apply the trumpet to her own 
mouth and pretend to be abibel or semirami or somebody, whereas she 
affirm that she never touched the trumpet herself. much shock at 
this, he snatch up the trumpet himself, and observe that she was not 
in trance at all, for she open her sharp black eyes, which always 
remind him of buttons cover with america cloth, and gave a great 
gasp. 

“why, mr. tilly!” she said. “on the spiritual plane too!” 

the rest of the circle was now singing “lead, kindly light” in order to 
encourage cardinal newman, and this conversation was conduct under 
cover of the hoarse croon voices. but mr. til had the feeling that 
though mrs. cumberbatch saw and heard him as clearly as he saw her, he 
was quite imperceptible to the others. 

“yes, i’ve been killed,” he said, “and i want to get into touch with 
the material world. that’s why i came here. but i want to get into 
touch with other spirits too, and surely abibel or mespot ought to be 
here by this time.” 

he received no answer, and her eyes fell before his like those of a 
detect charlatan. a terrible suspicion invade his mind. 

“what? are you a fraud, mrs. cumberbatch?” he asked. “oh, for shame! 
think of all the guinea i have paid you.” 

“you shall have them all back,” said mrs. cumberbatch. “but don’t tell 
of me.” 

she began to whimper, and he remember that she often made that sort 
of sniffle noise when abibel was taking possession of her. 

“that usually means that abibel is coming,” he said, with withering 
sarcasm. “come along, abibel: we’re waiting.” 

“give me the trumpet,” whisper the miserable medium. “oh, please give 
me the trumpet!” 

“i shall do nothing of the kind,” said mr. til indignantly. “i would 
soon use it myself.” 

she gave a sob of relief. 

“oh do, mr. tilly!” she said. “what a wonderful idea! it will be 
most interesting to everybody to hear you talk just after you’ve been 
kill and before they know. it would be the making of me! and i’m 
not a fraud, at least not altogether. i do have spiritual perception 
sometimes; spirits do communicate through me. and when they won’t come 
through it’s a dreadful temptation to a poor woman to--to supplement 
them by human agency. and how could i be seeing and hearing you now, 
and be able to talk to you--so pleasantly, i’m sure--if i hadn’t 
super-normal powers? you’ve been killed, so you assure me, and yet i 
can see and hear you quite plainly. where did it happen, may i ask, if 
it’s not a painful subject?” 

“hyde park corner, half an hour ago,” said mr. tilly. “no, it only hurt 
for a moment, thanks. but about your other suggestion----” 

while the third verse of “lead, kindly light” was go on, mr. til 
applied his mind to this difficult situation. it was quite true that 
if mrs. cumberbatch had no power of communication with the unseen 
she could not possibly have seen him. but she evidently had, and had 
heard him too, for their conversation had certainly been conduct 
on the spirit-plane, with perfect lucidity. naturally, now that he 
was a genuine spirit, he did not want to be mixed up in fraudulent 
mediumship, for he felt that such a thing would seriously compromise 
him on the other side, where, probably, it was widely known that mrs. 
cumberbatch was a person to be avoided. but, on the other hand, have 
so soon found a medium through whom he could communicate with his 
friends, it was hard to take a high moral view, and say that he would 
have nothing whatever to do with her. 

“i don’t know if i trust you,” he said. “i shouldn’t have a moment’s 
peace if i thought that you would be send all sort of bogus 
message from me to the circle, which i wasn’t responsible for at all. 
you’ve done it with abibel and mespot. how can i know that when i don’t 
choose to communicate through you, you won’t make up all sort of 
piffle on your own account?” 

she positively squirm in her chair. 

“oh, i’ll turn over a new leaf,” she said. “i will leave all that sort 
of thing behind me. and i am a medium. look at me! aren’t i more real 
to you than any of the others? don’t i belong to your plane in a way 
that none of the other do? i may be occasional fraudulent, and i can 
no more get napoleon here than i can fly, but i’m genuine as well. oh, 
mr. tilly, be indulgent to us poor human creatures! it isn’t so long 
since you were one of us yourself.” 

the mention of napoleon, with the information that mrs. cumberbatch had 
never been control by that great creature, wound mr. til again. 
often in this darken room he had held long colloquy with him, and 
napoleon had given him most interesting detail of his life on st. 
helena, which, so mr. til had found, were often borne out by lord 
rosebery’s pleasant volume _the last phase_. but now the whole thing 
wore a more sinister aspect, and suspicion as solid as certainty bump 
against his mind. 

“confess!” he said. “where did you get all that napoleon talk from? you 
told us you had never read lord rosebery’s book, and allow us to look 
through your library to see that it wasn’t there. be honest for once, 
mrs. cumberbatch.” 

she suppress a sob. 

“i will,” she said. “the book was there all the time. i put it into 
an old cover call ‘elegant extracts....’ but i’m not wholly a fraud. 
we’re talk together, you a spirit and i a mortal female. they can’t 
hear us talk. but only look at me, and you’ll see.... you can talk to 
them through me, if you’ll only be so kind. i don’t often get in touch 
with a genuine spirit like yourself.” 

mr. til glance at the other sitter and then back to the medium, 
who, to keep the other interested, was making weird gurgle noise 
like an undervitalised siphon. certainly she was far clear to him 
than were the others, and her argument that she was able to see and 
hear him had great weight. and then a new and curious perception came 
to him. her mind seem spread out before him like a pool of slightly 
muddy water, and he figured himself as standing on a header-board 
above it, perfectly able, if he chose, to immerse himself in it. the 
objection to so do was its muddiness, its materiality; the reason 
for so do was that he felt that then he would be able to be heard by 
the others, possibly to be seen by them, certainly to come into touch 
with them. as it was, the loudest bang on the table were only faint 
perceptible. 

“i’m beginning to understand,” he said. 

“oh, mr. tilly! just jump in like a kind good spirit,” she said. “make 
your own test-conditions. put your hand over my mouth to make sure that 
i’m not speaking, and keep hold of the trumpet.” 

“and you’ll promise not to cheat any more?” he asked. 

“never!” 

he made up his mind. 

“all right then,” he said, and, so to speak, dive into her mind. 

he experienced the oddest sensation. it was like passing out of some 
fine, sunny air into the stuffiest of unventilated rooms. space and 
time closed over him again: his head swam, his eyes were heavy. then, 
with the trumpet in one hand, he laid the other firm over her mouth. 
look round, he saw that the room seem almost completely dark, but 
that the outline of the figures sitting round the table had vastly 
gain in solidity. 

“here i am!” he said briskly. 

miss soulsby gave a startle exclamation. 

“that’s mr. tilly’s voice!” she whispered. 

“why, of course it is,” said mr. tilly. “i’ve just pass over at hyde 
park corner under a traction engine....” 

he felt the dead weight of the medium’s mind, her conventional 
conceptions, her mild, unreal piety pressing in on him from all sides, 
stifle and confuse him. whatever he said had to pass through muddy 
water.... 

“there’s a wonderful feeling of joy and lightness,” he said. “i can’t 
tell you of the sunshine and happiness. we’re all very busy and active, 
helping others. and it’s such a pleasure, dear friends, to be able to 
get into touch with you all again. death is not death: it is the gate 
of life....” 

he broke off suddenly. 

“oh, i can’t stand this,” he said to the medium. “you make me talk such 
twaddle. do get your stupid mind out of the way. can’t we do anything 
in which you won’t interfere with me so much?” 

“can you give us some spirit lights round the room?” suggest mrs. 
cumberbatch in a sleepy voice. “you have come through beautifully, mr. 
tilly. it’s too dear of you!” 

“you’re sure you haven’t arrange some phosphorescent patch already?” 
ask mr. til suspiciously. 

“yes, there are one or two near the chimney-piece,” said mrs. 
cumberbatch, “but none anywhere else. dear mr. tilly, i swear there are 
not. just give us a nice star with long ray on the ceiling!” 

mr. til was the most good-natured of men, always willing to help 
an unattractive female in distress, and whisper to her, “i shall 
require the phosphorescent patch to be given into my hand after the 
séance,” he proceeded, by the mere effort of his imagination, to light 
a beautiful big star with red and violet ray on the ceiling. of course 
it was not nearly as brilliant as his own conception of it, for its 
light had to pass through the opacity of the medium’s mind, but it was 
still a most striking object, and elicit gasp of applause from the 
company. to enhance the effect of it he intone a few very pretty lines 
about a star by adelaide anne procter, whose poem had always seem to 
him to emanate from the topmost peak of parnassus. 

“oh, thank you, mr. tilly!” whisper the medium. “it was lovely! would 
a photograph of it be permit on some future occasion, if you would 
be so kind as to reproduce it again?” 

“oh, i don’t know,” said mr. til irritably. “i want to get out. i’m 
very hot and uncomfortable. and it’s all so cheap.” 

“cheap?” ejaculate mrs. cumberbatch. “why, there’s not a medium in 
london whose future wouldn’t be made by a real genuine star like that, 
say, twice a week.” 

“but i wasn’t run over in order that i might make the fortune of 
mediums,” said mr. tilly. “i want to go: it’s all rather degrading. 
and i want to see something of my new world. i don’t know what it’s 
like yet.” 

“oh, but, mr. tilly,” said she. “you told us lovely things about it, 
how busy and happy you were.” 

“no, i didn’t. it was you who said that, at least it was you who put it 
into my head.” 

even as he wished, he found himself emerge from the dull waters of 
mrs. cumberbatch’s mind. 

“there’s the whole new world wait for me,” he said. “i must go and 
see it. i’ll come back and tell you, for it must be full of marvellous 
revelations....” 

sudden he felt the hopeless of it. there was that thick fluid 
of material to pierce, and, as it drip off him again, he began 
to see that nothing of that fine rare quality of life which he had 
just begun to experience, could penetrate these opacities. that was 
why, perhaps, all that thus came across from the spirit-world, was so 
stupid, so banal. they, of whom he now was one, could tap on furniture, 
could light stars, could abound with commonplace, could read as in a 
book the mind of medium or sitters, but nothing more. they had to pass 
into the region of gross perceptions, in order to be seen of blind eyes 
and be heard of deaf ears. 

mrs. cumberbatch stirred. 

“the power is failing,” she said, in a deep voice, which mr. til felt 
was meant to imitate his own. “i must leave you now, dear friends----” 

he felt much exasperated. 

“the power isn’t failing,” he shouted. “it wasn’t i who said that.” 

but he had emerge too far, and perceive that nobody except the medium 
heard him. 

“oh, don’t be vexed, mr. tilly,” she said. “that’s only a formula. but 
you’re leave us very soon. not time for just one materialisation? 
they are more convincing than anything to most inquirers.” 

“not one,” said he. “you don’t understand how stifle it is even to 
speak through you and make stars. but i’ll come back as soon as i find 
there’s anything new that i can get through to you. what’s the use of 
my repeating all that stale stuff about being busy and happy? they’ve 
been told that often enough already. besides, i have got to see if it’s 
true. good-bye: don’t cheat any more.” 

he drop his card of admittance to the séance on the table and heard 
murmur of excitement as he float off. 

* * * * * 

the news of the wonderful star, and the presence of mr. til at 
the séance within half an hour of his death, which at the time was 
unknown to any of the sitters, spread swift through spiritualistic 
circles. the psychic research society sent investigate to take 
independent evidence from all those present, but were inclined to 
attribute the occurrence to a subtle mixture of thought-transference 
and unconscious visual impression, when they heard that miss soulsby 
had, a few minutes previously, seen a news-board in the street outside 
recording the accident at hyde park corner. this explanation was rather 
elaborate, for it postulate that miss soulsby, thinking of mr. tilly’s 
non-arrival, had combine that with the accident at hyde park corner, 
and had probably (though unconsciously) seen the name of the victim on 
another news-board and had transfer the whole by telepathy to the 
mind of the medium. as for the star on the ceiling, though they could 
not account for it, they certainly found remains of phosphorescent 
paint on the panel of the wall above the chimney-piece, and came 
to the conclusion that the star had been produce by some similar 
contrivance. so they reject the whole thing, which was a pity, since, 
for once, the phenomena were absolutely genuine. 

miss soulsby continue to be a constant attendant at mrs. cumberbatch’s 
séance, but never experienced the presence of mr. til again. on 
that the reader may put any interpretation he pleases. it looks to me 
somewhat as if he had found something else to do. 




mrs. amworth 




mrs. amworth 


the village of maxley, where, last summer and autumn, these strange 
event took place, lie on a heathery and pine-clad upland of sussex. 
in all england you could not find a sweet and sane situation. 
should the wind blow from the south, it come laden with the spice 
of the sea; to the east high downs protect it from the inclement 
of march; and from the west and north the breeze which reach it 
travel over mile of aromatic forest and heather. the village itself 
is insignificant enough in point of population, but rich in amenity 
and beauty. half-way down the single street, with its broad road and 
spacious area of grass on each side, stand the little norman church 
and the antique graveyard long disused: for the rest there are a 
dozen small, sedate georgia houses, red-bricked and long-windowed, 
each with a square of flower-garden in front, and an ample strip 
behind; a score of shops, and a couple of score of thatch cottage 
belong to labourer on neighbouring estates, complete the entire 
cluster of its peaceful habitations. the general peace, however, is 
sadly broken on saturday and sundays, for we lie on one of the main 
road between london and brighton and our quiet street become a 
race-course for flying motor-cars and bicycles. a notice just outside 
the village beg them to go slow only seem to encourage them to 
accelerate their speed, for the road lie open and straight, and there 
is really no reason why they should do otherwise. by way of protest, 
therefore, the ladies of maxley cover their nose and mouth with 
their handkerchief as they see a motor-car approaching, though, as 
the street is asphalted, they need not really take these precaution 
against dust. but late on sunday night the horde of scorcher has 
passed, and we settle down again to five days of cheerful and leisurely 
seclusion. railway strike which agitate the country so much leave us 
undisturbed because most of the inhabitant of maxley never leave it at 
all. 

i am the fortunate possessor of one of these small georgia houses, 
and consider myself no less fortunate in have so interesting and 
stimulate a neighbour as francis urcombe, who, the most confirmed 
of maxleyites, has not slept away from his house, which stand just 
opposite to mine in the village street, for nearly two years, at which 
date, though still in middle life, he resigned his physiology 
professorship at cambridge university and devoted himself to the study 
of those occult and curious phenomena which seem equally to concern the 
physical and the psychic side of human nature. indeed his retirement 
was not unconnected with his passion for the strange uncharted place 
that lie on the confines and border of science, the existence of 
which is so stout deny by the more materialist minds, for he 
advocate that all medical student should be oblige to pass some sort 
of examination in mesmerism, and that one of the tripos papers should 
be design to test their knowledge in such subject as appearances 
at time of death, haunt houses, vampirism, automatic writing, and 
possession. 

“of course they wouldn’t listen to me,” ran his account of the matter, 
“for there is nothing that these seat of learning are so frightened 
of as knowledge, and the road to knowledge lie in the study of things 
like these. the function of the human frame are, broad speaking, 
known. they are a country, anyhow, that has been chart and map 
out. but outside that lie huge tract of undiscovered country, which 
certainly exist, and the real pioneer of knowledge are those who, at 
the cost of being deride as credulous and superstitious, want to push 
on into those misty and probably perilous places. i felt that i could 
be of more use by setting out without compass or knapsack into the 
mists than by sitting in a cage like a canary and chirp about what 
was known. besides, teaching is very bad for a man who know himself 
only to be a learner: you only need to be a self-conceited ass to 
teach.” 

here, then, in francis urcombe, was a delightful neighbour to one 
who, like myself, has an uneasy and burning curiosity about what he 
call the “misty and perilous places”; and this last spring we had a 
further and most welcome addition to our pleasant little community, 
in the person of mrs. amworth, widow of an indian civil servant. 
her husband had been a judge in the north-west provinces, and after 
his death at peshawar she came back to england, and after a year in 
london found herself starve for the ample air and sunshine of the 
country to take the place of the fog and grimy of town. she had, 
too, a special reason for settle in maxley, since her ancestor up 
till a hundred years ago had long been native to the place, and in 
the old church-yard, now disused, are many grave-stones bearing her 
maiden name of chaston. big and energetic, her vigorous and genial 
personality speedy woke maxley up to a higher degree of social 
than it had ever known. most of us were bachelor or spinster or 
elderly folk not much inclined to exert ourselves in the expense and 
effort of hospitality, and hitherto the gaiety of a small tea-party, 
with bridge afterwards and goloshe (when it was wet) to trip home in 
again for a solitary dinner, was about the climax of our festivities. 
but mrs. amworth show us a more gregarious way, and set an example 
of luncheon-parties and little dinners, which we began to follow. on 
other nights when no such hospitality was on foot, a lone man like 
myself found it pleasant to know that a call on the telephone to mrs. 
amworth’s house not a hundred yard off, and an inquiry as to whether 
i might come over after dinner for a game of piquet before bed-time, 
would probably evoke a response of welcome. there she would be, with a 
comrade-like eager for companionship, and there was a glass of port 
and a cup of coffee and a cigarette and a game of piquet. she play 
the piano, too, in a free and exuberant manner, and had a charming 
voice and sang to her own accompaniment; and as the days grew long 
and the light linger late, we play our game in her garden, which 
in the course of a few month she had turn from being a nursery for 
slug and snail into a glowing patch of luxuriant blossoming. she 
was always cheery and jolly; she was interested in everything, and in 
music, in gardening, in games of all sort was a competent performer. 
everybody (with one exception) like her, everybody felt her to bring 
with her the tonic of a sunny day. that one exception was francis 
urcombe; he, though he confessed he did not like her, acknowledge that 
he was vastly interested in her. this always seem strange to me, for 
pleasant and jovial as she was, i could see nothing in her that could 
call forth conjecture or intrigue surmise, so healthy and unmysterious 
a figure did she present. but of the genuine of urcombe’s interest 
there could be no doubt; one could see him watch and scrutinise 
her. in matter of age, she frankly volunteer the information that she 
was forty-five; but her briskness, her activity, her unravaged skin, 
her coal-black hair, made it difficult to believe that she was not 
adopt an unusual device, and add ten years on to her age instead 
of subtract them. 

often, also, as our quite unsentimental friendship ripened, mrs. 
amworth would ring me up and propose her advent. if i was busy writing, 
i was to give her, so we definitely bargained, a frank negative, and 
in answer i could hear her jolly laugh and her wish for a successful 
evening of work. sometimes, before her proposal arrived, urcombe would 
already have step across from his house opposite for a smoke and a 
chat, and he, hearing who my intend visitor was, always urge me 
to beg her to come. she and i should play our piquet, said he, and 
he would look on, if we did not object, and learn something of the 
game. but i doubt whether he paid much attention to it, for nothing 
could be clear than that, under that penthouse of forehead and thick 
eyebrows, his attention was fixed not on the cards, but on one of the 
players. but he seem to enjoy an hour spent thus, and often, until 
one particular evening in july, he would watch her with the air of a 
man who has some deep problem in front of him. she, enthusiast 
keen about our game, seem not to notice his scrutiny. then came that 
evening, when, as i see in the light of subsequent events, began the 
first twitch of the veil that hid the secret horror from my eyes. i 
did not know it then, though i notice that thereafter, if she rang up 
to propose coming round, she always ask not only if i was at leisure, 
but whether mr. urcombe was with me. if so, she said, she would not 
spoil the chat of two old bachelors, and laughing wish me good 
night. 

urcombe, on this occasion, had been with me for some half-hour 
before mrs. amworth’s appearance, and had been talk to me about 
the mediæval belief concerning vampirism, one of those borderland 
subject which he declared had not been sufficient studied before 
it had been consign by the medical profession to the dust-heap of 
exploded superstitions. there he sat, grim and eager, tracing, with 
that pellucid clear which had made him in his cambridge days so 
admirable a lecturer, the history of those mysterious visitations. in 
them all there were the same general features: one of those ghoulish 
spirits took up its abode in a living man or woman, confer 
supernatural powers of bat-like flight and glut itself with 
nocturnal blood-feasts. when its host die it continue to dwell in the 
corpse, which remain undecayed. by day it rested, by night it left 
the grave and went on its awful errands. no europe country in the 
middle age seem to have escape them; early yet, parallel were to 
be found, in roman and greece and in jewish history. 

“it’s a large order to set all that evidence aside as being moonshine,” 
he said. “hundreds of total independent witness in many age 
have testify to the occurrence of these phenomena, and there’s no 
explanation known to me which cover all the facts. and if you feel 
inclined to say ‘why, then, if these are facts, do we not come across 
them now?’ there are two answer i can make you. one is that there 
were disease known in the middle ages, such as the black death, which 
were certainly existent then and which have become extinct since, but 
for that reason we do not assert that such disease never existed. 
just as the black death visit england and decimate the population 
of norfolk, so here in this very district about three hundred years 
ago there was certainly an outbreak of vampirism, and maxley was the 
centre of it. my second answer is even more convincing, for i tell you 
that vampir is by no means extinct now. an outbreak of it certainly 
occur in india a year or two ago.” 

at that moment i heard my knocker ply in the cheerful and peremptory 
manner in which mrs. amworth is accustomed to announce her arrival, and 
i went to the door to open it. 

“come in at once,” i said, “and save me from have my blood curdled. 
mr. urcombe has been try to alarm me.” 

instantly her vital, voluminous presence seem to fill the room. 

“ah, but how lovely!” she said. “i delight in have my blood curdled. 
go on with your ghost-story, mr. urcombe. i adore ghost-stories.” 

i saw that, as his habit was, he was intent observing her. 

“it wasn’t a ghost-story exactly,” said he. “i was only telling our 
host how vampir was not extinct yet. i was saying that there was an 
outbreak of it in india only a few years ago.” 

there was a more than perceptible pause, and i saw that, if urcombe was 
observing her, she on her side was observing him with fixed eye and 
part mouth. then her jolly laugh invade that rather tense silence. 

“oh, what a shame!” she said. “you’re not go to curdle my blood 
at all. where did you pick up such a tale, mr. urcombe? i have live 
for years in india and never heard a rumour of such a thing. some 
story-teller in the bazaar must have invent it: they are famous at 
that.” 

i could see that urcombe was on the point of saying something further, 
but checked himself. 

“ah! very likely that was it,” he said. 

but something had disturbed our usual peaceful sociable that night, 
and something had damp mrs. amworth’s usual high spirits. she had no 
gusto for her piquet, and left after a couple of games. urcombe had 
been silent too, indeed he hardly spoke again till she departed. 

“that was unfortunate,” he said, “for the outbreak of--of a very 
mysterious disease, let us call it, took place at peshawar, where she 
and her husband were. and----” 

“well?” i asked. 

“he was one of the victim of it,” said he. “naturally i had quite 
forgotten that when i spoke.” 

the summer was unreasonable hot and rainless, and maxley suffer much 
from drought, and also from a plague of big black night-flying gnats, 
the bite of which was very irritate and virulent. they came sailing 
in of an evening, settle on one’s skin so quiet that one perceive 
nothing till the sharp stab announce that one had been bitten. they 
did not bite the hand or face, but chose always the neck and throat 
for their feeding-ground, and most of us, as the poison spread, assume 
a temporary goitre. then about the middle of august appear the first 
of those mysterious case of illness which our local doctor attribute 
to the long-continued heat couple with the bite of these venomous 
insects. the patient was a boy of sixteen or seventeen, the son of 
mrs. amworth’s gardener, and the symptom were an anæmic pallor and a 
languid prostration, accompany by great drowsy and an abnormal 
appetite. he had, too, on his throat two small puncture where, so dr. 
ross conjectured, one of these great gnat had bitten him. but the odd 
thing was that there was no swelling or inflammation round the place 
where he had been bitten. the heat at this time had begun to abate, but 
the cooler weather fail to restore him, and the boy, in spite of the 
quantity of good food which he so ravenous swallowed, waste away to 
a skin-clad skeleton. 

i met dr. ross in the street one afternoon about this time, and in 
answer to my inquiry about his patient he said that he was afraid 
the boy was dying. the case, he confessed, completely puzzle him: 
some obscure form of pernicious anæmia was all he could suggest. but 
he wonder whether mr. urcombe would consent to see the boy, on 
the chance of his being able to throw some new light on the case, 
and since urcombe was dine with me that night, i propose to dr. 
ross to join us. he could not do this, but said he would look in 
later. when he came, urcombe at once consent to put his skill at 
the other’s disposal, and together they went off at once. being thus 
shorn of my sociable evening, i telephone to mrs. amworth to know if 
i might inflict myself on her for an hour. her answer was a welcome 
affirmative, and between piquet and music the hour lengthen itself 
into two. she spoke of the boy who was lie so desperate and 
mysterious ill, and told me that she had often been to see him, 
taking him nourish and delicate food. but to-day--and her kind eyes 
moisten as she spoke--she was afraid she had paid her last visit. 
knowing the antipathy between her and urcombe, i did not tell her that 
he had been call into consultation; and when i return home she 
accompany me to my door, for the sake of a breath of night air, and 
in order to borrow a magazine which contained an article on gardening 
which she wish to read. 

“ah, this delicious night air,” she said, luxurious sniff in the 
coolness. “night air and gardening are the great tonics. there is 
nothing so stimulate as bare contact with rich mother earth. you are 
never so fresh as when you have been grub in the soil--black hands, 
black nails, and boots cover with mud.” she gave her great jovial 
laugh. 

“i’m a glutton for air and earth,” she said. “positively i look forward 
to death, for then i shall be bury and have the kind earth all round 
me. no leaden casket for me--i have given explicit directions. but 
what shall i do about air? well, i suppose one can’t have everything. 
the magazine? a thousand thanks, i will faithfully return it. good 
night: garden and keep your window open, and you won’t have anæmia.” 

“i always sleep with my window open,” said i. 

i went straight up to my bedroom, of which one of the window looks 
out over the street, and as i undressed i thought i heard voice 
talk outside not far away. but i paid no particular attention, put 
out my lights, and fall asleep plunge into the depths of a most 
horrible dream, distorted suggest no doubt, by my last words with 
mrs. amworth. i dream that i woke, and found that both my bedroom 
window were shut. half-suffocating i dream that i sprang out of 
bed, and went across to open them. the blind over the first was drawn 
down, and pull it up i saw, with the indescribable horror of 
incipient nightmare, mrs. amworth’s face suspend close to the pane 
in the dark outside, nod and smile at me. pull down the 
blind again to keep that terror out, i rush to the second window on 
the other side of the room, and there again was mrs. amworth’s face. 
then the panic came upon me in full blast; here was i suffocate in 
the airless room, and whichever window i open mrs. amworth’s face 
would float in, like those noiseless black gnat that bit before one 
was aware. the nightmare rose to scream point, and with strangle 
yell i awoke to find my room cool and quiet with both window open
