and blind up and a half-moon high in its course, casting an oblong 
of tranquil light on the floor. but even when i was awake the horror 
persisted, and i lay toss and turning. i must have slept long before 
the nightmare seize me, for now it was nearly day, and soon in the 
east the drowsy eyelid of morning began to lift. 

i was scarcely downstairs next morning--for after the dawn i slept 
late--when urcombe rang up to know if he might see me immediately. he 
came in, grim and preoccupied, and i notice that he was pull on a 
pipe that was not even filled. 

“i want your help,” he said, “and so i must tell you first of all 
what happen last night. i went round with the little doctor to see 
his patient, and found him just alive, but scarcely more. i instantly 
diagnose in my own mind what this anæmia, unaccountable by any other 
explanation, meant. the boy is the prey of a vampire.” 

he put his empty pipe on the breakfast-table, by which i had just 
sat down, and fold his arms, look at me steady from under his 
overhang brows. 

“now about last night,” he said. “i insist that he should be move 
from his father’s cottage into my house. as we were carry him on a 
stretcher, whom should we meet but mrs. amworth? she express shock 
surprise that we were moving him. now why do you think she did that?” 

with a start of horror, as i remember my dream that night before, i 
felt an idea come into my mind so preposterous and unthinkable that i 
instantly turn it out again. 

“i haven’t the smallest idea,” i said. 

“then listen, while i tell you about what happen later. i put out 
all light in the room where the boy lay, and watched. one window was 
a little open, for i had forgotten to close it, and about midnight i 
heard something outside, try apparently to push it farther open. i 
guess who it was--yes, it was full twenty feet from the ground--and 
i peep round the corner of the blind. just outside was the face of 
mrs. amworth and her hand was on the frame of the window. very soft i 
crept close, and then bang the window down, and i think i just caught 
the tip of one of her fingers.” 

“but it’s impossible,” i cried. “how could she be floating in the air 
like that? and what had she come for? don’t tell me such----” 

once more, with close grip, the remembrance of my nightmare seize me. 

“i am telling you what i saw,” said he. “and all night long, until it 
was nearly day, she was flutter outside, like some terrible bat, 
try to gain admittance. now put together various things i have told 
you.” 

he began check them off on his fingers. 

“number one,” he said: “there was an outbreak of disease similar to 
that which this boy is suffering from at peshawar, and her husband 
die of it. number two: mrs. amworth protest against my moving the 
boy to my house. number three: she, or the demon that inhabit her 
body, a creature powerful and deadly, try to gain admittance. and add 
this, too: in mediæval times there was an epidemic of vampir here 
at maxley. the vampire, so the account run, was found to be elizabeth 
chaston ... i see you remember mrs. amworth’s maiden name. finally, the 
boy is strong this morning. he would certainly not have been alive if 
he had been visit again. and what do you make of it?” 

there was a long silence, during which i found this incredible horror 
assume the hue of reality. 

“i have something to add,” i said, “which may or may not bear on it. 
you say that the--the spectre went away shortly before dawn.” 

“yes.” 

i told him of my dream, and he smile grimly. 

“yes, you did well to awake,” he said. “that warning came from your 
subconscious self, which never wholly slumbers, and cry out to you 
of deadly danger. for two reasons, then, you must help me: one to save 
others, the second to save yourself.” 

“what do you want me to do?” i asked. 

“i want you first of all to help me in watch this boy, and ensure 
that she do not come near him. eventually i want you to help me in 
track the thing down, in expose and destroy it. it is not 
human: it is an incarnate fiend. what steps we shall have to take i 
don’t yet know.” 

it was now eleven of the forenoon, and presently i went across to his 
house for a twelve-hour vigil while he slept, to come on duty again 
that night, so that for the next twenty-four hour either urcombe or 
myself was always in the room where the boy, now get strong 
every hour, was lying. the day following was saturday and a morning 
of brilliant, pellucid weather, and already when i went across to his 
house to resume my duty the stream of motor down to brighton had 
begun. simultaneous i saw urcombe with a cheerful face, which bode 
good news of his patient, coming out of his house, and mrs. amworth, 
with a gesture of salutation to me and a basket in her hand, walking up 
the broad strip of grass which border the road. there we all three 
met. i notice (and saw that urcombe notice it too) that one finger of 
her left hand was bandaged. 

“good morning to you both,” said she. “and i hear your patient is do 
well, mr. urcombe. i have come to bring him a bowl of jelly, and to sit 
with him for an hour. he and i are great friends. i am overjoyed at his 
recovery.” 

urcombe pause a moment, as if making up his mind, and then shot out a 
point finger at her. 

“i forbid that,” he said. “you shall not sit with him or see him. and 
you know the reason as well as i do.” 

i have never seen so horrible a change pass over a human face as that 
which now blanch hers to the colour of a grey mist. she put up her 
hand as if to shield herself from that point finger, which drew the 
sign of the cross in the air, and shrank back cower on to the road. 
there was a wild hoot from a horn, a grind of brakes, a shout--too 
late--from a passing car, and one long scream sudden cut short. her 
body rebound from the roadway after the first wheel had gone over it, 
and the second followed. it lay there, quiver and twitching, and was 
still. 

she was bury three days afterwards in the cemetery outside maxley, 
in accordance with the wish she had told me that she had devise 
about her interment, and the shock which her sudden and awful death 
had cause to the little community began by degree to pass off. to 
two people only, urcombe and myself, the horror of it was mitigate 
from the first by the nature of the relief that her death brought; 
but, naturally enough, we kept our own counsel, and no hint of what 
greater horror had been thus avert was ever let slip. but, oddly 
enough, so it seem to me, he was still not satisfy about something 
in connection with her, and would give no answer to my question on 
the subject. then as the days of a tranquil mellow september and 
the october that follow began to drop away like the leaves of the 
yellow trees, his uneasy relaxed. but before the entry of 
november the seeming tranquill broke into hurricane. 

i had been dine one night at the far end of the village, and about 
eleven o’clock was walking home again. the moon was of an unusual 
brilliance, rendering all that it shone on as distinct as in some 
etching. i had just come opposite the house which mrs. amworth had 
occupied, where there was a board up telling that it was to let, when i 
heard the click of her front gate, and next moment i saw, with a sudden 
chill and quake of my very spirit, that she stood there. her profile, 
vivid illuminated, was turn to me, and i could not be mistaken in 
my identification of her. she appear not to see me (indeed the shadow 
of the yew hedge in front of her garden envelop me in its blackness) 
and she went swift across the road, and enter the gate of the 
house directly opposite. there i lost sight of her completely. 

my breath was coming in short pants as if i had been running--and now 
indeed i ran, with fearful backward glances, along the hundred yard 
that separate me from my house and urcombe’s. it was to his that my 
flying steps took me, and next minute i was within. 

“what have you come to tell me?” he asked. “or shall i guess?” 

“you can’t guess,” said i. 

“no; it’s no guess. she has come back and you have seen her. tell me 
about it.” 

i gave him my story. 

“that’s major pearsall’s house,” he said. “come back with me there at 
once.” 

“but what can we do?” i asked. 

“i’ve no idea. that’s what we have got to find out.” 

a minute later, we were opposite the house. when i had pass it 
before, it was all dark; now lights gleam from a couple of window 
upstairs. even as we face it, the front door opened, and next moment 
major pearsall emerge from the gate. he saw us and stopped. 

“i’m on my way to dr. ross,” he said quickly. “my wife has been taken 
sudden ill. she had been in bed an hour when i came upstairs, and 
i found her white as a ghost and utterly exhausted. she had been to 
sleep, it seemed---- but you will excuse me.” 

“one moment, major,” said urcombe. “was there any mark on her throat?” 

“how did you guess that?” said he. “there was: one of those beastly 
gnat must have bitten her twice there. she was stream with blood.” 

“and there’s someone with her?” ask urcombe. 

“yes, i rouse her maid.” 

he went off, and urcombe turn to me. “i know now what we have to do,” 
he said. “change your clothes, and i’ll join you at your house.” 

“what is it?” i asked. 

“i’ll tell you on our way. we’re go to the cemetery.” 

* * * * * 

he carry a pick, a shovel, and a screwdriver when he rejoin me, and 
wore round his shoulder a long coil of rope. as we walked, he gave me 
the outline of the ghastly hour that lay before us. 

“what i have to tell you,” he said, “will seem to you now too fantastic 
for credence, but before dawn we shall see whether it outstrip 
reality. by a most fortunate happening, you saw the spectre, the 
astral body, whatever you choose to call it, of mrs. amworth, go on 
its grisly business, and therefore, beyond doubt, the vampire spirit 
which abode in her during life animate her again in death. that is 
not exceptional--indeed, all these week since her death i have been 
expect it. if i am right, we shall find her body undecayed and 
untouched by corruption.” 

“but she has been dead nearly two months,” said i. 

“if she had been dead two years it would still be so, if the vampire 
has possession of her. so remember: whatever you see done, it will be 
done not to her, who in the natural course would now be feed the 
grass above her grave, but to a spirit of untold evil and malignancy, 
which give a phantom life to her body.” 

“but what shall i see done?” said i. 

“i will tell you. we know that now, at this moment, the vampire clad in 
her mortal semblance is out; dine out. but it must get back before 
dawn, and it will pass into the material form that lie in her grave. 
we must wait for that, and then with your help i shall dig up her 
body. if i am right, you will look on her as she was in life, with the 
full vigour of the dreadful nutriment she has received pulse in her 
veins. and then, when dawn has come, and the vampire cannot leave the 
lair of her body, i shall strike her with this”--and he pointed to his 
pick--“through the heart, and she, who come to life again only with 
the animation the fiend give her, she and her hellish partner will be 
dead indeed. then we must bury her again, deliver at last.” 

we had come to the cemetery, and in the bright of the moonshine 
there was no difficulty in identify her grave. it lay some twenty 
yard from the small chapel, in the porch of which, obscure by shadow, 
we conceal ourselves. from there we had a clear and open sight of 
the grave, and now we must wait till its infernal visitor return 
home. the night was warm and windless, yet even if a freezing wind had 
been rage i think i should have felt nothing of it, so intense was 
my preoccupation as to what the night and dawn would bring. there was 
a bell in the turret of the chapel, that struck the quarters of the 
hour, and it amaze me to find how swift the chime succeed one 
another. 

the moon had long set, but a twilight of stars shone in a clear sky, 
when five o’clock of the morning sound from the turret. a few minutes 
more passed, and then i felt urcombe’s hand soft nudge me; and 
look out in the direction of his point finger, i saw that the 
form of a woman, tall and large in build, was approach from the 
right. noiselessly, with a motion more of gliding and floating than 
walking, she move across the cemetery to the grave which was the 
centre of our observation. she move round it as if to be certain 
of its identity, and for a moment stood directly facing us. in the 
grey to which now my eyes had grown accustomed, i could easily see 
her face, and recognise its features. 

she drew her hand across her mouth as if wipe it, and broke into a 
chuckle of such laughter as made my hair stir on my head. then she 
leap on to the grave, holding her hand high above her head, and inch 
by inch disappear into the earth. urcombe’s hand was laid on my arm, 
in an injunction to keep still, but now he remove it. 

“come,” he said. 

with pick and shovel and rope we went to the grave. the earth was light 
and sandy, and soon after six struck we had delve down to the coffin 
lid. with his pick he loosen the earth round it, and, adjust the 
rope through the handle by which it had been lowered, we try to 
raise it. this was a long and laborious business, and the light had 
begun to herald day in the east before we had it out, and lie by the 
side of the grave. with his screwdriver he loose the fastening of 
the lid, and slid it aside, and standing there we look on the face 
of mrs. amworth. the eyes, once closed in death, were open, the cheek 
were flushed with colour, the red, full-lipped mouth seem to smile. 

“one blow and it is all over,” he said. “you need not look.” 

even as he spoke he took up the pick again, and, lay the point of it 
on her left breast, measured his distance. and though i knew what was 
coming i could not look away.... 

he grasp the pick in both hands, raise it an inch or two for the 
taking of his aim, and then with full force brought it down on her 
breast. a fountain of blood, though she had been dead so long, spout 
high in the air, fall with the thud of a heavy splash over the 
shroud, and simultaneous from those red lip came one long, appalling 
cry, swelling up like some hoot siren, and die away again. with 
that, instantaneous as a lightning flash, came the touch of corruption 
on her face, the colour of it fade to ash, the plump cheek fell in, 
the mouth dropped. 

“thank god, that’s over,” said he, and without pause slip the coffin 
lid back into its place. 

day was coming fast now, and, working like men possessed, we lower 
the coffin into its place again, and shovel the earth over it.... 
the bird were busy with their earliest piping as we went back to 
maxley. 




in the tube 




in the tube 


“it’s a convention,” said anthony carl cheerfully, “and not a very 
convincing one. time, indeed! there’s no such thing as time really; it 
has no actual existence. time is nothing more than an infinitesimal 
point in eternity, just as space is an infinitesimal point in infinity. 
at the most, time is a sort of tunnel through which we are accustomed 
to believe that we are travelling. there’s a roar in our ear and a 
dark in our eyes which make it seem real to us. but before we came 
into the tunnel we exist for ever in an infinite sunlight, and after 
we have got through it we shall exist in an infinite sunlight again. 
so why should we bother ourselves about the confusion and noise and 
dark which only encompass us for a moment?” 

for a firm-rooted believer in such immeasurable idea as these, which 
he punctuate with brisk application of the poker to the brave sparkle 
and glow of the fire, anthony has a very pleasant appreciation of the 
measurable and the finite, and nobody with whom i have acquaintance has 
so keen a zest for life and its enjoyment as he. he had given us this 
evening an admirable dinner, had pass round a port beyond praise, 
and had illuminate the jolly hour with the light of his infectious 
optimism. now the small company had melt away, and i was left with 
him over the fire in his study. outside the tattoo of wind-driven sleet 
was audible on the window-panes, over-scoring now and again the flap 
of the flame on the open hearth, and the thought of the chilly blast 
and the snow-covered pavement in brompton square, across which, to 
skid taxicabs, the last of his other guest had scurried, made my 
position, resident here till to-morrow morning, the more delicate 
delightful. above all there was this stimulate and suggestive 
companion, who, whether he talk of the great abstraction which were 
so intense real and practical to him, or of the very remarkable 
experience which he had encounter among these convention of time 
and space, was equally fascinating to the listener. 

“i adore life,” he said. “i find it the most entrance plaything. it’s 
a delightful game, and, as you know very well, the only conceivable 
way to play a game is to treat it extremely seriously. if you say to 
yourself, ‘it’s only a game,’ you cease to take the slightest interest 
in it. you have to know that it’s only a game, and behave as if it 
was the one object of existence. i should like it to go on for many 
years yet. but all the time one has to be living on the true plane as 
well, which is eternity and infinity. if you come to think of it, the 
one thing which the human mind cannot grasp is the finite, not the 
infinite, the temporary, not the eternal.” 

“that sound rather paradoxical,” said i. 

“only because you’ve made a habit of thinking about things that seem 
bound and limited. look it in the face for a minute. try to imagine 
finite time and space, and you find you can’t. go back a million 
years, and multiply that million of years by another million, and you 
find that you can’t conceive of a beginning. what happen before 
that beginning? another beginning and another beginning? and before 
that? look at it like that, and you find that the only solution 
comprehensible to you is the existence of an eternity, something that 
never began and will never end. it’s the same about space. project 
yourself to the farthest star, and what come beyond that? emptiness? 
go on through the emptiness, and you can’t imagine it being finite and 
have an end. it must needs go on for ever: that’s the only thing 
you can understand. there’s no such thing as before or after, or 
beginning or end, and what a comfort that is! i should fidget myself 
to death if there wasn’t the huge soft cushion of eternity to lean 
one’s head against. some people say--i believe i’ve heard you say it 
yourself--that the idea of eternity is so tiring; you feel that you 
want to stop. but that’s because you are thinking of eternity in terms 
of time, and mumble in your brain, ‘and after that, and after that?’ 
don’t you grasp the idea that in eternity there isn’t any ‘after,’ 
any more than there is any ‘before’? it’s all one. eternity isn’t a 
quantity: it’s a quality.” 

sometimes, when anthony talks in this manner, i seem to get a glimpse 
of that which to his mind is so transparent clear and solid 
real, at other times (not have a brain that readily envisage 
abstractions) i feel as though he was push me over a precipice, 
and my intellectual faculty grasp wildly at anything tangible or 
comprehensible. this was the case now, and i hasty interrupted. 

“but there is a ‘before’ and ‘after,’” i said. “a few hour ago you 
gave us an admirable dinner, and after that--yes, after--we play 
bridge. and now you are go to explain things a little more clearly 
to me, and after that i shall go to bed----” 

he laughed. 

“you shall do exactly as you like,” he said, “and you shan’t be a slave 
to time either to-night or to-morrow morning. we won’t even mention 
an hour for breakfast, but you shall have it in eternity whenever you 
awake. and as i see it is not midnight yet, we’ll slip the bonds of 
time, and talk quite infinitely. i will stop the clock, if that will 
assist you in get rid of your illusion, and then i’ll tell you a 
story, which to my mind, show how unreal so-called reality are; or, 
at any rate, how fallacious are our senses as judge of what is real 
and what is not.” 

“something occult, something spookish?” i asked, prick up my ears, 
for anthony has the strangest clairvoyance and vision of things 
unseen by the normal eye. 

“i suppose you might call some of it occult,” he said, “though there’s 
a certain amount of rather grim reality mixed up in it.” 

“go on; excellent mixture,” said i. 

he threw a fresh log on the fire. 

“it’s a longish story,” he said. “you may stop me as soon as you’ve 
had enough. but there will come a point for which i claim your 
consideration. you, who cling to your ‘before’ and ‘after,’ has it 
ever occur to you how difficult it is to say _when_ an incident 
take place? say that a man commit some crime of violence, can we 
not, with a good deal of truth, say that he really commit that crime 
when he definitely plan and determine upon it, dwelling on it with 
gusto? the actual commission of it, i think we can reasonably argue, 
is the mere material sequel of his resolve: he is guilty of it when he 
make that determination. when, therefore, in the term of ‘before’ and 
‘after,’ do the crime truly take place? there is also in my story a 
further point for your consideration. for it seem certain that the 
spirit of a man, after the death of his body, is oblige to re-enact 
such a crime, with a view, i suppose we may guess, to his remorse and 
his eventual redemption. those who have second sight have seen such 
re-enactments. perhaps he may have done his deed blind in this life; 
but then his spirit re-commits it with its spiritual eyes open, and 
able to comprehend its enormity. so, shall we view the man’s original 
determination and the material commission of his crime only as prelude 
to the real commission of it, when with eyes unsealed he do it and 
repent of it?... that all sound very obscure when i speak in the 
abstract, but i think you will see what i mean, if you follow my tale. 
comfortable? got everything you want? here goes, then.” 

he lean back in his chair, concentrate his mind, and then spoke: 

“the story that i am about to tell you,” he said, “had its beginning 
a month ago, when you were away in switzerland. it reach its 
conclusion, so i imagine, last night. i do not, at any rate, expect to 
experience any more of it. well, a month ago i was return late on 
a very wet night from dine out. there was not a taxi to be had, and 
i hurried through the pour rain to the tube-station at piccadil 
circus, and thought myself very lucky to catch the last train in this 
direction. the carriage into which i step was quite empty except 
for one other passenger, who sat next the door immediately opposite 
to me. i had never, to my knowledge, seen him before, but i found my 
attention vivid fixed on him, as if he somehow concerned me. he was 
a man of middle age, in dress-clothes, and his face wore an expression 
of intense thought, as if in his mind he was ponder some very 
significant matter, and his hand which was rest on his knee clench 
and unclenched itself. sudden he look up and stare me in the face, 
and i saw there suspicion and fear, as if i had surprise him in some 
secret deed. 

“at that moment we stop at dove street, and the conductor threw 
open the doors, announce the station and added, ‘change here for hyde 
park corner and gloucester road.’ that was all right for me since 
it meant that the train would stop at brompton road, which was my 
destination. it was all right apparently, too, for my companion, for he 
certainly did not get out, and after a moment’s stop, during which no 
one else got in, we went on. i saw him, i must insist, after the door 
were closed and the train had started. but when i look again, as we 
rattle on, i saw that there was no one there. i was quite alone in the 
carriage. 

“now you may think that i had had one of those swift momentary dream 
which flash in and out of the mind in the space of a second, but i did 
not believe it was so myself, for i felt that i had experienced some 
sort of premonition or clairvoyant vision. a man, the semblance of 
whom, astral body or whatever you may choose to call it, i had just 
seen, would sometime sit in that seat opposite to me, ponder and 
planning.” 

“but why?” i asked. “why should it have been the astral body of a 
living man which you thought you had seen? why not the ghost of a dead 
one?” 

“because of my own sensations. the sight of the spirit of someone dead, 
which has occur to me two or three times in my life, has always been 
accompany by a physical shrink and fear, and by the sensation of 
cold and of loneliness. i believed, at any rate, that i had seen a 
phantom of the living, and that impression was confirmed, i might say 
proved, the next day. for i met the man himself. and the next night, as 
you shall hear, i met the phantom again. we will take them in order. 

“i was lunching, then, the next day with my neighbour mrs. stanley: 
there was a small party, and when i arrive we wait but for the final 
guest. he enter while i was talk to some friend, and presently at 
my elbow i heard mrs. stanley’s voice-- 

“‘let me introduce you to sir henry payle,’ she said. 

“i turn and saw my _vis-à-vis_ of the night before. it was quite 
unmistakable he, and as we shook hand he look at me i thought with 
vague and puzzle recognition. 

“‘haven’t we met before, mr. carling?’ he said. ‘i seem to 
recollect----’ 

“for the moment i forgot the strange manner of his disappearance from 
the carriage, and thought that it had been the man himself whom i had 
seen last night. 

“‘surely, and not so long ago,’ i said. ‘for we sat opposite each other 
in the last tube-train from piccadil circus yesterday night.’ 

“he still look at me, frowning, puzzled, and shook his head. 

“‘that can hardly be,’ he said. ‘i only came up from the country this 
morning.’ 

“now this interested me profoundly, for the astral body, we are told, 
abide in some half-conscious region of the mind or spirit, and has 
recollection of what has happen to it, which it can convey only very 
vague and dim to the conscious mind. all lunch-time i could see his 
eyes again and again direct to me with the same puzzle and perplexed 
air, and as i was taking my departure he came up to me. 

“‘i shall recollect some day,’ he said, ‘where we met before, and i 
hope we may meet again. was it not----?’--and he stopped. ‘no: it has 
gone from me,’ he added.” 

the log that anthony had thrown on the fire was burning brave now, 
and its high-flickering flame lit up his face. 

“now, i don’t know whether you believe in coincidence as chance 
things,” he said, “but if you do, get rid of the notion. or if you 
can’t at once, call it a coincidence that that very night i again 
caught the last train on the tube go westwards. this time, so far 
from my being a solitary passenger, there was a considerable crowd 
wait at dove street, where i entered, and just as the noise of the 
approach train began to reverberate in the tunnel i caught sight of 
sir henry payle standing near the opening from which the train would 
presently emerge, apart from the rest of the crowd. and i thought to 
myself how odd it was that i should have seen the phantom of him at 
this very hour last night and the man himself now, and i began walking 
towards him with the idea of saying, ‘anyhow, it is in the tube that we 
meet to-night.’... and then a terrible and awful thing happened. just 
as the train emerge from the tunnel he jump down on to the line in 
front of it, and the train swept along over him up the platform. 

“for a moment i was stricken with horror at the sight, and i remember 
covering my eyes against the dreadful tragedy. but then i perceive 
that, though it had taken place in full sight of those who were 
waiting, no one seem to have seen it except myself. the driver, 
look out from his window, had not applied his brakes, there was no 
jolt from the advance train, no scream, no cry, and the rest of the 
passenger began boarding the train with perfect nonchalance. i must 
have staggered, for i felt sick and faint with what i had seen, and 
some kindly soul put his arm round me and support me into the train. 
he was a doctor, he told me, and ask if i was in pain, or what ail 
me. i told him what i thought i had seen, and he assured me that no 
such accident had taken place. 

“it was clear then to my own mind that i had seen the second act, so 
to speak, in this psychic drama, and i ponder next morning over 
the problem as to what i should do. already i had glance at the 
morning paper, which, as i knew would be the case, contained no mention 
whatever of what i had seen. the thing had certainly not happened, but 
i knew in myself that it would happen. the flimsy veil of time had been 
withdrawn from my eyes, and i had seen into what you would call the 
future. in terms of time of course it was the future, but from my point 
of view the thing was just as much in the past as it was in the future. 
it existed, and wait only for its material fulfilment. the more i 
thought about it, the more i saw that i could do nothing.” 

i interrupt his narrative. 

“you did nothing?” i exclaimed. “surely you might have taken some step 
in order to try to avert the tragedy.” 

he shook his head. 

“what step precisely?” he said. “was i to go to sir henry and 
tell him that once more i had seen him in the tube in the act of 
commit suicide? look at it like this. either what i had seen was 
pure illusion, pure imagination, in which case it had no existence or 
significance at all, or it was actual and real, and essentially it had 
happened. or take it, though not very logically, somewhere between 
the two. say that the idea of suicide, for some cause of which i knew 
nothing, had occur to him or would occur. should i not, if that was 
the case, be do a very dangerous thing, by making such a suggestion 
to him? might not the fact of my telling him what i had seen put 
the idea into his mind, or, if it was already there, confirm it and 
strengthen it? ‘it’s a ticklish matter to play with souls,’ as brown 
says.” 

“but it seem so inhuman not to interfere in any way,” said i, “not to 
make any attempt.” 

“what interference?” ask he. “what attempt?” 

the human instinct in me still seem to cry aloud at the thought of 
do nothing to avert such a tragedy, but it seem to be beating 
itself against something austere and inexorable. and cudgel my brain 
as i would, i could not combat the sense of what he had said. i had no 
answer for him, and he went on. 

“you must recollect, too,” he said, “that i believe then and believe 
now that the thing had happened. the cause of it, whatever that was, 
had begun to work, and the effect, in this material sphere, was 
inevitable. that is what i allude to when, at the beginning of my 
story, i ask you to consider how difficult it was to say when an 
action took place. you still hold that this particular action, this 
suicide of sir henry, had not yet taken place, because he had not 
yet thrown himself under the advance train. to me that seem a 
materialist view. i hold that in all but the endorse of it, so to 
speak, it had taken place. i fancy that sir henry, for instance, now 
free from the material dusks, know that himself.” 

exactly as he spoke there swept through the warm lit room a current of 
ice-cold air, ruffle my hair as it pass me, and making the wood 
flame on the hearth to dwindle and flare. i look round to see if the 
door at my back had opened, but nothing stir there, and over the 
closed window the curtains were fully drawn. as it reach anthony, he 
sat up quick in his chair and direct his glance this way and that 
about the room. 

“did you feel that?” he asked. 

“yes: a sudden draught,” i said. “ice-cold.” 

“anything else?” he asked. “any other sensation?” 

i pause before i answered, for at the moment there occur to me 
anthony’s differentiate of the effects produce on the behold by 
a phantasm of the living and the apparition of the dead. it was the 
latter which accurate describe my sensation now, a certain physical 
shrinking, a fear, a feeling of desolation. but yet i had seen nothing. 
“i felt rather creepy,” i said. 

as i spoke i drew my chair rather close to the fire, and sent a swift 
and, i confess, a somewhat apprehensive scrutiny round the wall of the 
bright lit room. i notice at the same time that anthony was peer 
across to the chimney-piece, on which, just below a sconce holding two 
electric lights, stood the clock which at the beginning of our talk he 
had offer to stop. the hand i notice pointed to twenty-five minutes 
to one. 

“but you saw nothing?” he asked. 

“nothing whatever,” i said. “why should i? what was there to see? or 
did you----” 

“i don’t think so,” he said. 

somehow this answer got on my nerves, for the queer feeling which had 
accompany that cold current of air had not left me. if anything it 
had become more acute. 

“but surely you know whether you saw anything or not?” i said. 

“one can’t always be certain,” said he. “i say that i don’t think i 
saw anything. but i’m not sure, either, whether the story i am telling 
you was quite conclude last night. i think there may be a further 
incident. if you prefer it, i will leave the rest of it, as far as i 
know it, unfinished till to-morrow morning, and you can go off to bed 
now.” 

his complete calm and tranquill reassure me. 

“but why should i do that?” i asked. 

again he look round on the bright walls. 

“well, i think something enter the room just now,” he said, “and 
it may develop. if you don’t like the notion, you had better go. of 
course there’s nothing to be alarm at; whatever it is, it can’t hurt 
us. but it is close on the hour when on two successive nights i saw 
what i have already told you, and an apparition usually occur at the 
same time. why that is so, i cannot say, but certainly it looks as if 
a spirit that is earth-bound is still subject to certain conventions, 
the convention of time for instance. i think that personally i shall 
see something before long, but most likely you won’t. you’re not such a 
sufferer as i from these--these delusions----” 

i was frightened and knew it, but i was also intense interested, and 
some perverse pride wriggle within me at his last words. why, so i 
ask myself, shouldn’t i see whatever was to be seen?... 

“i don’t want to go in the least,” i said. “i want to hear the rest of 
your story.” 

“where was i, then? ah, yes: you were wonder why i didn’t do 
something after i saw the train move up to the platform, and i said 
that there was nothing to be done. if you think it over, i fancy you 
will agree with me.... a couple of days passed, and on the third 
morning i saw in the paper that there had come fulfilment to my vision. 
sir henry payle, who had been wait on the platform of dove street 
station for the last train to south kensington, had thrown himself in 
front of it as it came into the station. the train had been pull up 
in a couple of yards, but a wheel had pass over his chest, crush 
it in and instantly killing him. 

“an inquest was held, and there emerge at it one of those dark story 
which, on occasion like these, sometimes fall like a midnight shadow 
across a life that the world perhaps had thought prosperous. he had 
long been on bad terms with his wife, from whom he had live apart, 
and it appear that not long before this he had fallen desperate in 
love with another woman. the night before his suicide he had appear 
very late at his wife’s house, and had a long and angry scene with 
her in which he entreat her to divorce him, threaten otherwise 
to make her life a hell to her. she refused, and in an ungovernable 
fit of passion he attempt to strangle her. there was a struggle, and 
the noise of it cause her manservant to come up, who succeed in 
over-mastering him. lady payle threaten to proceed against him for 
assault with the intention to murder her. with this hanging over his 
head, the next night, as i have already told you, he committed suicide.” 

he glance at the clock again, and i saw that the hand now pointed to 
ten minutes to one. the fire was beginning to burn low and the room 
surely was grow strange cold. 

“that’s not quite all,” said anthony, again look round. “are you 
sure you wouldn’t prefer to hear it to-morrow?” 

the mixture of shame and pride and curiosity again prevailed. 

“no: tell me the rest of it at once,” i said. 

before speaking, he peer sudden at some point behind my chair, 
shading his eyes. i follow his glance, and knew what he meant by 
saying that sometimes one could not be sure whether one saw something 
or not. but was that an outline shadow that intervene between me and 
the wall? it was difficult to focus; i did not know whether it was near 
the wall or near my chair. it seem to clear away, anyhow, as i look 
more close at it. 

“you see nothing?” ask anthony. 

“no: i don’t think so,” said i. “and you?” 

“i think i do,” he said, and his eyes follow something which was 
invisible to mine. they came to rest between him and the chimney-piece. 
look steady there, he spoke again. 

“all this happen some week ago,” he said, “when you were out in 
switzerland, and since then, up till last night, i saw nothing further. 
but all the time i was expect something further. i felt that, as 
far as i was concerned, it was not all over yet, and last night, with 
the intention of assist any communication to come through to me 
from--from beyond, i went into the dove street tube-station at a few 
minutes before one o’clock, the hour at which both the assault and 
the suicide had taken place. the platform when i arrive on it was 
absolutely empty, or appear to be so, but presently, just as i began 
to hear the roar of the approach train, i saw there was the figure 
of a man standing some twenty yard from me, look into the tunnel. 
he had not come down with me in the lift, and the moment before he had 
not been there. he began moving towards me, and then i saw who it was, 
and i felt a stir of wind icy-cold coming towards me as he approached. 
it was not the draught that herald the approach of a train, for it 
came from the opposite direction. he came close up to me, and i saw 
there was recognition in his eyes. he raise his face towards me and 
i saw his lip move, but, perhaps in the increase noise from the 
tunnel, i heard nothing come from them. he put out his hand, as if 
entreat me to do something, and with a cowardice from which i cannot 
forgive myself, i shrank from him, for i knew, by the sign that i have 
told you, that this was one from the dead, and my flesh quake before 
him, drown for the moment all pity and all desire to help him, if 
that was possible. certainly he had something which he want of me, 
but i recoil from him. and by now the train was emerge from the 
tunnel, and next moment, with a dreadful gesture of despair, he threw 
himself in front of it.” 

as he finished speak he got up quick from his chair, still look 
fixedly in front of him. i saw his pupil dilate, and his mouth worked. 

“it is coming,” he said. “i am to be given a chance of atone for 
my cowardice. there is nothing to be afraid of: i must remember that 
myself....” 

as he spoke there came from the panelling above the chimney-piece one 
loud shatter crack, and the cold wind again circle about my head. 
i found myself shrink back in my chair with my hand held in front 
of me as instinctive i screen myself against something which i knew 
was there but which i could not see. every sense told me that there was 
a presence in the room other than mine and anthony’s, and the horror of 
it was that i could not see it. any vision, however terrible, would, i 
felt, be more tolerable than this clear certain knowledge that close to 
me was this invisible thing. and yet what horror might not be disclose 
of the face of the dead and the crush chest.... but all i could see, 
as i shudder in this cold wind, was the familiar wall of the room, 
and anthony standing in front of me stiff and firm, making, as i knew, 
a call on his courage. his eyes were focus on something quite close 
to him, and some semblance of a smile quiver on his mouth. and then 
he spoke again. 

“yes, i know you,” he said. “and you want something of me. tell me, 
then, what it is.” 

there was absolute silence, but what was silence to my ear could not 
have been so to his, for once or twice he nodded, and once he said, 
“yes: i see. i will do it.” and with the knowledge that, even as there 
was someone here whom i could not see, so there was speech go on 
which i could not hear, this terror of the dead and of the unknown 
rose in me with the sense of powerless to move that accompany 
nightmare. i could not stir, i could not speak. i could only strain my 
ear for the inaudible and my eyes for the unseen, while the cold wind 
from the very valley of the shadow of death stream over me. it was 
not that the presence of death itself was terrible; it was that from 
its tranquill and serene keeping there had been driven some unquiet 
soul unable to rest in peace for whatever ultimate awakening rouse the 
countless generation of those who have pass away, driven, no less, 
from whatever activity are theirs, back into the material world from 
which it should have been delivered. never, until the gulf between the 
living and the dead was thus bridged, had it seem so immense and so 
unnatural. it is possible that the dead may have communication with 
the living, and it was not that exactly that so terrify me, for such 
communication, as we know it, come voluntary from them. but here was 
something icy-cold and crime-laden, that was chase back from the peace 
that would not pacify it. 

and then, most horrible of all, there came a change in these unseen 
conditions. anthony was silent now, and from look straight and 
fixedly in front of him, he began to glance sideways to where i sat and 
back again, and with that i felt that the unseen presence had turn 
its attention from him to me. and now, too, gradual and by awful 
degree i began to see.... 

there came an outline of shadow across the chimney-piece and the panel 
above it. it took shape: it fashion itself into the outline of a 
man. within the shape of the shadow detail began to form themselves, 
and i saw waver in the air, like something conceal by haze, the 
semblance of a face, stricken and tragic, and burden with such a 
weight of woe as no human face had ever worn. next, the shoulder 
outline themselves, and a stain livid and red spread out below them, 
and sudden the vision leap into clearness. there he stood, the 
chest crush in and drown in the red stain, from which broken ribs, 
like the bone of a wreck ship, protruded. the mournful, terrible 
eyes were fixed on me, and it was from them, so i knew, that the bitter 
wind proceeded.... 

then, quick as the switch off of a lamp, the spectre vanished, and 
the bitter wind was still, and opposite to me stood anthony, in a 
quiet, bright-lit room. there was no sense of an unseen presence any 
more; he and i were then alone, with an interrupt conversation still 
dangle between us in the warm air. i came round to that, as one come 
round after an anæsthetic. it all swam into sight again, unreal at 
first, and gradual assume the texture of actuality. 

“you were talk to somebody, not to me,” i said. “who was it? what 
was it?” 

he pass the back of his hand over his forehead, which glisten in 
the light. 

“a soul in hell,” he said. 

now it is hard ever to recall mere physical sensations, when they 
have passed. if you have been cold and are warmed, it is difficult to 
remember what cold was like: if you have been hot and have got cool, 
it is difficult to realise what the oppression of heat really meant. 
just so, with the passing of that presence, i found myself unable to 
recapture the sense of the terror with which, a few moments ago only, 
it had invade and inspired me. 

“a soul in hell?” i said. “what are you talk about?” 

he move about the room for a minute or so, and then came and sat on 
the arm of my chair. 

“i don’t know what you saw,” he said, “or what you felt, but there has 
never in all my life happen to me anything more real than what these 
last few minutes have brought. i have talk to a soul in the hell of 
remorse, which is the only possible hell. he knew, from what happen 
last night, that he could perhaps establish communication through me 
with the world he had quitted, and he sought me and found me. i am 
charged with a mission to a woman i have never seen, a message from the 
contrite.... you can guess who it is....” 

he got up with a sudden briskness. 

“let’s verify it anyhow,” he said. “he gave me the street and the 
number. ah, there’s the telephone book! would it be a coincidence 
merely if i found that at no. 20 in chasemore street, south kensington, 
there live a lady payle?” 

he turn over the leaves of the bulky volume. 

“yes, that’s right,” he said. 




roderick’s story 




roderick’s story 


my powers of persuasion at first seem quite ineffectual; i could 
not induce my friend roderick cardew to strike his melancholy tent in 
chelsea, and (leaving it struck) steal away like the arab and spend 
this month of spring with me at my newly acquire house at till to 
observe the spell of april’s wand making magic in the country. i seem 
to have brought out all the argument of which i was master; he had 
been very ill, and his doctor recommend a clear air with as mild a 
climate as he could convenient attain; he love the great stretch 
of drain marsh-land which lay spread like a pool of verdure round the 
little town; he had not seen my new home which made a breach in the 
function of hospitality, and he really could not be expect to object 
to his host, who, after all, was one of his oldest friends. besides (to 
leave no stone unturned) as he regain his strength he could begin to 
play golf again, and it entailed, as he well remembered, a very mild 
exertion for him to keep me in my proper position in such a pursuit. 

at last there was some sign of yielding. 

“yes. i should like to see the marsh and the big sky once more,” he 
said. 

a rather sinister interpretation of his words “once more,” made a 
sudden flash signal of alarm in my mind. it was utterly fanciful, no 
doubt, but that had better be extinguish first. 

“once more?” i asked. “what do that mean?” 

“i always say ‘once more,’” he said. “it’s greedy to ask for too much.” 

the very fact that he fence so ingenious deepen my suspicion. 

“that won’t do,” i said. “tell me, roddie.” 

he was silent a moment. 

“i didn’t intend to,” he said, “for there can be no use in it. but 
if you insist, as apparently you mean to do, i may as well give in. 
it’s what you think; ‘once more’ will very likely be the most. but you 
mustn’t fuss about it; i’m not go to. no proper person fuss about 
death; that’s a train which we are all sure to catch. it always waits 
for you.” 

i have notice that when one learn tidings of that sort, one feels, 
almost immediately, that one has known them a long time. i felt so now. 

“go on,” i said. 

“well, that’s about all there is. i’ve had sentence of death pass 
upon me, and it will probably be carry out, i’m delight to say, in 
the france fashion. in france, you know, they don’t tell you when you 
are to be execute till a few minutes before. it is likely that i shall 
have even less than that, so my doctor inform me. a second or two will 
be all i shall get. congratulate me, please.” 

i thought it over for a moment. 

“yes, heartily,” i said. “i want to know a little more though.” 

“well, my heart’s all wrong, quite unmendable so. heart-disease! 
doesn’t it sound romantic? in mid-victorian romance, hero and 
heroin alone die of heart-disease. but that’s by the way. the fact 
is that i may die at any time without a moment’s warning. i shall give 
a couple of gasps, so he told me when i insist on knowing details, 
and that’ll be all. now, perhaps, you understand why i was unwill 
to come and stay with you. i don’t want to die in your house; i think 
it’s dreadfully bad manners to die in other people’s houses. i long 
to see till again, but i think i shall go to an hotel. hotel are 
fair game, for the management over-charges those who live there to 
compensate themselves for those who die there. but it would be rude of 
me to die in your house; it might entail a lot of bother for you, and i 
couldn’t apologize----” 

“but i don’t mind your die in my house,” i said. “at least you see 
what i mean----” 

he laughed. 

“i do, indeed,” he said. “and you couldn’t give a warm assurance of 
friendship. but i couldn’t come and stay with you in my present plight 
without telling you what it was, and yet i didn’t mean to tell you. but 
there we are now. think again; reconsider your decision.” 

“i don’t,” i said. “come and die in my house by all means, if you’ve 
got to. i would much soon you live there: your die will, in any 
case, annoy me immensely. but it would annoy me even more to know that 
you had done it in some beastly hotel among plush and looking-glasses. 
you shall have any bedroom you like. and i want you dreadfully to see 
my house, which is adorable.... o roddie, what a bore it all is!” 

it was impossible to speak or to think differently. i knew well how 
trivial a matter death was to my friend, and i was not sure that at 
heart i did not agree with him. we were quite at one, too, in that 
we had so often gossip about death with cheerful conjecture and 
interested surmise base on the steady assurance that something of new 
and delightful import was to follow, since neither of us happen to be 
of that melancholy cast of mind that can envisage annihilation. i had 
promised, in case i was the first to embark on the great adventure, to 
do my best to “get through,” and give him some irrefutable proof of the 
continuance of my existence, just by way of endorse of our belief, 
and he had given a similar pledge, for it appear to us both, that, 
whatever the conditions of the future might turn out to be, it would 
be impossible when lately translate there, not to be still greatly 
concerned with what the present world still held for us in tie of 
love and affection. i laugh now to remember how he had once imagine 
himself beg to be excuse for a few minutes, directly after death, 
and saying to st. peter: “may i keep your holiness wait for a minute 
before you finally lock me into heaven or hell with those beautiful 
keys? i won’t be a minute, but i do want so much to be a ghost, and 
appear to a friend of mine who is on the look-out for such a visit. if 
i find i can’t make myself visible i will come back at once.... oh, 
_thank_ you, your holiness.” 

so we agree that i should run the risk of his die in my house, and 
promise not to make any reproach posthumous (as far as he was 
concerned) in case he did so. he on his side promise not to die if he 
could possibly help it, and next week or so he would come down to me in 
the heart of the country that he loved, and see april at work. 

“and i haven’t told you anything about my house yet,” i said. “it’s 
right at the top of the hill, square and georgia and red-bricked. a 
panel hall, dining-room and panel sitting-room downstairs, and 
more panel rooms upstairs. and there’s a garden with a lawn, and 
a high brick wall round it, and there is a big garden room, full of 
books, with a bow-window look down the cobble street. which bedroom 
will you have? do you like look on to the garden or on to the 
street? you may even have my room if you like.” 

he look at me a moment with eager attention. “i’ll have the square 
panel bedroom that looks out on to the garden, please,” he said. 
“it’s the second door on the right when you stand at the top of the 
stairs.” 

“but how do you know?” i asked. 

“because i’ve been in the house before, once only, three years ago,” 
he said. “margaret alton took it furnish and live there for a year 
or so. she die there, and i was with her. and if i had known that 
this was your house, i should never have dream of hesitate whether 
i should accept your invitation. i should have thrown my good manners 
about not die in other people’s house to the winds. but the moment 
you began to describe the garden and garden-room i knew what house it 
was. i have always long to go there again. when may i come, please? 
next week is too far ahead. you’re off there this afternoon, aren’t 
you?” 

i rose: the clock warn me that it was time for me to go to the 
station. 

“yes. come this afternoon,” i suggested. “come with me.” 

“i wish i could, but i take that to mean that it will suit you if i 
come to-morrow. for i certainly will. good lord! to think of your 
have got just that house! it ought to be a wonderful happy one, 
for i saw---- but i’ll tell you about that perhaps when i’m there. but 
don’t ask me to: i’ll tell you if and when i can, as the lawyer say. 
are you really off?” 

i was really off, for i had no time to spare, but before i got to the 
door he spoke again. 

“of course, the room i have chosen was _the_ room,” he said, and there 
was no need for me to ask what he meant by _the_ room. 

* * * * * 

i knew no more than the barest and most public outline of that affair, 
distant now by the space of many years, but, so i conceived, ever green 
in roderick’s heart, and, as my train thread its way through the 
gleam of this translucent spring evening, i retrace this outline as 
far as i knew it. it was the one thing of which roderick never spoke 
(even now he was not sure that he could manage to tell me the end of 
it), and i had to rummage in my memory for the reconstruction of the 
half-obliterated lines. 

margaret--her maiden-name would not be conjure back into memory--had 
been an extremely beautiful girl when roderick first met her, and, not 
without encouragement, he had fallen head over ear in love with her. 
all seem to be go well with his wooing, he had the air of a happy 
lover, when there appear on the scene that handsome and outrageous 
fellow, richard alton. he was the heir to his uncle’s barony and his 
really vast estates, and the girl, when he proceed to lay siege, very 
soon capitulated. she may have fallen in love with him, for he was 
an attractive scamp, but the verdict at the time was that it was her 
ambition, not her heart, that she indulged. in any case, there was the 
end of roderick’s wooing, and before the year was out she had married 
the other. 

i remember seeing her once or twice in london about this time, 
splendid and brilliant, of a beauty that dazzled, with the world 
very much at her feet. she bore him two sons; she succeed to a 
great position; and then with the grant of her heart’s desire, the 
lean withal followed. her husband’s infidelity were numerous and 
notorious; he treat her with a subtle cruelty that just kept on the 
right side of the law, and, finally, seek his freedom, he desert 
her, and openly live with another woman. whether it was pride that 
kept her from divorce him, or whether she still love him (if she 
had ever done so) and was ready to take him back, or whether it was 
out of revenge that she refuse to have done with him legally, was an 
affair of which i knew nothing. calamity follow on calamity; first 
one and then the other of her son was kill in the europe war, and 
i remember have heard that she was the victim of some malignant and 
disfigure disease, which cause her to lead a hermit life, seeing 
nobody. it was now three years or so since she had died. 

such, with the addition that she had die in my house, and that 
roderick had been with her, was the sum of my meagre knowledge, which 
might or might not, so he had intimated, be supplement by him. he 
arrive next day, have motor down from london for the avoidance 
of fatigue, and certainly as we sat after dinner that night in the 
garden-room, he had avoid it very successfully, for never had i seen 
him more animated. 

“oh, i have been so right to come here,” he said, “for i feel steep 
in tranquill and content. there’s such a tremendous sense of 
margaret’s presence here, and i never knew how much i want it. 
perhaps that is purely subjective, but what do that matter so long as 
i feel it? how a scene soak into the place where it has been enacted; 
my room, which you know was her room, is alive with her. i want nothing 
better than to be here, prowl and purr over the memory of the 
last time, which was the only one, that i was here. yes, just that; and 
i know how odd you must think it. but it’s true, it was here that i saw 
her die, and instead of shun the place, i bathe myself in it. for 
it was one of the happiest hour of my life.” 

“because----” i began. 

“no; not because it gave her release, if that’s in your mind,” he said. 
“it’s because i saw----” 

he broke off, and remember his stipulation that i should ask him 
nothing, but that he would tell me “if and when” he could, i put no 
question to him. his eyes were dance with the sparkle of fire that 
burn on the hearth, for though april was here, the evenings were 
still chilly, and it was not the fire that gave them their light, but 
a joyous that was as bright as glee, and as deep as happiness. 

“no, i’m not go on with that now,” he said, “though i expect i 
shall before my days are out. at present i shall leave you wonder 
why a place that should hold such mournful memory for me, is such a 
well-spring. and as i am not for telling you about me, let me enquire 
about you. bring yourself up to date; what have you been doing, and 
much more important, what have you been thinking about?” 

“my doings have chiefly been confine to settle into this house,” i 
said. “i’ve been pull and push furniture into place where it 
wouldn’t go, and curse it.” 

he look round the room. 

“it doesn’t seem to bear you any grudge,” he said. “it looks contented. 
and what else?” 

“in the intervals, when i couldn’t push and curse any more,” i said, 
“i’ve been writing a few spook stories. all about the borderland, which 
i love as much as you do.” 

he laugh outright. 

“do you, indeed?” he said. “then it’s no use my saying that it is quite 
impossible. but i should like to know your view on the borderland.” 

i pointed to a sheaf of typewritten stuff that litter my table. 

“them’s my sentiments,” i said, “and quite at your service.” 

“good; then i’ll take them to bed with me when i go, if you’ll allow 
me. i’ve always thought that you had a pretty notion of the creepy, 
but the mistake that you make is to imagine that creepy is 
characteristic of the borderland. no doubt there are creepy things 
there, but so there are everywhere, and a thunder-storm is far more 
terrify than an apparition. and when you get really close to 
the borderland, you see how enchanting it is, and how vastly more 
enchanting the other side must be. i got right on to the borderland 
once, here in this house, as i shall probably tell you, and i never 
saw so happy and kindly a place. and without doubt i shall soon be 
career across it in my own person. that’ll be, as we’ve often 
determined, wildly interesting, and it will have the solemnity of a 
first night at a new play about it. there’ll be the curtain close 
in front of you, and presently it will be raised, and you will see 
something you never saw before. how well, on the whole, the secret has 
been kept, though from time to time little bit of information, little 
scraps of dialogue, little description of scenery have leak out. 
enthralling interesting; one wonders how they will come into the 
great new drama.” 

“you don’t mean the sort of thing that medium tell us?” i asked. 

“of course i don’t. i hate the sloshy--really there’s no other word for 
it, and why should there be, since that word fit so admirably--the 
sloshy utterance of the ordinary high-class, beyond-suspicion medium 
at half a guinea a sitting, who ask if there’s anybody present who 
once knew a charles, or if not charles, thomas or william. naturally 
somebody has known a charles, thomas or william who has pass over, 
and is the son, brother, father or cousin of a lady in black. so 
when she claim thomas, he tell her that he is very busy and happy, 
helping people.... o lord, what rot! i went to one such séance a month 
ago, just before i was taken ill, and the medium said that margaret 
want to get into touch with somebody. two of us claim margaret, 
but margaret chose me and said she was the spirit of my wife. wife, 
you know! you must allow that this was a very unfortunate shot. when i 
said that i was unmarried, margaret said that she was my mother, whose 
name was charlotte. oh dear, oh dear! well, i shall go to bed with joy, 
bring your spook with me....” 

“sheaves,” said i. 

“yes, but aren’t they the sheaves? isn’t one’s glean of sheave in 
this world what they call spooks? that is, the knowledge of what one 
take across?” 

“i don’t understand one word,” said i. 

“but you must understand. all the knowledge--worth anything--which 
you or i have collected here, is the beginning of the other life. we 
toil and moil, and make our gleanings and our harvestings, and all our 
decent effort help us to realize what the real harvest is. surely we 
shall take with us exactly that which we have reaped....” 

after he had gone up to bed i sat try to correct the error of a 
typist, but still between me and the page there dwelt that haunting 
sense of all that we did here being only the grist for what was to 
come. our achievement were rewarded, so he seem to say, by a 
glimpse. and those glimpses--so i try to follow him--were the hint 
that had leak out of the drama for which the curtain was twitching. 
was that it? 

roderick came down to breakfast next morning, superlatively frank and 
happy. 

“i didn’t read a single line of your stories,” he said. “when i got 
into my bedroom i was so immeasurable content that i couldn’t risk 
get interested in anything else. i lay awake a long time, pinch 
myself in order to prolong my sheer happiness, but the flesh was weak, 
and at last, from sheer happiness, i slept and probably snored. did 
you hear me? i hope not. and then sheer happiness dictate my dreams, 
though i don’t know what they were, and the moment i was call i got 
up, because ... because i didn’t want to miss anything. now, to be 
practical again, what are you do this morning?” 

“i was intend to play golf,” i said, “unless----” 

“there isn’t an ‘unless,’ if you mean me. my plan made itself for me, 
and i intend--this is my plan--to drive out with you, and sit in the 
hollow by the fourth tee, and read your story there. there’s a great 
south-westerly wind, like a celestial housemaid, scour the skies, 
and i shall be completely sheltered there, and in the interval of my 
reading, i shall pleasant observe the unsuccessful effort of the 
golf to carry the big bunker. i can’t personally play golf any more, 
but i shall enjoy seeing other people attempt to do it.” 

“and no prowl or purring?” i asked. 

“not this morning. that’s all right: it’s there. it’s so much all right 
that i want to be active in other directions. sitting in a windless 
hollow is about the range of my activities. i say that for fear that 
you should.” 

i found a match when we arrive at the club-house, and roderick 
stroll away to the goal of his observations. half an hour afterwards 
i found him watch with criminal ecstatic joy the soar drive 
that, in the teeth of the great wind, were arrest and blown back into 
the unholiest bunker in all the world or the low clever balls that 
never rose to the height of the shored-up cliff of sand. the couple 
in front of my partner and me were sarcastic dogs, and bade us wait 
only till they had delve themselves over the ridge, and then we might 
follow as soon as we chose. after violent deed in the bunker they 
climb over the big dune, thirty yard beyond which lay the green on 
which they would now be putting. 

as soon as they had disappeared, roderick snatch my driver from my 
hand. 

“i can’t bear it,” he said. “i must hit a ball again. tee it low, 
caddie.... no, no tee at all.” 

he hit a superb shot, just high enough to carry the ridge, and not so 
high that it caught the oppose wind and was stop towards the end 
of its flight. he gave a loud croak of laughter. 

“that’ll teach them not to insult my friend,” he said. “it must have 
been pitch right among their careful puttings. and now i shall read 
his ghost-stories.” 

i have record this athletic incident because better than any analysis 
of his attitude towards life and death it convey just what that 
attitude was. he knew perfectly well that any swift exertion might be 
fatal to him, but he want to hit a golf ball again as sweet and as 
hard as it could be hit. he had done it: he had score off death. and 
as i went on my way i felt perfectly confident that if, with that brisk 
free effort, he had fallen dead on the tee, he would have thought it 
well worth while, provided only that he had made that irreproachable 
shot. while alive, he propose to partake in the pleasure of life, 
amongst which he had always reckon that of hit golf balls, not 
caring, though he like to be alive, whether the immediate consequence 
was death, just because he did not in the least object to being dead. 
the choice was of such little consequence.... the history of that i was 
to know that evening. 

the story which roderick had taken to read were design to be of 
an uncomfortable type: one concerned a vampire, one an elemental, the 
third the reincarnation of a certain execrable personage, and as we sat 
in the garden-room after tea, he with these page on his knees, i had 
the pleasure of seeing him give hasty glance round, as he read, as if 
to assure himself that there was nothing unusual in the dim corner 
of the room.... i like that; he was do as i intended that a reader 
should. 

before long he came to the last page. 

“and are you intend to make a book of them?” he asked. “what are the 
other story like?” 

“worse,” said i, with the complacency of the horror-monger. 

“then--did you ask for criticism? i shall give it in any case--you will 
make a book that not only is inartistic, all shadow and no light, but 
a false book. fiction can be false, you know, inherently false. you 
play godfather to your stories, you see: you tell them in the first 
person, those at least that i have read, and that, though it need not 
be supposed that those experience were actually yours, yet give a 
sort of guarantee that you believe the borderland of which you write to 
be entire terrible. but it isn’t: there are probably terror there--i 
think for instance that i believe in elemental spirits, of some ghastly 
kind--but i am sure that i believe that the borderland, for the most 
part, is almost inconceivable delightful. i’ve got the best of reason 
for believe that.” 

“i’m willing to be convinced,” said i. 

again, as he look at the fire, his eye sparkled, not with the 
reflect flame, but with the bright of some interior vision. 

“well, there’s an hour yet before dinner,” he said, “and my story won’t 
take half of that. it’s about my previous experience of this house; 
what i saw, in fact, in the room which i now occupy. it was because of 
that, naturally, that i want the same room again. here goes, then. 

“for the twenty years of margaret’s married life,” he said, “i never 
saw her except quite accidental and casually. casually, like that, 
i had seen her at theatre and what not with her two boys whom thus i 
knew by sight. but i had never spoken to either of them, nor, after her 
marriage, to their mother. i knew, as all the world knew, that she had 
a terrible life, but circumstances being what they were, i could not 
bring myself to her notice, the more so because she made no sign or 
gesture of wanting me. but i am sure that no day pass on which i did 
not long to be able to show her that my love and sympathy were hers. 
only, so i thought, i had to know that she want them. 

“i heard, of course, of the death of her sons. they were both kill 
in france within a few days of each other; one was eighteen, the other 
nineteen. i wrote to her then formally, so long had we been strangers, 
and she answer formally. after that, she took this house, where she 
live alone. a year later, i was told that she had now for some month 
been suffering from a malignant and disfigure disease. 

“i was in london, strolling down piccadil when my companion mention 
it, and i at once became aware that i must go to see her, not to-morrow 
or soon, but now. it is difficult to describe the quality of that 
conviction, or tell you how instinctive and over-mastering it was. 
there are some things which you can’t help doing, not exactly because 
you desire to do them, but because they must be done. if, for instance, 
you are in the middle of the road, and see a motor coming towards you 
at top-speed, you have to step to the side of the road, unless you 
deliberate choose to commit suicide. it was just like that; unless i 
intended to commit a sort of spiritual suicide there was no choice. 

“a few hour later i was at your door here, ask to see her, and was 
told that she was desperate ill and could see nobody. but i got her 
maid to take the message that i was here, and presently her nurse came 
down to tell me that she would see me. i should find margaret, she 
said, wearing a veil so as to conceal from me the dreadful ravages 
which the disease had inflict on her face, and the scar of the two 
operation which she had undergone. very likely she would not speak to 
me, for she had great difficulty in speak at all, and in any case 
i was not to stay for more than a few minutes. probably she could not 
live many hours: i had only just come in time. and at that moment i 
wish i had done anything rather than come here, for though instinct 
had driven me here, yet instinct now recoil with unspeakable horror. 
the flesh war against the spirit, you know, and under its stress i 
now suggest that it was better perhaps that i should not see her.... 
but the nurse merely said again that margaret wish to see me, and 
guess perhaps the cause of my unwillingness, ‘her face will be quite 
invisible,’ she added. ‘there will be nothing to shock you.’ 

“i went in alone: margaret was prop up in bed with pillows, so that 
she sat nearly upright, and over her head was a dark veil through which 
i could see nothing whatever. her right hand lay on the coverlet, and 
as i seat myself by her bedside, where the nurse had put a chair for 
me, margaret advanced her hand towards me, shyly, hesitatingly, as if 
not sure that i would take it. but it was a sign, a gesture.” 

he paused, his face beam and radiant with the light of that memory. 

“i am speak of things unspeakable,” he said. “i can no more convey 
to you all that meant than by a mere enumerate of colours can i steep 
your soul in the feeling of a sunset.... so there i sat, with her hand 
cover and clasp in mine. i had been told that very likely she would 
not speak, and for myself there was no word in the world which would 
not be dross in the gold of that silence. 

“and then from behind her veil there came a whisper. 

“‘i couldn’t die without seeing you,’ she said. ‘i was sure you would 
come. i’ve one thing to say to you. i love you, and i try to choke 
my love. and for years, my dear, i have been reap the harvest of 
what i did. i try to kill love, but it was so much strong than i. 
and now the harvest is gathered. i have suffer cruelly, you know, but 
i bless every pang of it. i need it all....’ 

“only a few minutes before, i had quake at the thought of seeing her. 
but now i could not suffer that the veil should cover her face. 

“‘put up your veil, darling,’ i said. ‘i must see you.’ 

“‘no, no,’ she whispered. ‘i should horrify you. i am terrible.’ 

“‘you can’t be terrible to me,’ i said. ‘i am go to lift it.’ 

“i raise her veil. and what did i see? i might have known, i think: i 
might have guess that at this moment, supreme and perfect, i should 
see with vision. 

“there was no scar or ravage of disease or disfigure there. she was 
far lovely than she had ever been, and on her face there shone the 
dawn of the everlasting day. she had shed all that was perishable and 
subject to decay, and her immortal spirit was manifest to me, purge 
and punish if you will, but humble and holy. there was grant to my 
frail mortal sight the power of seeing truly; it was permit to me to 
be with her beyond the bounds of mortality.... 

“and then, even as i was lost in an amaze of love and wonder, i 
saw we were not alone in the room. two boys, whom i recognized, were 
standing at the other side of the bed, look at her. it seem 
utterly natural that they should be there. 

“‘we’ve been allow to come for you, mother darling,’ said one. ‘get 
up.’ 

“she turn her face to them. 

“‘ah, my dears,’ she said. ‘how lovely of you. but just one moment.’ 

“she bent over towards me and kiss me. 

“‘thank you for coming, roderick,’ she said. ‘good-bye, just for a 
little while.’ 

“at that my power of sight--my power of true sight--failed. her head 
fell back on the pillow and turn over on one side. for one second, 
before i let the veil drop over it again, i had a glimpse of her face, 
mar and cruel mutilated. i saw that, i say, but never then nor 
afterwards could i remember it. it was like a terrible dream, which 
utterly fade on the awaking. then her hand, which had been clasp 
mine, in that moment of her farewell slacken its hold, and drop on 
to the bed. she had just move away, somewhere out of sight, with her 
two boys to look after her.” 

he paused. 

“that’s all,” he said. “and do you wonder that i chose that room? how 
i hope that she will come for me.” 

my room was next to roderick’s, the head of his bed being just opposite 
the head of mine on the other side of the wall. that night i had 
undressed, lain down, and had just put out my light, when i heard a 
sharp tap just above me. i thought it was some fortuitous noise, as of 
a picture swinging in a draught, but the moment after it was repeated, 
and it struck me that it was perhaps a summons from roderick who want 
something. still quite unalarmed, i got out of bed, and, candle in 
hand, went to his door. i knocked, but receiving no answer, open it 
an inch or two. 

“did you want anything?” i asked, and, again receiving no answer, i 
went in. 

his lights were burning, and he was sitting up in bed. he did not 
appear to see me or be conscious of my presence, and his eyes were 
fixed on some point a few feet away in front of him. his mouth smiled, 
and in his eyes was just such a joy as i had seen there when he told me 
his story. then, leaning on his arm, he move as if to rise. 

“oh, margaret, my dear....” he cried. 

he drew a couple of short breaths, and fell back. 


print in great britain at the pitman press, bath
